Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
beginner

Three Proofs for Beginning with Salawat
Explains reasons for opening with salawat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three reasons Hanafi scholars begin books with salawat after hamd and identify the Quranic and rational proofs.

Core Idea
Starting a book with salawat is an act of compliance, a rational necessity, and a means of divine help.

When Hanafi scholars open their works, they traditionally follow a specific sequence: first praising Allah, then sending blessings upon the Prophet. This passage from a usul text unpacks three powerful reasons behind this practice. The first is direct obedience to the Quran, the second is a rational argument about human impurity needing a mediator, and the third is a practical need for divine assistance in completing the work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Quranic imperative
Locate the Quranic verse quoted: 'O you who believe, send blessings upon him and greet him with peace' (33:56). Observe the imperative mood (صلوا, سلموا). Reflect on how the author uses this as a command to be obeyed in writing.
The dallala of the imperative is zanni here because it is applied to a specific context (beginning a book), but the thubut is qati.
2
Analyze the rational argument
Follow the author's reasoning: our souls are impure; Allah is absolutely pure; we need an intermediary (wasita). That intermediary is sending salawat on the Prophet, because Allah promises ten-fold mercy in return. Understand that this is a rational (aqli) justification.
The concept of wasita is key in Hanafi thought for acts of worship.
3
Uncover the third reason (seeking help)
The author adds that compiling a book, especially on usul al-fiqh, is difficult. One needs Allah's help, and du'a is more readily accepted when preceded by salawat. This is based on Prophetic practice.
This reason is not in the title but is a common practice among scholars.
4
Synthesize the three proofs
Combine the three: Quranic command (naqli), rational need (aqli), and sunnah of du'a (hadith). Together they show that beginning with salawat is a firmly grounded, multi-faceted act of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والصلاة والسلام على سيدنا محمد المتمم للحكم، بطريق الأمم، والمبعوث بجوامع الكلم إلى أفهام الأمم
۱ - أورد الصلاة والسلام" بعد الحمد لوجهين :
الأول : الايتمار والامتثال بقول الله تعالى يا ايها الذين آمنوا صلوا عليه وسلّموا تسليما اذ المذكور في الآية هو الصلاة والسلام، ومن سنة العلماء السلف الجمع بين الصلاة والسلام في أوائل كتبهم ( بعد الحمد).
والوجه الثاني (وهو عقلى): أن نفوسنا متدنسة بالذنوب والأدناس البشرية، والله تعالى قدوس بما لا يتناهى تقدسه ، ومنزه ومتعال بما لا يدرك نزاهته وعلوه ، فلا بد من الواسطة بيننا وبينه لنتقرب بها إليه ، وتلك الواسطة هي الصلاة والسلام على رسوله ، لأنه قال : من صلى على مرة صلى الله عليه عشراً، والصلاة هو ثناء الله تعالى على رسوله ورحمته إياه فرحمة الله تعالى هي وسيلة التقرب لنا.
والوجه الثالث الذي لم يذكره الشيخ في العنوان هو : أن المصنف لما أراد تأليف الكتاب لا بد أنه استعان من الله إما بلسان القال واما بلسان الحال لإتمام كتابه وإكماله ، فإن التصنيف وجمع أصول الفقه، وضبط قواعده أمر صعب لا يمكن النجاح والفوز فيه الا بالاستعانة والاستمداد والدعاء وطلب التسهيل من الله تعالى ، وإنما يتقبل الله تعالى الدعاء بالعجلة بعد تقديم الصلاة والسلام على رسوله ، فذكر الصلاة والسلام بعد الحمد، إنما يكون للاستجابة ، لأنه جاء في الحديث ما معناه : " من سنة الدعاء أن يحمد الله تعالى ، ثم يسلم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ثم يدعو لحاجته ما شاء "، وكذالك جاء في الحديث أن رجلاً جاء إلى المسجد (المسجد النبوي) ، فصلّى ، ولم يصل ويسلّم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : "عجلت (1) . (۱)"
And blessings and peace upon our master Muhammad, the completer of wisdom in the most balanced way, sent with comprehensive words to the understanding of nations.
1. He mentioned blessings and peace after praise for two aspects:
First: Compliance with Allah's saying: 'O you who believe, send blessings upon him and greet him with peace' since what is mentioned in the verse is blessing and peace. And it is the tradition of the early scholars to combine blessings and peace at the beginnings of their books (after praise).
Second (rational): Our souls are stained with sins and human impurities, and Allah is holy with unlimited holiness, so a mediator is needed between us and Him to draw near. That mediator is blessings and peace upon His Messenger, because He said: 'Whoever sends blessings upon me once, Allah sends blessings upon him ten times.' Blessing is Allah's praise of His Messenger and His mercy upon him, so Allah's mercy is our means of closeness.
Third, which the author did not mention: When the author intended to write this book, he needed Allah's assistance, directly or indirectly, to complete it. Compiling usul al-fiqh is difficult and can only succeed with seeking help and supplication. Allah accepts supplication quickly after sending blessings upon His Messenger. So mentioning blessings after praise is for acceptance, as it came in the hadith: 'It is the practice of supplication to praise Allah, then send blessings upon the Prophet, then ask for one's need.' Also there is the hadith that a man came to the mosque, prayed, did not send blessings upon the Prophet, and the Prophet said: 'You have been hasty.'
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage explains why the author begins with salawat after praising Allah. The first reason is acting upon the Quranic command in Surah al-Ahzab. The second is rational: we need an intermediary because Allah is transcendent; that intermediary is the Prophet's blessings. The third reason, not explicitly in the author's title, is the need for divine help in writing the book. The author cites traditions to support the practice.

Key Terms
الواسطة (al-wasita): The intermediary; here the blessings on the Prophet serve as a means to draw closer to Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the reasons given in the passage for beginning with salawat?
A) Compliance with the Quranic command in Surah al-Ahzab
B) Rational need for an intermediary due to human impurity
C) Following the sunnah of ending prayers with salawat
D) Seeking divine help by placing salawat before supplication

Key Takeaway
Beginning a book with salawat after hamd is a multi-layered practice grounded in Quran, reason, and Prophetic guidance.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
Dispute over the Founder of Usul al-Fiqh
Dispute over the founder of Usul al-Fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the origin of Usul al-Fiqh and evaluate the evidence for the chain of transmission from Abu Hanifa to al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa is the origin of Usul al-Fiqh; al-Shafi'i learned from his students.

This passage addresses a well-known dispute among the schools: who first established the science of Usul al-Fiqh? Each madhhab claims its own founder, but the Hanafi school provides a detailed chain of transmission tracing the origins back to Imam Abu Hanifa. Understanding this historical claim clarifies the hierarchical relationship between the schools and the development of usul as a discipline.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi claim: that the real founder of Usul al-Fiqh is Imam Abu Hanifa, not al-Shafi'i. Note the chain: Abu Hanifa taught Abu Yusuf, who taught Muhammad al-Shaybani, who taught al-Shafi'i.
The author's teacher al-Mardanqi is cited as the source of this position.
2
Identify the opposing school and their claim
The Shafi'i school claims Imam al-Shafi'i as the first to systematize usul, citing his works al-Risalah and al-Umm. Similarly, Malikis claim Malik, Hanbalis claim Ibn Qudamah.
The passage mentions each school's representative work.
3
Follow the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi evidence is the chain of transmission: al-Shafi'i studied under Muhammad al-Shaybani, who studied under Abu Yusuf, who studied under Abu Hanifa. Therefore, the knowledge originated with Abu Hanifa.
The passage explicitly states that al-Shafi'i gained firmness in usul from Muhammad.
4
Analyze the chain of transmission
Trace the chain: Abu Hanifa → Abu Yusuf → Muhammad → al-Shafi'i. Note that Abu Yusuf received both fiqh and usul from Abu Hanifa, and Muhammad received them from Abu Yusuf. Al-Shafi'i received usul from Muhammad.
The passage also notes that during this era, fiqh and usul split into separate disciplines.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence of this dispute is which school is recognized as the chronological and authoritative source of usul. The Hanafi position asserts priority over the Shafi'i school in this regard.
This affects how later scholars view the relationship between the schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واضع علم أصول الفقه
واعلم أن مقلّد كل مذهب من المذاهب الأربعة المتبوعة يضع التاج و جائزة وضع أصول الفقه على رأس إمامه.
فالعالم الشافعي يقول أوّل من قام لتقعيد وتأصيل علم أصول الفقه هو الامام الشافعي ، ويمثل بالرسالة وبـ كتاب الأم للامام الشافعى.. والمالكي يقول : أوّل من دوّن أصول الفقه هو الامام مالك ويمثل بـ "الموطأ" وأقوال مالك فيه، وكذلك يدعى الحنبلى ، ويمثل بـ"روضة الناظر ، وشيخنا المارتونكى . بما أنه حنفي يقول في هذا الصدد: وفي الحقيقة إنماجاء وضع أصول الفقه من الامام الأعظم أبي حنيفة، وأخذ الامام أبو يوسف التفقه والتفهم في الفقه وأصوله من شيخه الإمام أبي حنيفة، ولم يحصل الإمام محمد الرسوخ والملكة في الفقه وأصوله) من أبي حنيفة، بل حصل له التفقه والرسوخ في الفقه وأصوله من الإمام أبي يوسف ، فالأصل أن الإمام أبا يوسف حصل الفقه وأصوله كليهما من الامام الأكبر أبي حنيفة ، ففي عصر أبي يوسف ومحمد انقسم التفقه إلى نوعين : الى الفقه وهو فن مستقل ، والى أصول الفقه وهو فنّ آخر ، بل وجاء هناك بتأليف الفقه الأكبر قسم ثالث وهو علم الكلام .
وأما الامام الشافعي فإنما حصل له الرسوخ في أصول الفقه من الامام محمد ، فثبت أن المعدن الأصلى لأصول الفقه ومخزنه الأساسى، هو الإمام الأعظم أبي حنيفة،
لا الإمام الشافعي، كما يزعمه الشافعية وهو المشهور عندهم.
The founder of the science of Usul al-Fiqh. Know that the follower of each of the four followed schools places the crown and prize of founding Usul al-Fiqh upon the head of his imam. The Shafi'i scholar says: The first to establish and systematize the science of Usul al-Fiqh is Imam al-Shafi'i, and he gives as example al-Risalah and Kitab al-Umm by Imam al-Shafi'i. The Maliki says: The first to compile Usul al-Fiqh is Imam Malik, and he gives as example al-Muwatta and Malik's statements in it. Similarly, the Hanbali claims, and gives as example Rawdat al-Nazir. Our teacher al-Mardanqi, since he is a Hanafi, says in this regard: In reality, the founding of Usul al-Fiqh came from the Greatest Imam Abu Hanifa. Imam Abu Yusuf took the deep understanding in fiqh and its principles from his teacher Imam Abu Hanifa. Imam Muhammad did not attain firmness and mastery in fiqh and its principles from Abu Hanifa directly; rather, he attained deep understanding and firmness in fiqh and its principles from Imam Abu Yusuf. Thus originally, Imam Abu Yusuf attained both fiqh and its principles from the Greatest Imam Abu Hanifa. In the era of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, fiqh understanding split into two types: fiqh, which is an independent discipline, and Usul al-Fiqh, which is another discipline. Moreover, there came about a third type: the greatest fiqh, which is the science of theology (kalam). As for Imam al-Shafi'i, he attained firmness in Usul al-Fiqh from Imam Muhammad. So it is established that the original source and primary repository of Usul al-Fiqh is the Greatest Imam Abu Hanifa, not Imam al-Shafi'i, as the Shafi'is claim and as is well-known among them.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a dispute over who first established the science of Usul al-Fiqh. The author, a Hanafi, acknowledges that each madhhab attributes the founding of Usul to its own imam. The Shafi'is point to al-Shafi'i's al-Risalah and al-Umm, the Malikis to Malik's al-Muwatta, and the Hanbalis to Ibn Qudamah's Rawdat al-Nazir. The author then presents the Hanafi position, citing his teacher al-Mardanqi, that in reality the origins lie with Abu Hanifa. He traces the chain: Abu Hanifa taught Abu Yusuf both fiqh and its principles. Abu Yusuf then taught Muhammad al-Shaybani. Imam al-Shafi'i studied under Muhammad al-Shaybani and thus benefited from the Hanafi tradition. Therefore, the ultimate source is Abu Hanifa, not al-Shafi'i. The passage also notes that during the time of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the field split into three: fiqh, usul al-fiqh, and kalam.

Key Terms
مقلّد (muqallid): A follower of a specific madhhab who accepts its rulings without independent reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, from whom did Imam al-Shafi'i learn Usul al-Fiqh?
A) Imam Abu Hanifa
B) Imam Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Imam Malik

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school traces Usul al-Fiqh to Abu Hanifa through the chain of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, from whom al-Shafi'i learned.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Meaning of 'Asl' in Usul al-Fiqh: Evidence over Rule
Defining 'asl' as evidence when added to a science, and refuting the view that it means a rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the meaning of 'asl' as evidence vs. rule when added to a science, and apply this distinction to identify the correct definition of usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
When added to a science, 'asl' means its evidence, not its rule—this unlocks the definition of usul al-fiqh.

In the study of usul al-fiqh, the term 'asl' (plural: usul) is foundational, but its precise meaning is debated. This passage examines whether 'asl' in 'usul al-fiqh' refers to evidence (dalil) or rules (qawa'id). The author argues strongly for the former when the term is added to a science, and refutes a prominent alternative view. Understanding this distinction is critical for properly grasping the subject matter and methodology of usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its context
Notice the phrase 'usul al-fiqh'. The author focuses on the meaning of 'asl' when it is added (idafah) to a science. List the three possible meanings he mentions: rajih (preponderant), mustashab (continuity), and qa'idah (rule), and see which he selects.
The term 'asl' has multiple uses in fiqh, but here the context is the science name.
2
Understand the rule of idafah
The author states a principle: when 'asl' is added to a science, it means the evidence (dalil) of that science. This is supported by the indicator (qarinah) of the addition itself. However, note the exception for usul al-tafsir and usul al-hadith.
This rule is not universal; it applies specifically to fiqh and similar sciences where evidence is the primary subject.
3
Examine the counter-argument and refutation
The author of al-Tawdih interpreted 'usul' in 'usul al-fiqh' as rules (qawa'id). The author refutes this with two points: (1) ignoring the idafah principle, and (2) if it meant rules, the meaning would be corrupted because the rules of a science are its issues (masa'il), not its evidences or principles. However, the lexical meaning of 'asl' (foundation) can encompass both evidence and rule, allowing the titular definition of usul al-fiqh as a science of rules.
The refutation relies on the distinction between the science's subject matter (evidences) and its derived issues (rules).
4
Synthesize the definition of usul al-fiqh
The author concludes that usul al-fiqh is the total evidence (adillah ijmaliyyah) for fiqh, which the mujtahid uses to derive detailed rulings. This ties the meaning of 'asl' as evidence to the practical purpose of the science.
The relationship between fiqh and usul is like that between a building and its foundation: the evidence supports the rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بعد ذكر تعريف الأصل لغة واصطلاحاً، أشار المصنف إلى تعيين المعنى الذي هو المراد هنا وقال:
أفيد ( في شرح مختصر الأصول أنه إذا أضيف (لفظ الأصل) إلى العلم كما قال هنا أصول الفقه فالمراد بالأصل دليله (دليل ذلك العلم)
فليس الأصول في قولهم : أصول الفقه بمعنى الراجح أو المستصحب أو القاعدة ، بل بمعنى الدليل، والقرينة الاضافة اضافة الأصول إلى الفقه)، ولكن لاتمشي هذه القاعدة قاعدة الاضافة) في أصول التفسير وأصول الحديث وغيرهما من أصول العلوم.
ولما حمل بعض الأصوليين كصاحب التوضيح) الأصول المضافة إلى (الفقه)
بمعنى القواعد، رد عليه المصنّف وقال:
فمن حمل (الأصل) على القاعدة فقد أخطأ) وغفل عن هذا الأصل ( وهو أن عند إضافة الأصل إلى علم يراد به الدليل على أن قواعد العلم
مسائله لا مباديه
وحاصل الردّ أنّ من حمل الأصل هنا على القاعدة كصاحب التوضيح في
التعريف اللقبي لأصول الفقه فقد أخطأ من وجهين:
الأول: غفلته عن هذا الأصل المعروف من أن الأصل) عند الاضافة إلى علم
يكون بمعنى الدليل.
والثاني: أن الأصول في اضافتها إلى علم إن كانت بمعنى القواعد يفسد المعنى ؛ لأن
قواعد العلم عبارة عن نفس مسائله لا عن دلائله ومباديه . نعم إذا أريد من الأصل الذي جمعه (أصول المعنى اللغوى ، وهو ما يبتنى عليه غيره وهو عام يشمل الدليل والقاعدة ، فقد يراد منه الدليل، فيقال أصول الفقه أي دلائله، وقد يراد منه القاعدة الأصولية التي يبتنى عليها جزئيات أصول الفقه، ومن أجل هذا عرفه المصنف لقباً بقوله : " فهو علم بقواعد يتوصل بها (المجتهد) إلى استنباط الأحكام الفقهية".
ثم هذا العلم علم أصول الفقه أدلة اجمالية للفقه يحتاج (يقع الاحتياج إليها عند تطبيق الدلائل التفصيلية على أحكامها (المدلولة)
هذه العبارة إشارة إلى بيان الربط بين الفقه وأصوله ، وأيضاً فيها إيماء إلى بيان المناسبة بين المعنى اللغوى (المنقول عنه للأصل ، وبين المعنى الاصطلاحي (المنقول
And after mentioning the lexical and technical definition of 'asl', the author pointed to determining the meaning intended here and said: It is beneficial (in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul) that when the word 'al-asl' is added to a science, as he said here 'usul al-fiqh', what is meant by 'al-asl' is its evidence (the evidence of that science). So 'al-usul' in their statement 'usul al-fiqh' is not in the meaning of the preponderant (al-rajih) or the continuous (al-mustashab) or the rule (al-qa'idah), but rather in the meaning of evidence (al-daleel), and the indicator (qareenah) is the addition of 'al-usul' to 'al-fiqh'. But this rule (the rule of addition) does not apply in usul al-tafsir, usul al-hadith, and other usul of sciences.
And when some usulis, like the author of al-Tawdih, interpreted 'al-usul' added to 'al-fiqh' as meaning the rules (qawā'id), the author refuted him and said: Whoever interprets 'al-asl' as 'al-qa'idah' has made an error and is heedless of this principle, which is that when 'al-asl' is added to a science, what is intended by it is the evidence; because the rules of a science are its issues (masā'il), not its principles (mabādi).
The summary of the refutation is that whoever interprets 'al-asl' here as 'al-qa'idah', like the author of al-Tawdih in the titular definition of usul al-fiqh, has erred in two ways:
First: His heedlessness of this well-known principle that 'al-asl', when added to a science, means evidence.
Second: If 'al-usul' in its addition to a science were in the meaning of rules, the meaning would be corrupted, because the rules of a science are its very issues, not its evidences and principles. Yes, if what is intended by 'al-asl' whose plural is 'al-usul' is the lexical meaning, which is that on which something else is built (i.e., foundation), and it is general, including evidence and rule, then it may be intended as evidence, so 'usul al-fiqh' means its evidences, and it may be intended as the usuli rule on which the particulars of usul al-fiqh are built, and for this reason the author defined it as a title by his statement: 'So it is a science of rules by which the mujtahid arrives at deriving fiqh rulings.'
Then this science is usul al-fiqh: the total evidence (adillah ijmaliyyah) for fiqh, which is needed when applying the detailed evidences to their rulings.
This statement is an indication of explaining the connection between fiqh and its usul, and it also alludes to explaining the relationship between the lexical meaning (transferred from) of 'asl' and the technical meaning (transferred to).
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that clarifies the meaning of the term 'al-asl' (plural: al-usul) when it is added to a science like 'usul al-fiqh'. The author argues that 'al-asl' in such constructions denotes 'daleel' (evidence), not 'qa'idah' (rule) or other possible meanings like 'rajih' (preponderant) or 'mustashab' (continuity). He supports this by citing a principle: when added to a science, 'asl' means evidence. He then refutes the view of some usulis, notably the author of al-Tawdih, who took 'usul' to mean 'qawā'id' (rules). The refutation has two points: (1) ignoring the established principle that 'asl' added to a science means evidence; (2) if it meant rules, the addition would be false because the rules of a science are its issues, not its principles. However, the author acknowledges that in the lexical sense ('foundation'), 'asl' can include both evidence and rule, and thus the definition of usul al-fiqh as a science of rules (qawā'id) is acceptable as a titular definition (ta'rif laqabi). The passage concludes by stating that usul al-fiqh is the total evidence (adillah ijmaliyyah) needed for deriving fiqh rulings, highlighting the relationship between the two sciences.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-aṣl): When added to a science (e.g., aṣl al-fiqh), it means the evidence (dalīl) of that science; in the lexical sense, it means that on which something is built (foundation), which can include both evidence and rule.
الدليل (al-dalīl): The evidence or proof that grounds a ruling.
القاعدة (al-qāʿidah): A rule (qa'idah) that summarizes the science's principles, not its evidence.
الأدلة الإجمالية (al-adillah al-ijmāliyyah): The total or general evidences (e.g., Quran, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) that are the subject of usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author reject the interpretation of 'asl' in 'usul al-fiqh' as 'qa'idah' (rule)?
A) Because 'qa'idah' does not have a lexical meaning that fits.
B) Because the rules of a science are its issues, not its evidence or principles.
C) Because all other usul sciences use 'asl' to mean evidence.
D) Because the author of al-Tawdih is not a reliable authority.

Key Takeaway
The term 'asl' in 'usul al-fiqh' means evidence (daleel), not rule (qa'idah), because when added to a science it indicates the evidence of that science, and interpreting it as rules corrupts the meaning since rules are the issues of the science.

Refuting the Analogy Between Usul and Logic: Why Fiqh Proofs Are the Building Blocks of Usul
Refutes the false comparison of usul al-fiqh's relation to fiqh with logic's relation to philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that usul al-fiqh relates to fiqh as logic relates to philosophy, and explain why the Hanafi school rejects this analogy based on the nature of detailed proofs.

Core Idea
A fiqh proof is itself an instance of a usul principle; a philosophical proof is not an instance of a logical topic.

Some scholars mistakenly compared the relationship of usul al-fiqh to fiqh to that of logic to philosophy. In this passage, al-Badakhshani dismantles that analogy by showing that the very proofs used in fiqh—like the Quranic verses about zakah and zina—are the building blocks of usul. In contrast, logic deals with secondary intelligibles, while philosophical proofs involve external existents, so they never overlap. This lesson will help you understand the intrinsic connection between usul and fiqh in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false analogy
State the analogy that some scholars propose: usul al-fiqh is to fiqh as logic is to philosophy. Understand that they view both as instrumental sciences.
The author labels this view as an error (wahm).
2
Grasp the Hanafi counter-claim
The Hanafi position is that the relation is fundamentally different because the detailed proofs (adillah tafsiliyya) of fiqh rulings are themselves individuals (afrad) of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh.
Usul does not merely provide tools; its subject matter includes the very evidence used in fiqh.
3
Examine the Quranic examples
Look at the two verses: 'Ātū al-zakāh' (imperative) and 'Lā taqrabū al-zinā' (prohibitive). Each is a detailed proof for a fiqh ruling (obligation of zakah, prohibition of zina) and simultaneously an instance of a usul topic (command indicates obligation, prohibition indicates prohibition). Trace how the imperative form 'ātū' is the matter and form that makes it an individual of the usul topic.
This demonstrates the overlap: the same textual phrase serves both as proof for fiqh and as example for usul.
4
Contrast with the logic-philosophy relationship
Logic investigates secondary intelligibles (universality, particularity, etc.), while philosophy's proofs involve external existents (primary intelligibles like the celestial sphere). A philosophical proof (e.g., 'the celestial sphere is spherical because it has a single nature') cannot be an individual of a logical topic because its subject is not a secondary intelligible. Hence the analogy fails.
The author provides a detailed example to solidify the distinction.

وليس نسبته (نسبة علم الأصول إلى الفقه كنسبة علم الميزان إلى الفلسفة كما وهم ؛ ثمّ ردّ على وهمه وخطئه) فقال : فانّ الدلائل التفصيلية (جزئيات الادلة (الأربعة بموادها وصورها من أفراد مسائل الأصول (أصول الفقه بخلاف المنطق الباحث عن المعقولات الثانية (أي بخلاف الدلائل الفلسفية ؛ فانها ليست بموادها

وكذلك حرمة الزنا مسألة فقهية ، ودليلها قوله تعالى ولا تقربوا الزنا و لا تقربوا " فرد من أفراد موضوع المسألة الأصولية ، وهي أن النهي للتحريم ، لأن لا تقربوا صيغة النهي ، فيكون فرد من أفراد النهي المطلق.

المثال التطبيقى : قولنا : "الفلك كروى الشكل مسألة من مسائل الفلسفة، ودليلها التفصيلى قولنا : لأن الفلك ذو طبيعة واحدة، لأنه جسم وكل جسم يكون ذا طبيعة واحدة فهو كروى الشكل، ينتج أنّ الفلك كروى الشكل، فالدليل التفصيلي لهذه المسألة الفلسفية قولنا : لأن الفلك ذو طبيعة واحدة، وهي صغرى الدليل، كما أنّ آتوا الزكوة دليل تفصيلى لمسألة فقهية، وهي أن الزكوة واجبة، وكانت الآية دليل الصغرى (لأنها مأمور بها).

And since some scholars have erroneously thought that the attachment of usul al-fiqh to fiqh is like the attachment of the science of measurement (logic) to (ancient) philosophy, the author refuted that group and said: And its relationship (the relationship of usul to fiqh) is not like the relationship of the science of measurement to philosophy as they erroneously thought; then he refuted his error and mistake and said: For the detailed proofs (particulars of the four proofs with their materials and forms) are individuals of the topics of usul (usul al-fiqh), unlike logic which investigates secondary intelligibles (i.e., unlike philosophical proofs; for they are not with their materials and forms individuals of the logical topics which investigate secondary intelligibles). And our teacher says: The relationship of usul al-fiqh to fiqh is not like the relationship of logic to philosophy; because the detailed proofs of fiqh issues with their materials and forms are among the individuals of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh topics. For their materials consist of the source [verb] with the subject and object, and their forms are the structure resulting from the imperative and prohibitive forms and others. For "And give the zakah" is a detailed proof for a fiqh issue, which is the obligation of zakah, and "And give the zakah" is an individual of the subject of the usul issue which is that the command indicates obligation because "give" is an imperative form. Similarly, the prohibition of zina is a fiqh issue, and its proof is His statement the Most High: "And do not approach zina" and "Do not approach" is an individual of the subject of the usul issue, which is that the prohibition indicates prohibition, because "do not approach" is a prohibitive form. Thus it is an individual of the absolute prohibition. Unlike logic, for it investigates secondary intelligibles (and the secondary intelligible is what occurs in the mind in the second order, like the universal and the particular, and the genus and the differentia. For man or Zaid or animal or rational occurs in the intellect firstly, then in the second order it occurs as being universal or particular or genus or differentia. This is the meaning of the secondary intelligible (whose plural is secondary intelligibles). And philosophy (or wisdom) investigates external existents, like the celestial sphere, water, fire, earth, and air. So the detailed proof for a philosophical issue is not an individual of the subject of a logical issue, because the subject of the proof in philosophy is from external existents, while the subject of a logical issue is from secondary intelligibles and mental matters. The applied example: Our statement: "The celestial sphere is spherical" is an issue of philosophy, and its detailed proof is our statement: "Because the celestial sphere has a single nature, because it is a body, and every body that has a single nature is spherical" resulting in that the celestial sphere is spherical. So the detailed proof for this philosophical issue is our statement "because the celestial sphere has a single nature" which is the minor premise of the proof, just as "And give the zakah" is a detailed proof for a fiqh issue, namely that zakah is obligatory, and the verse was the evidence of the minor premise (because it is commanded). And the detailed proof for the philosophical issue "because the celestial sphere has a single nature" is not, with its matter and form, an individual of the subject of a logical issue, because the subject of a logical issue is either a secondary intelligible or an individual of it or an essential accident of it (i.e., the subject of a logical issue is either a universal or a type of it, like man, or an essential accident of it, like laughter for man). And the celestial sphere in the mentioned proof is not any of these; rather it is from primary intelligibles like man and Zaid. So how can the subject of a philosophical proof be an individual of the subject of a logical issue? You will hardly find this [explanation] elsewhere. So take this and be among the thankful.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute, specifically a refutation of a mistaken analogy. The author (al-Badakhshani) addresses those who wrongly claim that usul al-fiqh relates to fiqh in the same way that logic relates to philosophy. He argues that this is false because the detailed proofs (adillah tafsiliyyah) of fiqh rulings—such as the Quranic verse 'And give zakah'—are themselves individuals (afrad) of the subject matter of usul al-fiqh. For example, the verse 'And give zakah' is a proof for the fiqh ruling of zakah obligation; simultaneously, it is an instance of the usul topic 'command indicates obligation' because it is an imperative. Similarly, 'Do not approach zina' is a proof for the prohibition of zina and is an instance of the usul topic 'prohibition indicates prohibition'. In contrast, logic deals with secondary intelligibles (ma'qulat thanawiyya)—concepts like universality, particularity, genus, and species that arise in the mind after primary concepts. Philosophy deals with external existents like the celestial sphere. The proof for a philosophical statement (e.g., 'the celestial sphere is spherical because it has a single nature') involves physical objects, which are primary intelligibles, not secondary. Therefore, such a proof cannot be an individual of a logical topic, which deals only with secondary intelligibles. Thus the analogy fails. The author emphasizes that the relationship is fundamentally different: usul al-fiqh's subject matter actually includes the very proofs of fiqh, whereas logic's subject matter is separate from philosophy's proofs.

Key Terms
المعقولات الثانية (al-ma'qulat al-thaniyya): Concepts that occur in the mind in a second order, such as universality, particularity, genus, and species, after primary concepts like man or Zaid have been grasped.
الدلائل التفصيلية (al-adillah al-tafsiliyya): Detailed proofs: particular evidence (Quran, Sunnah, etc.) used to derive a specific fiqh ruling, as opposed to the general principles of evidence studied in usul.
مادة وصورة (maddah wa surah): In the context of a proof: the 'matter' is the verbal components (source verb, subject, object), and the 'form' is the grammatical structure (e.g., imperative, prohibitive).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the analogy between usul al-fiqh:fiqh and logic:philosophy?
A) Because logic is not a science.
B) Because the detailed proofs of fiqh are individuals of usul topics, while philosophical proofs are not individuals of logical topics.
C) Because philosophy is older than logic.
D) Because usul al-fiqh does not deal with secondary intelligibles.

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh is not merely a tool for fiqh; its subject matter includes the very proof texts that constitute fiqh rulings, making the relationship intrinsic and fundamentally different from the logic-philosophy relationship.

Objections to the Definition of Fiqh and Their Resolution
Explains and resolves objections to the definition of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two objections to the Hanafi definition of fiqh and explain how they are resolved using the concepts of malakah and the mujtahid-muqallid distinction.

Core Idea
Fiqh requires a faculty (malakah) to derive rulings from speculative proofs – a muqallid cannot be called a faqih.

The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of Shari'i rulings from their detailed proofs' faces two critical objections. If it requires actual knowledge of all rulings, even great imams like Abu Hanifa would be excluded. If it requires only some rulings, a muqallid who knows a single ruling would be included. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi response that reinterprets 'knowledge' as a potential faculty (malakah) and 'proofs' as speculative proofs that yield certainty of obligation, thereby preserving the definition's exclusivity to mujtahids.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Recognize the objection: either 'all rulings' excludes great imams, or 'some rulings' includes muqallids. Understand why both are problematic for the definition.
The objection assumes 'knowledge' means actual knowledge and 'proofs' means any proof.
2
First answer – knowledge as malakah
The Hanafi response: 'knowledge' in the definition means the malakah (faculty) to derive rulings, not actual knowledge of every ruling. Thus Abu Hanifa and Malik still qualify as fuqaha' because they possess this faculty.
Malakah allows potential knowledge even if not actualized.
3
Second answer – proofs as zanni proofs
The Hanafi response: 'detailed proofs' refers specifically to zanni (speculative) proofs. Obtaining certainty of obligation from such proofs is a unique characteristic of mujtahids, excluding muqallids.
Muqallids rely on the qat'i statement of the mujtahid, not on zanni proofs.
4
Address the counter-objection on zann
An objection arises: how can zanni proofs lead to qat'i knowledge? The answer: the certainty is about the obligation to act (wujub al-'amal), not about the proof itself. This reconciles the speculative nature of the proofs with the definitive nature of fiqh.
Fiqh is qat'i in terms of bindingness, not in terms of the proof's certainty.
5
Apply the distinction
Distinguish between a mujtahid (has malakah, uses zanni proofs, obtains certainty of obligation) and a muqallid (relies on mujtahid's statement, does not derive from proofs). Test with examples like Abu Hanifa admitting ignorance on some issues.
The muqallid's basis is the qat'i statement of the mujtahid, not his own opinion.

-۲- قوله : من أدلتها التفصيلية: قال صاحب التوضيح (في شرح قيود تعريف الفقه تحت قوله : أي العلم الحاصل عن أدلتها الخ) فعلم أن قوله : عن أدلتها) متعلق بالعلم، فاضافة (الأدلة) الى الضمير العائد إلى (الأحكام) للعهد، والمعهود الأدلة الأربعة، فخرج عن تعريف الفقه علم المقلد بالمسائل الفقهية ; لأنّ علمه يحصل من قول مجتهده، أو من أقوال مقلديه، لا من الأدلة الأربعة . لأن أكثر علمه يحصل من قول مجتهده دون الأدلة. ثم أجاب المصنف عن هذا الايراد باعتبار الشقين وقال : وأجيب الخ. -١- وملخص الجواب باعتبار الشق الأول: أن المراد بالأحكام جميعها، والمراد بالعلم الملكة الحاصلة من التصديق بالمسائل، فلا شك أن أبا حنيفة ومالكاوكل فقيه عنده ملكة فقهية للاستنباط يستطيع العلم بجميع الأحكام لأجل تلك الملكة، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً له بالفعل، فالمراد بالعلم بالأحكام هو العلم بالقوة دون الفعل، فشمل تعريف الفقه علم هؤلاء الفقهاء، وصار التعريف جامعاً لأفراده . -۲ وحاصل الجواب باعتبار الشق الثاني : أن المراد بالأدلة في قوله ( من أدلتها التفصيلية الأمارات والعلامات أي الأدلة الظنية، فيكون التعريف مانعاً عن دخول الغير، وهو فقه المقلّد ; لأن تحصيل العلم بالأحكام لوجوب العمل من الأدلة الظنية إنما يكون عن خواص المجتهد، وليست هذه المرتبة حاصلة للمقلّد بالاجماع. فإن المقلّد لو حصل له العلم بوجوب العمل بمسألة شرعية عن دليل ظني لا يكون له سنداً ، ولا يجب عليه العمل بتلك المسألة، وكذا لا يكون سنداً لغيره، ولا يجب على غيره العمل بها أيضاً، فإن الشند للمقلّد قول المجتهد القطعي، لا قول المقلّد نفسه على الظن ، ولا قول المجتهد المبنى على الظن، وإنما يكون دليل المقلّد القول القطعى للمجتهد ، وهذا معنى قوله : " وأما المقلد فمستنده قول مجتهده لاظنه ولاظنه. و يرد على هذا الجواب إشكال : وهو أن الدلائل الظنية مفيدة للظن، والظن لا يكون موصلاً إلى العلم القطعى، والفقه علم قطعى ، فكيف يصح أن يراد من الأدلة الأمارات والأدلة الظنية ؟ والجواب عنه أن الظنّ هنا يفضى إلى العلم القطعى بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية، فالظن قد يفضي إلى العلم بوجوب العمل، وان لم يفض إلى الجزم بثبوت الدليل. وتقدير العبارة في الأصل هكذا : "بأنه العلم القطعى بتقدير الصفة) بالأحكام، أي بوجوب العمل ( بتقدير المضاف والمضاف اليه بالأحكام الشرعية. ثم أراد المصنّف تفصيل الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني وقال: و تحصيل العلم بوجوب العمل بتوسط أي بواسطة) الظنّ من خواص المجتهد إجماعاً و أما المقلد فمستنده (دليله) قول مجتهده (أي القول
-2- His statement: 'from its detailed proofs.' The author of al-Tawdih said (in explaining the conditions of the definition of fiqh under his statement: 'i.e., knowledge obtained from its proofs, etc.') So it is understood that his statement 'from its proofs' is attached to knowledge. The addition of 'proofs' to the pronoun referring to 'rulings' is for specification, and the specified proofs are the four proofs. Thus the knowledge of the muqallid of fiqhi issues exits from the definition of fiqh because his knowledge comes from the statement of his mujtahid or from the statements of his muqallids, not from the four proofs. And since the condition 'from the point of view of its being obtained from the proofs' is considered and estimated in the definition like other definitions, the knowledge of the Prophet exits from the definition of fiqh because his knowledge was obtained by revelation through the Book and Sunnah, not by consensus or analogy; and because the knowledge of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is intuitive, not theoretical and acquired, except in what he exercised ijtihad from rulings. Likewise, the knowledge of Jibril exits because it is intuitive, and his knowledge is not for acting upon them; and the knowledge of Allah Almighty also exits because it is presential, not acquired such that it needs intuition or consideration; and because knowledge from proof is only obtained after arranging the proof and its premises, and His knowledge is instantaneous, not gradual, and not dependent on acquisition and consideration. -3- His statement: 'And it is objected: if the intended meaning is all...' The gist of the objection is: either what is meant by 'rulings' in the definition of fiqh in his statement 'knowledge of rulings' is all Shari'i rulings, or what is meant is some of them? If the first is intended (knowledge of all rulings), then the definition does not apply to the knowledge of one who is like the sun at midday, such as Imam Abu Hanifa, for he did not claim knowledge of all rulings; rather he admitted not knowing some issues (like the issue of al-dahr) and said: 'I do not know what al-dahr is.' Likewise Imam Malik, for he admitted not knowing the answer to thirty-six fiqhi issues. So neither is the definition of faqih applicable to them. And if the second is intended (knowledge of some rulings), then the definition of fiqh would not be exclusive of others; thus the fiqh of the muqallid would enter, if he knows one or more issues from a detailed proof, while he is not a faqih because most of his knowledge comes from the statement of his mujtahid, not from proofs. Then the author responded to this objection considering both aspects and said: 'And the answer is...' -1- Summary of the answer considering the first aspect: that what is meant by 'rulings' is all of them, and what is meant by 'knowledge' is the malakah (faculty) resulting from affirming the issues. So there is no doubt that Abu Hanifa, Malik, and every faqih have a fiqhi faculty for istinbat (derivation) by which they can know all rulings due to that faculty, even if it is not actually obtained. So what is meant by 'knowledge of rulings' is knowledge in potency, not in actuality. Thus the definition of fiqh includes the knowledge of these fuqaha', and the definition becomes comprehensive of its individuals. -2- And the gist of the answer considering the second aspect: that what is meant by 'proofs' in his statement 'from its detailed proofs' are the indicators and signs, i.e., the zanni (speculative) proofs. Thus the definition becomes exclusive of others, i.e., the fiqh of the muqallid, because obtaining knowledge of rulings for the obligation of acting from zanni proofs is only a characteristic of the mujtahid, and this level is not attained by the muqallid by consensus. For if the muqallid obtains knowledge of the obligation of acting upon a Shari'i issue from a zanni proof, it is not a support for him, nor is he obliged to act upon that issue; likewise it is not a support for others, nor are others obliged to act upon it. Indeed, the support for the muqallid is the qat'i (definitive) statement of the mujtahid, not the muqallid's own opinion based on zann, nor the mujtahid's opinion based on zann. The proof for the muqallid is only the qat'i statement of the mujtahid. This is the meaning of his statement: 'As for the muqallid, his basis is the statement of his mujtahid, not his own zann nor his zann.' An objection arises against this answer: namely, that zanni proofs yield zann, and zann does not lead to qat'i knowledge, while fiqh is qat'i knowledge. So how can it be correct that by 'proofs' the indicators and zanni proofs are meant? The answer to this is that here zann leads to qat'i knowledge of the obligation of acting upon Shari'i rulings. Thus zann may lead to knowledge of the obligation of acting, even if it does not lead to certainty of the proof's validity. The phrasing in the original is thus: 'that it is qat'i knowledge (by estimating the attribute) of rulings, i.e., of the obligation of acting (by estimating the construct state) of Shari'i rulings.' Then the author intended to detail the answer by choosing the second aspect and said: 'And obtaining knowledge of the obligation of acting through (i.e., by means of) zann is a characteristic of the mujtahid by consensus. As for the muqallid, his basis (his proof) is the statement of his mujtahid (i.e., the statement).'
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the definition of fiqh given in the matn. The commentator, drawing from al-Tawdih, addresses two major objections. First, if fiqh requires knowledge of all Shari'i rulings, then even great imams like Abu Hanifa and Malik would not qualify because they admitted ignorance of some issues. The answer distinguishes between actual knowledge (al-ilm bil-fi'l) and potential knowledge (al-ilm bil-quwwa) as a faculty (malakah) for deriving rulings. Thus, a mujtahid possesses the malakah that enables him to know all rulings potentially, even if he hasn't actually derived every one. Second, if fiqh requires only knowledge of some rulings from detailed proofs, then a muqallid who knows even one ruling from a proof would be a faqih — but he is not, by consensus. The answer here is that 'detailed proofs' in the definition refer specifically to zanni (speculative) proofs, and obtaining certain knowledge of obligation from zanni proofs is exclusive to mujtahids. A muqallid's basis is the qat'i statement of his mujtahid, not his own opinion or the mujtahid's zann. The passage then addresses a further objection: how can zanni proofs lead to qat'i knowledge? The resolution is that the qat'i knowledge obtained is of the obligation to act (wujub al-'amal), not necessarily certainty in the proof itself. This is a classic Hanafi usul position that fiqh is qat'i in terms of obligation derived from speculative proofs.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Knowledge of Shari'i rulings from detailed (speculative) proofs, requiring a malakah (faculty) for derivation, resulting in certainty of obligation to act.
الملكة (al-malakah): A firmly established faculty (habitus) in the soul enabling the mujtahid to derive rulings from proofs, even if he has not actually derived every ruling.
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): One who possesses the malakah of istinbat and can derive rulings from detailed speculative proofs.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows the ruling of a mujtahid without knowing the proof; his basis is the qat'i statement of the mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason that a muqallid is not considered a faqih according to the Hanafi definition?
A) Because he does not know all the rulings.
B) Because he lacks the malakah and his basis is the qat'i statement of the mujtahid, not derivation from zanni proofs.
C) Because his knowledge comes from taqlid, which is forbidden.
D) Because he only knows rulings from the Qur'an and Sunnah, not from consensus or analogy.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh carefully balances inclusiveness of mujtahids and exclusiveness of muqallids through the concepts of malakah and the epistemic role of zanni proofs.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Classification of Hujjiyya of Ijma' and Qiyas: A Usul Dispute
Dispute on disciplinary classification of hujjiyya of ijma' and qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three positions on the classification of hujjiyya of ijma' and qiyas, evaluate the arguments for each, and apply the Hanafi tarjih to determine that it belongs to kalam.

Core Idea
The authority of ijma' and qiyas is a theological matter, not a fiqh ruling—because obligation to act is its fruit, not its essence.

This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut addresses a foundational question: in which science do we investigate the authority (hujjiyya) of ijma' (consensus) and qiyas (analogy)? Some argued it belongs to fiqh because it concerns obligation; others claimed it is so self-evident that no investigation is needed. The Hanafi position, upheld by Ibn al-Humam, places it in kalam (theology). Let's unpack the reasoning behind each view and see why the kalam classification prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three opinions: (1) hujjiyya of ijma' and qiyas is a fiqh issue; (2) it is a self-evident (badihiyya) matter not requiring investigation; (3) it belongs to kalam, similar to investigating the Book and Sunnah.
The author declares the third opinion as the correct one.
2
Analyze the first opinion and its refutation
The first opinion claims that since 'ijma' is authoritative' means acting upon it is obligatory, and obligation is a fiqh concept, the investigation falls under fiqh. The Hanafi response: (a) obligation is a branch of authority, not identical to it; (b) authority can also yield permissibility or prohibition, not just obligation.
This refutation uses the branch/root distinction.
3
Analyze the second opinion and its refutation
The second opinion claims that hujjiyya is self-evident and needs no investigation. Refutation: (a) disagreement among scholars proves it is not self-evident; (b) even if the effects (rulings derived via ijma'/qiyas) are clear, the authority in itself is theoretical.
The analogy to the oneness of God is instructive: clear to common sense but still requires theological proof.
4
Conclude with the tarjih
The matn author (Ibn al-Humam) declared the third opinion correct, so the Hanafi relied-upon position is that investigating the hujjiyya of ijma' and qiyas belongs to kalam. This determines the nature of proofs used.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if fiqh, proofs would be from within fiqh; if kalam, proofs are theological (e.g., rational arguments for the authority of consensus).

(۲) وحاصل الرد ثانياً: أن وجوب العمل كما يثبت بالاجماع والقياس كذلك جواز العمل بل كراهته أيضاً يثبت بالاجماع أو القياس ؛ لأن صفة العمل الذي هو فعل المكلف ليس بمنحصر في الوجوب، بل الجواز والاباحة والكراهة أيضاً من صفات فعل المكلف، وبالاجماع والقياس يثبت جميع صفات عمل المكلف، فالقول بأن معنى حجية الاجماع والقياس أن وجوب العمل يكون مقتضاهما خطأ؛ فإنه جاز أن يكون معناها جواز العمل أو كراهة العمل بمقتضاهما ، فإنّها أيضاً من ثمرات حجيتهما . القول الثاني ومن قال ليست (حجية الاجماع والقياس) مسألة أصلاً، (لا) من الفقه ولا من غيره لأنها ضرورة دينية، فقد بعد عن الحق وان سُلّم إِنَّا فلا يُسلّم لِمَاً -٣- شرح القول الثاني : أي قال هذا القائل: ان المسألة : "الاجماع حجة أو القياس حجة ليست مسألة نظرية أصلاً، لا من الفقه ولا من أصوله ولا من الكلام، لأن مسائل هذه الفنون (العلوم) تكون نظرية يبحث عن ثبوتها وعللها، ومسألة حجيتهما مسألة بديهية من ضروريات و بديهيات الدين، فلا حاجة إلى البحث عنها. الرد على هذا القول : قال المصنف في الرد عليه فقد بعد هذا القائل عن الحق، أي لا نسلم أولاً أن تكون هذه المسألة بديهية، وإلّا فما اختلف في حجيتهما أحد، وقد اختلفوا، كما يدل عليه قول المصنف ( لأنها كثر فيها الشعب) وان سلّمنا بداهة هذه المسألة وكونها من ضروريات الدين إنا أي من حيث الاستدلال من المعلول إلى العله أي من بداهة المسائل الثابتة بالاجماع والقياس إلى بداهة حجية الاجماع والقياس، فلا نسلّم لما ، أي من حيث الاستدلال من العلة إلى المعلول، أي من نفس الاجماع والقياس إلى بداهة المسائل الثابتة بهما. فيجوز أن تكون حجية الاجماع والقياس باعتبار معلولهما وهو وجوب العمل أو جواز العمل بالمسائل الاجماعية أو القياسية بديهية وباعتبار علتهما (حجية الاجماع والقياس نظرية، كما أن مسألة توحيد البارى عند الناس بديهية، وأما في نفسها فليست
Removing Dread from the Difficulties of Musallam al-Thubut: It is a self-evident matter from the necessities of religion. The third (opinion): that it is from the matters of kalam, like the investigation of the Book and Sunnah, and the author made this statement correct. He referred to the first opinion then refuted it, and said: What is said that the investigation of the authority of ijma' and qiyas is from fiqh; because the meaning (i.e., the intended meaning of their authority) is that acting upon them is obligatory (then he began refuting this opinion, saying: In it (firstly) that this is a branch of authority, and (secondly) that permissibility of action is also among its fruits and implications). Evidence: Explanation of the first opinion: When he established that the subject of usul al-fiqh is the four evidences in terms of leading (to rulings), he now indicates a disputed matter regarding the investigation of the authority of two of the four, namely ijma' and qiyas: which science does this matter belong to? He mentioned the first opinion and said: It is said that the investigation of their authority is from fiqh, because what is meant by the statement "ijma' is authoritative, qiyas is authoritative" and its purpose is that acting upon them is obligatory. So the subject of this statement (this proposition) is action, and its predicate is obligatory, and every matter in which the investigation is about the obligation of action is from fiqh. So it is established that the investigation of the authority of ijma' and qiyas is from fiqh. Explanation of the refutation of it: (1) The summary of the refutation firstly: I do not concede that the meaning of their statement "ijma' is authoritative, qiyas is authoritative" is that acting upon them is obligatory. Rather, this matter, that acting upon ijma' and qiyas is obligatory, is a branch dependent on the authority of ijma' and qiyas, or on the issues that ijma' is authoritative and qiyas is authoritative. Because if their authority is not established, the obligation of acting upon them is not established. So the conclusion is that the obligation of acting upon ijma' and qiyas is a branch dependent on them, and their authority is the root and is depended upon. So the obligation of acting upon them is not identical to their authority. (2) The summary of the refutation secondly: Just as the obligation of action is established by ijma' and qiyas, similarly the permissibility of action, nay, even its prohibition, is established by ijma' or qiyas. Because the attribute of action, which is the act of the obligated person, is not confined to obligation; rather, permissibility, permissibility (mubah), and prohibition are also attributes of the act of the obligated person. And by ijma' and qiyas all attributes of the act of the obligated person are established. So the statement that the meaning of the authority of ijma' and qiyas is that obligation of action follows from them is wrong; for it is possible that their meaning is permissibility of action or prohibition of action according to them, for these are also among the fruits of their authority. The second opinion: And whoever said that the authority of ijma' and qiyas is not an issue at all, neither from fiqh nor from other sciences, because it is a religious necessity, has strayed from the truth. And if conceded, we do not concede why. Explanation of the second opinion: That is, this speaker said: The issue "ijma' is authoritative" or "qiyas is authoritative" is not a theoretical issue at all, neither from fiqh nor from its principles (usul) nor from kalam, because the issues of these disciplines (sciences) are theoretical, investigating their establishment and causes. But the issue of their authority is a self-evident matter from the necessities and self-evidences of religion, so there is no need to investigate it. Refutation of this opinion: The author said in refuting him: This speaker has strayed from the truth. That is, firstly we do not concede that this issue is self-evident; otherwise, no one would have differed about its authority, yet they have differed, as indicated by the author's statement (because there is much branching in it). And if we concede the self-evidence of this issue and that it is from the necessities of religion, we do not concede why — meaning from the perspective of reasoning from effect to cause, i.e., from the self-evidence of issues established by ijma' and qiyas to the self-evidence of the authority of ijma' and qiyas. So it is possible that the authority of ijma' and qiyas, in terms of their effect, which is the obligation or permissibility of acting upon ijma'i or qiyasi issues, is self-evident; but in terms of their cause (the authority itself), it is theoretical, just as the issue of the oneness of the Creator is self-evident to people, but in itself it is not.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding the classification of the topic of hujjiyya (authoritativeness) of ijma' (consensus) and qiyas (analogy). The author, Anwar al-Badakhshani, is commenting on Musallam al-Thubut. He states that the correct view (according to the matn author) is that this investigation belongs to kalam, like investigating the Qur'an and Sunnah. Then he presents two incorrect views and refutes them. The first view is that the investigation of hujjiyya of ijma' and qiyas is part of fiqh because its meaning is that acting upon them is obligatory. The author refutes this in two ways: (1) The obligation to act is a branch of their authority, not the same as authority itself. (2) The authority of ijma' and qiyas can also establish permissibility or prohibition, not just obligation. The second view is that this issue is not a theoretical matter at all but self-evident from religion. The author refutes this by noting that scholars have indeed differed over it, showing it is not self-evident. Even if we concede its self-evidence in terms of effects (the rulings derived), the authority itself remains theoretical, similar to the oneness of God which is self-evident to people but requires proof in itself. Thus, the investigation of hujjiyya belongs to kalam, not fiqh or as a self-evident matter.

Key Terms
حجية (hujjiyya): The quality of being an authoritative proof (hujja) that must be followed.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the Muslim community on a legal ruling after the death of the Prophet.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning that extends a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illa).
بديهية (badihiyya): A self-evident truth that requires no proof; a necessary cognition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is the correct Hanafi position on the classification of the investigation of hujjiyya of ijma' and qiyas?
A) It belongs to fiqh because it concerns obligation to act.
B) It is a self-evident matter that does not require investigation.
C) It belongs to kalam, like the investigation of the Qur'an and Sunnah.
D) It is part of usul al-fiqh proper without further division.

Key Takeaway
Investigating the authority of ijma' and qiyas belongs to kalam because it concerns the proof of the proofs themselves, not the rulings derived from them.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Second Definition of Essential (al-Dhati) and the Rebuttal of the Possibility Objection
Definition of essential and rebuttal of an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the second definition of essential (al-dhati) and explain why possibility (al-imkan) is not essential by applying the Hanafi usul argument that if possibility were caused, it would invert the nature of the possible.

Core Idea
Essential requires no cause; possibility is accidental because if caused, it would negate possible status.

This lesson delves into a classical definition of 'essential' (al-dhati) and a nuanced rebuttal. The second definition holds that an essential attribute exists without any external cause—the essence itself suffices. But an objector raises: what about possibility (al-imkan)? It seems to require no cause, yet it is considered accidental. The author's response reveals a deeper argument that possibility, if caused, would collapse into necessity or impossibility—an absurdity. Understanding this logical move sharpens your grasp of how Hanafi usul classifies attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second definition
Locate in the passage the statement that the essential (al-dhati) is that which does not need a cause for its affirmation and existence; the essence itself indicates it.
This is the baseline definition under attack.
2
Spot the objection
The objector introduces possibility (al-imkan) as a counterexample: it is established for a possible being without any cause, yet it is accidental, not essential. Understand why this challenges the definition—if definition allows uncaused attributes, why is possibility not essential?
The objection targets the sufficiency of the definition.
3
Follow the first response
The author suggests a possible defense: the essence's 'requirement' of possibility could be considered a cause. But this is quickly set aside. Grasp why this move is insufficient—it leads to a second, more decisive proof.
This is a provisional reply; the main rebuttal follows.
4
Grasp the second proof
The second proof argues: if possibility were due to a cause, then when that cause is absent or ineffective, the possible thing would cease to be possible—it would become either necessary or impossible. That would invert the fundamental nature of the possible (which must remain possible regardless). This is absurd, so possibility cannot be caused.
The proof uses a rational reductio ad absurdum (dalil aqli).
5
Conclude the classification
Since possibility is not caused, it meets the definition's negative condition, but it is still accidental—it is not part of the quiddity of the possible. Thus the definition of essential stands: an attribute that is both uncaused AND constitutive of the essence. Possibility is only the former.
The definition is refined: essential = uncaused + part of the essence; accidental = uncaused but not part of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل التعريف الثاني : أن الذاتي هو ما لا يحتاج في ثبوته ووجوده إلى علة، بل تكفي نفس الذات للدلالة عليه. ثم ذكر النقض الوارد على التعريف الثاني وقال : "ونقض بصفة الإمكان" ; لأنها ثابتة لذات الممكن من غير الاحتياج إلى علّة، مع أنها عرضى لها، وليس بذاتى للمكن; إذ لا يثبت صفة الإمكان بدليل آخر غير اقتضاء الذات اياه، نعم، إلا أن يقال: إن اقتضاء الذات علة لثبوت الإمكان، ولكن يَرُدُّ هذا الاقتضاء الدليل الثاني الآتى . وهذا نقض إجمالي سنده (دليله ( قوله : "اذ لا إمكان بالغير "، والدليل الثاني أن الإمكان لوكان لأجل العلة، فيلزم عند عدم تاثير العلة أو عدمها أن لا يكون الممكن ممكناً لذاته، بل إمّا أن يكون واجباً، وإما أن يكون ممتنعاً ، وهذا قلب الحقائق وهو محال ; لأن كل مفهوم يتصوره العقل لا يخلو عن الأحوال الثلاث الإمكان أو الوجوب أو الامتناع، فالممكن باعتبار الذات يحتمل إحدى هذه الثلاث، فإذا لم يبق ممكناً باعتبار الذات، فلا يخلو عن الاثنين الباقيين الواجب أو الممتنع.
And the summary of the second definition: that the essential is that which does not need a cause in its affirmation and existence, rather the essence itself suffices for indicating it. Then he mentioned the objection entered against the second definition and said: 'And it is refuted by the attribute of possibility'; because it is established for the essence of the possible without needing a cause, while it is accidental to it, and is not essential to the possible; for the attribute of possibility is not established by another proof other than the essence's requirement of it, yes, unless it is said that the essence's requirement is a cause for the affirmation of possibility, but this requirement is rebutted by the second proof that follows. This is a summary objection whose basis (proof) is his statement: 'since there is no possibility by another', and the second proof is that if possibility were due to a cause, then when the cause is ineffective or absent, it would follow that the possible would not be possible in its essence, but rather it would be either necessary or impossible, and this is an inversion of realities and is absurd; because every concept that the mind imagines is free from the three states: possibility, necessity, or impossibility. So the possible, by virtue of essence, bears one of these three; so if it does not remain possible by virtue of essence, it is not free from the remaining two: the necessary or the impossible.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the second definition of 'essential' (al-dhati) and defends it against a specific objection. The definition states that the essential is that whose existence does not require an external cause; the essence itself is sufficient to indicate it. The objection raised is that the attribute of possibility (al-imkan) seems to fit this definition: it is established for a possible being without a cause, yet it is considered an accidental attribute (aradi), not essential. The author replies by distinguishing that the requirement of the essence for possibility could be considered a cause, but then a second proof refutes that: if possibility were caused, then if the cause fails, the thing would either become necessary or impossible, which is absurd. Thus, possibility is not caused by an external cause but is rather inherent in the essence, yet it is still accidental because it is not part of the quiddity. This discussion is about ontological categories: essential vs. accidental. The Hanafi usul tradition uses such analysis to understand how attributes relate to the essence.

Key Terms
الذاتي (al-dhati): Essential attribute that does not need a cause for its existence; the essence itself suffices to indicate it.
الإمكان (al-imkan): Possibility; the state of being possible (i.e., neither necessary nor impossible).
العرضي (al-aradi): Accidental attribute that is not part of the essence but attaches to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the attribute of possibility not considered essential (dhati) for a possible being?
A) Because it requires an external cause.
B) Because it is not established by the essence itself.
C) Because if it were essential, then when the cause fails the thing would become necessary or impossible, which is absurd.
D) Because possibility is a mental concept, not real.

Key Takeaway
The second definition of essential requires the attribute to be uncaused and constitutive of the essence; possibility is only uncaused, hence accidental.

Objection of Imam al-Razi to Real Definition and the Hanafi Reply
Presents al-Razi's critique and Hanafi reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Imam al-Razi's four-part objection to real definition and evaluate the Hanafi response using the concept of tahsil al-hasil.

Core Idea
Detailed conceptualization distinguishes real definition from tautology.

Imam al-Razi raised a powerful logical objection: how can a definition tell us something new without falling into circularity? This lesson unpacks his four arguments and the Hanafi counter-argument, which defends real definition as a valid tool for clarifying legal concepts in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Razi's four possibilities
List the four ways a definition could be attempted: by itself, by all its parts, by some parts, or by accidents.
These exhaust the logical options.
2
Analyze each possibility's flaw
For each, explain why al-Razi considers it invalid: self-definition is circular, parts constitute the thing, etc.
The core flaw is tahsil al-hasil (obtaining what is already obtained).
3
Understand the Hanafi reply
The reply argues that arranging parts in sequence (e.g., genus then differentia) creates a new mental image not previously present.
This yields new knowledge, avoiding circularity.
4
Apply to usul al-fiqh definitions
Consider how this applies to defining terms like 'wajib' or 'haram' — real definition allows precise conceptualization.
Hanafi usul utilizes real definition; Mutakallimin may prefer nominal definition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Continuation of the discussion of the definition with the objection of Imam al-Razi and the answer to it. He said: And he objected to the definition of quiddity that either it is by itself, or by its parts—this is obtaining what is already obtained—or by the accidents which are external, so the reality is not obtained by them. As for the answer to it: indeed the perceptions related to the parts (parts of the definition) in detail, when arranged by preceding the general (like 'animal') and delaying the specific (like 'rational'), and restricting by making the specific a quality of the general, then this totality is the definition that leads to the unitary image related to all the parts (parts of the definition). And it (i.e., the totality) is the defined, so there—in the place of real definition—an obtaining of something that was not obtained, so contemplate. And it is obtaining what is already obtained. As for the nominal definition which is by accidents, the reality is not obtained by it; yes, by it—nominal definition—distinction of the reality from others can occur if it is by properties (khawass) rather than the general accident. And the aspect of the necessity of obtaining what is already obtained in definition: that defining any quiddity among quiddities or reality among realities—for quiddity is the reality after it occurs as an answer to the question 'what is it?'—does not escape from one of four possibilities: either its definition is by itself, or by all of its parts, or by some of its parts, or by its accidents, and all are invalid. 1. As for the invalidity of the first possibility: defining a thing by itself is obtaining what is already obtained, i.e., obtaining the thing that was already obtained, and this is vain; also it necessitates the priority of the thing (insofar as it is the definer) over itself insofar as it is the defined) for the definition is prior to the defined. 2. As for the second possibility: all the parts are the same thing in detail, so defining by them is defining by the thing itself with also the entailment of the impossibility (i.e., the priority of the thing over itself and the priority of parts over the whole). 3. As for the third possibility: if the whole (all parts) does not yield knowledge, rather it entails the thing's priority over itself and the priority of parts, whole or part, over the whole is prohibited, so how can definition by some parts be? 4. As for the fourth possibility (definition by accidents): it is not possible to know the reality by the accidents external to it, for they do not yield knowledge of the reality; yes, distinction of the reality from all others is obtained by them if the accidents are of the properties; otherwise no. So it is established that defining quiddity (reality) is not possible by real definition nor by nominal definition. Detailed answer: that the perceptions related to the parts, i.e., the parts of the definition in detail, such as the perception of 'animal' and the perception of 'rational' in defining human, or the perception of the word, the meaning, and the position...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents a classic objection by Imam al-Razi (from the Mutakallimin tradition) against the possibility of real definition (al-ta'rif al-haqiqi). He argues that definition cannot be by the thing itself (circular), by its parts (also circular since parts constitute the thing), or by its accidents (which are external and do not convey the essence). Only nominal definition (al-ta'rif al-rasmi) using distinctive properties may distinguish the reality but does not define its essence. The Hanafi reply, as presented by the commentator, argues that detailed conceptualization of the parts (such as the genus and differentia) when properly arranged yields a new, unitary mental image that was not previously present. Thus, real definition does produce new knowledge; it is not a mere repetition. This issue touches on the nature of definition in logic and its application in usul al-fiqh for clarifying concepts like 'wajib' or 'haram'. The Hanafi position defends the utility of real definition.

Key Terms
تعريف حقيقي (ta'rif haqiqi): Real definition that conveys the essence by listing the essential parts (genus and differentia).
تعريف رسمي (ta'rif rasmi): Nominal definition that distinguishes the thing by its accidents, especially properties (khawass).
تحصيل الحاصل (tahsil al-hasil): Obtaining something already obtained; a logical fallacy of tautology or circularity.
الماهية (al-mahiyyah): Quiddity or essence; what a thing is in answer to 'what is it?'
العوارض (al-awarid): Accidents that are external to the essence but attach to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, Imam al-Razi objects to real definition because it leads to which logical problem?
A) It requires infinite regress.
B) It obtains what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل).
C) It is based on unprovable premises.
D) It cannot be communicated.

Key Takeaway
Real definition avoids circularity when its parts are presented sequentially, yielding a new unified concept.

Ibn al-Hajib's Objection on Productivity and Its Refutation
Presents and refutes Ibn al-Hajib's claim on syllogistic productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn al-Hajib's objection that only the first syllogistic figure is productive, and evaluate the Hanafi refutation based on the concept of entailment (luzūm).

Core Idea
Productivity is not exclusive to the first figure; entailment holds even after conversion.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, syllogistic logic (mantiq) is used to derive rulings through qiyas. A key dispute arose when Ibn al-Hajib claimed that only the first figure of a syllogism is spontaneously productive—meaning the conclusion follows without any transformation. This lesson presents his objection and the Hanafi response, clarifying that productivity can be mediate (through conversion) and still valid. Understanding this dispute helps you appreciate the rigor of logical reasoning in istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn al-Hajib's position
Read the passage and pinpoint Ibn al-Hajib's claim: 'there is no productivity except by the first figure.' Note that he means immediate, self-evident entailment without any conversion.
This is called 'intāj badīhī'.
2
Understand the basis of his objection
Ibn al-Hajib argues that true entailment (luzūm) is a direct relationship between premises and conclusion. If conversion is needed, the conclusion is not entailed by the original premises but by the converted ones.
He views the conversion as altering the premises.
3
Present the Hanafi refutation
The author responds that luzūm is not particular to the first figure. The conclusion still follows necessarily from the converted premises; conversion is merely a means to reveal the entailment.
Rotation of the conclusion with the first figure does not negate productivity.
4
Analyze the concept of entailment (luzūm)
The key point: entailment can be immediate or mediate. In mediate entailment, the conclusion depends on the premises after a logical operation (like conversion). This does not make the syllogism unproductive; it only means the validity is not self-evident but proven.
Compare with the Hanafi principle that qiyas is valid even if it requires middle terms.
5
Evaluate the practical implication
In Hanafi istinbat, jurists often use the second and third figures. This refutation affirms that such syllogisms are logically sound after proper conversion, thus supporting their use in deriving fiqh rulings.
The dispute shows that methodological rigor does not preclude broader logical tools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض ابن الحاجب على انتاج الشكل الثاني والجواب عنه و ما في مختصر المنتهى لابن الحاجب من أنه لا انتاج إلّا بالشكل) الأول ، فادّعاء؛ لأنّ اللزوم (لزوم النتيجة للقياس لا يكون لأجل مقدمة أجنبية، ويجوز أن يكون ذلك اللزوم) مع متعدد من الأشكال) ولا يكون اللزوم) خاصاً بالشكل الأول ودوران النتيجة مع الشكل الأول (وجوداً وعدماً) لا ينافي الانتاج أو اللزوم مع بقية الأشكال حاصل الاعتراض : أنّ المصنف ذكر في السّلّم أن إنتاج الأشكال الأربعة إنما يكون بالرد الشكل الثاني إلى الأول بعكس الكبرى، وبرد الشكل الثالث إلى الأول بعكس الصغرى وبرد الشكل الرابع إلى الأول بعكس المقدمتين، أو بعكس ترتيب الصغرى والكبرى، وذكر في هذا الكتاب (المسلّم) انتاج الأشكال الثلاثة غير الشكل الرابع واعترض عليه ابن الحاجب في المختصر بقوله لا انتاج إلا بالشكل الأول أي الانتاج البديهي من غير حاجة إلى التغيير والرد، إنما يكون بالشكل الأول فقط، فذكر انتاج سائر الأشكال اشتغال بما لا يعنيه .
Objection of Ibn al-Hajib on the productivity of the second figure and the answer to it. And what is in Mukhtasar al-Muntaha by Ibn al-Hajib: that there is no productivity except by the first figure. So it is a claim, because the entailment (the entailment of the conclusion to the syllogism) is not due to an extraneous premise, and it is permissible that this entailment be with multiple figures, and the entailment is not particular to the first figure. The rotation of the conclusion with the first figure (in existence and non-existence) does not negate productivity or entailment with the rest of the figures. The summary of the objection: that the author (of al-Sullam) mentioned that the productivity of the four figures is only by converting the second figure to the first by inverting the major, converting the third figure to the first by inverting the minor, and converting the fourth figure to the first by inverting both premises or by reversing the order of minor and major. And he mentioned in this book (al-Musallam) the productivity of the three figures other than the fourth. Ibn al-Hajib objected in al-Mukhtasar saying: there is no productivity except by the first figure, i.e., primary productivity without need for change and conversion is only in the first figure; so mentioning productivity of other figures is engaging in what does not concern him.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a logical dispute in the context of syllogistic theory used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Ibn al-Hajib, in his Mukhtasar al-Muntaha, argues that only the first figure is spontaneously productive—that is, the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises without any transformation. The author of al-Musallam (al-Bihari) held that the other figures are also productive after conversion (al-radd) to the first figure. The commentator (al-Badakhshani) refutes Ibn al-Hajib by arguing that entailment (luzūm) is not exclusive to the first figure; the conclusion still follows even if conversion is needed. The fact that the conclusion depends on the first figure after conversion does not negate productivity; it merely means that the validity is mediated. Thus, it is not 'engagement in that which does not concern him' but rather a legitimate extension of logical reasoning.

Key Terms
انتاج (intāj): The property of a syllogism such that its conclusion necessarily follows from its premises.
لزوم (luzūm): The entailment relationship between premises and conclusion.
رد (radd): Conversion or transformation of a syllogistic figure to the first figure by operations like inversion of premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only the first figure is self-evidently productive without need for conversion
D) The second figure is invalid

Key Takeaway
Entailment (luzūm) is not exclusive to the first figure; other figures are productive after conversion, affirming their validity in Hanafi istinbat.
