The Epistemological Validity of Nazar: Refuting the Samaniyya
Refuting the Samaniyya who deny nazar yields ilm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that nazar yields knowledge and evaluate the Samaniyya's argument against it.

Core Idea
Nazar yields knowledge; correct reasoning is discernible by its attributes and the alternative of pure sense-perception is insufficient.

The Samaniyya, an Indian sect that worshipped idols, denied that theoretical reasoning (nazar) can produce certain knowledge. They argued that since nazar can result in ignorance as well, we cannot be certain of its outcomes. The Hanafi school affirms that correct nazar is distinguishable by its unique attributes and that relying solely on sense-perception cannot provide universal knowledge necessary for jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
The Samaniyya claim that nazar does not yield knowledge because the conviction it produces may be ignorance that does not conform to reality. They argue that there is no certain way to distinguish between knowledge and ignorance obtained through nazar.
This is a skeptical challenge: because error is possible, certainty cannot be attained.
2
Reconstruct the Samaniyya's syllogism
The commentator reconstructs their reasoning as a hidden syllogism: major premise: nothing from intelligibles yields knowledge; minor premise: nazar is from intelligibles; conclusion: nazar does not yield knowledge. The major premise is supported by the claim that conviction from nazar may be ignorance.
This makes explicit the logical structure behind their denial.
3
Analyze the initial Hanafi response
The author of the original matn (Musallam al-Thubut) gives two responses: (1) knowledge is distinguished from ignorance by its attributes (e.g., disclosure and distinctness), and (2) intuition (badahah) judges that correct nazar yields knowledge.
The response relies on the existence of distinguishing features and innate human recognition.
4
Examine the rebuttal to the initial response
The commentator then points out a problem: even if knowledge has unique attributes, how do we know that a particular instance of nazar is correct? The possibility of error exists at every step from premises to conclusions, leading to a regress.
This shows that the initial response does not fully address the skeptical challenge.
5
Identify the final Hanafi resolution
The commentator resolves by denying the similarity between knowledge and ignorance – they are fundamentally different, not just two sides of the same coin. Also, sense-perception only yields particular knowledge, not universal truths, so it cannot replace nazar.
This aligns with the Hanafi school's affirmation of the validity of rational deduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبدأ الأول من المبادى الكلامية

فحكماء الهند الذين كانوا يعبدون الصنم المسمى بـ (السومنات) والذي كسره محمود الغزنوي في عهد سلطنته واستولى على الهند وكانوا يقولون : لا علم إلا بالحس، فهذا الكلام إشارة إلى الكبرى التي صغراها محذوفة، وهي أن النظر من المعقولات، ولا شيء من المعقولات بمفيد للعلم، فلا شيء من النظر بمفيد للعلم، الصغرى ظاهرة، لأن النظر هو ترتيب المعقول لتحصيل المجهول، وأثبت المصنف الكبرى بقوله : لأن الجزم الحاصل بالنظر قد يكون جهلاً غير مطابق للواقع، أي سلمنا أن الأمور المعقولة تفيد الجزم بشيء، ولكن قد يكون ذلك الجزم جهلاً أيضاً، فكيف نعلم أن ما حصل بالنظر هو علم لا جهل؟ فكلاهما أي العلم والجهل يحصلان بالنظر
وحاصل الجواب أن العلم الحاصل بالنظر يمتاز عن الجهل بالعوارض، فإن عوارض العلم من الانكشاف والوصول إلى امتياز المعلوم عن غيره غير عوارض الجهل، وبالنظر الصحيح نميز بين عوارض العلم وعوارض الجهل، فإذا كان النظر صحيحاً توجد عوارض العلم، وإذا كان فاسداً فالعوارض عوارض الجهل، فالفرق بين الجزم الذي هو علم وبين الجزم الذي هو جهل ثابت بالنظر .
ثم رد على هذا الجواب وقال : "وفيه " في هذا الجواب إشكال بأنه بماذا يعلم ويفرق بأن ذلك النظر الذي حصل منه الجزم نظر صحيح أم لا : فإن هذا الاحتمال (احتمال الخطأ) قائم وموجود من المبادى (الأمور المعلومة) الى المقاطع (أي المقاصد والنتائج التي ينقطع فيها المبادى وتكون المبادى مثل المقاطع في وجود احتمال الخطاء
The first principle of theological matters: Refuting the worshippers of Somnath. The author first defined nazar (theoretical reasoning), then mentioned its two divisions: nazar regarding the conceptual means and nazar regarding the assertoric means. Then he defined dalil (proof) and pointed to its divisions, and at the end he mentioned the five forms of conjunctive and disjunctive qiyas (syllogism). Now he begins with 'the first principle of theological matters' and mentions an idea and a view that negates nazar and what branches from it of sciences acquired through thought and nazar, whether conceptual or assertoric; that negates that the intellect acquires sciences; and that denies sciences acquired by the intellect. Thus the proof related to the intellect and its types of syllogisms becomes futile without substance. Issue of nazar yielding knowledge. The Samaniyya denied that nazar yields knowledge absolutely, in whatever thing that nazar occurs, saying (arguing) that no knowledge can be obtained except through sense-perception because the certainty and conviction obtained through nazar may be ignorance not conforming to reality, and that (ignorance) is like knowledge (in being obtained through nazar). So by what (by what proof) is it known that what is obtained after nazar is knowledge? The author mentioned a response to this problem, then at the end a rebuttal to this response. It is answered (firstly) that knowledge obtained through nazar is distinguished from ignorance by its attributes, and the attributes of knowledge are not the attributes of ignorance. (Secondly) because intuition (badahah) judges that what is obtained after correct nazar is knowledge, not ignorance. In this response there is a problem: that by what is it known that that nazar is correct? For the possibility exists from the premises to the conclusions (objectives) equally. (Thirdly) that sense-perception yields only particular knowledge, and particular knowledge is not productive. Rather, the truth is to deny the similarity (between knowledge and ignorance) as is our school, so reflect. The sages of India who used to worship the idol called Somnath, which Mahmud Ghaznavi broke during his reign and conquered India, and they used to say: 'No knowledge except through sense-perception.' This statement refers to the major premise whose minor premise is omitted, namely: 'nazar is among the intelligibles, and nothing among the intelligibles yields knowledge, therefore nothing of nazar yields knowledge.' The minor premise is apparent because nazar is the arrangement of intelligibles to acquire the unknown. The author established the major premise by his statement: 'Because the conviction obtained through nazar may be ignorance not conforming to reality,' meaning: we concede that intelligible matters yield conviction about something, but that conviction may also be ignorance, so how do we know that what is obtained through nazar is knowledge and not ignorance? For both, i.e., knowledge and ignorance, are obtained through nazar. The gist of the response is that knowledge obtained through nazar is distinguished from ignorance by attributes. For the attributes of knowledge—such as disclosure and reaching the distinction of the known from other things—are not the attributes of ignorance. And through correct nazar we distinguish between the attributes of knowledge and attributes of ignorance. So if the nazar is correct, the attributes of knowledge exist; and if it is corrupt, then the attributes are those of ignorance. Thus the difference between conviction that is knowledge and conviction that is ignorance is established through nazar. Then he rebutted this response and said: 'And in it'—in this response there is a problem: that by what is it known and distinguished whether the nazar from which the conviction resulted is correct or not? For this possibility (possibility of error) exists and is present from the premises (the known matters) to the conclusions (i.e., the objectives and results at which the premises terminate), and the premises are like the conclusions in the existence of the possibility of error.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a theological dispute concerning the reliability of theoretical reasoning (nazar). The author of the original matn, Musallam al-Thubut, defines nazar and its types, then addresses the argument of the Samaniyya (a group from India who worshipped idols and denied knowledge beyond sense-perception). They argue that since nazar can produce both correct knowledge and error (ignorance), we cannot be certain that what we obtain from it is true knowledge. The commentator, in 'Izalat al-Rahbut', expands on this by first presenting the counter-argument: knowledge is distinguished from ignorance by its attributes (such as clarity and distinctness of the known), and intuition (badahah) tells us that correct nazar yields knowledge. However, a rebuttal is then offered: how do we know that a given instance of nazar is correct? The possibility of error persists from the premises to the conclusions. The passage concludes by noting that sense-perception only gives particular knowledge, not universal, and that the true position is to deny any similarity between knowledge and ignorance — a point that aligns with the Hanafi approach of affirming the validity of intellectual reasoning. This discussion is foundational for usul al-fiqh because it establishes the epistemological basis for deriving rulings through rational methods like qiyas.

Key Terms
النظر (an-nazar): Theoretical reasoning: the arrangement of intelligibles (ma'qulat) to acquire the unknown.
العلم (al-ilm): Certain knowledge that conforms to reality and is distinguished from ignorance by attributes like clarity and distinctness.
الجهل (al-jahl): Ignorance: conviction not conforming to reality; may share outward similarity with knowledge but differs in essential attributes.
السمنية (as-Samaniyya): A group from India who worshipped idols (Somnath) and denied any knowledge beyond sense-perception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary issue in the dispute between the Hanafis and the Samaniyya?
A) Whether sense-perception yields knowledge at all.
B) Whether theoretical reasoning (nazar) can produce certain knowledge or only ignorance.
C) Whether Mahmud Ghaznavi broke the Somnath idol.
D) Whether the syllogism has two premises.

Key Takeaway
Correct nazar produces knowledge, and the Hanafi school upholds the validity of rational deduction as a source of certain knowledge, provided it follows correct methods and is distinguished from error by clear attributes.

Four Theological Schools on How Reasoning Yields Knowledge
Classification of four theological views on how reasoning yields knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four theological positions on how nazar produces knowledge and explain their relevance to the epistemological status of qiyas in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The modality of knowledge from reasoning determines the certitude of inferential evidence in fiqh.

This passage from a commentary on Muslim al-Thubut classifies four prominent views on how reasoning (nazar) leads to knowledge. While these views are theological, they directly impact usul al-fiqh: they shape how mujtahids understand the reliability of qiyas and istidlal. The Hanafi school, while leaning towards the Ash'ari 'ada view with its emphasis on kasb, also finds merit in al-Razi's subsequent necessity. Understanding this ikhtilaf helps you appreciate the epistemological foundation of Hanafi legal inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four schools and their mechanisms.
List the four groups: Ash'ari ('ada), Mu'tazila (tawlid), Philosophers (i'dad), al-Razi (wujub 'aqib).
Note that the Ash'ari view is elaborated further.
2
Define the key Arabic terms.
Define 'ada (habit), tawlid (generation), i'dad (preparation), and wujub 'aqib (subsequent necessity).
3
Analyze the causal structure of each view.
Compare who or what is the effective cause of knowledge: God alone ('ada), human act (tawlid), Active Intellect (i'dad), or logical necessity (luzum).
Note that al-Razi's view avoids both tawlid and divine obligation.
4
Identify the author's tarjih and its rationale.
The author says al-Razi's choice is 'ashab (more correct), using the example of accident requiring substance as evidence of necessary concomitance.
This suggests a preference for a moderate position that grants some logical necessity without implying human causation.
5
Connect to Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
Consider how each view affects the certainty attributed to qiyas. Hanafis, following Maturidi theology, accept the Ash'ari 'ada but emphasize kasb (acquisition).
The practical result: qiyas yields zanni (probable) knowledge, which is sufficient for legal obligation but not for matters of creed.

ثم ذكر المصنف في المبدأ الثاني من المبادى الكلامية أن في كيفية افادة النظر تحصيل المجهول والعلم به وفي افادة الدليل المدلول وفي افادة القياس والاستقراء والتمثيل النتيجة أربعة مذاهب : مذهب الامام أبي الحسن الأشعرى ومذهب المعتزلة، ومذهب الحكماء، ومذهب الامام الرازي . المذاهب فى إفادة الدليل العلم -١ قال الأشعري إن الإفادة إفادة النظر والدليل والقياس وكل ما يدل على شيئ النتيجة أو المدلو) بالعادة أي عادة الله جارية بإلقاء النتيجة أوالمدلول في القلب عند النظر والاستدلال والقياس إذ لا مؤثر الا الله تعالى بلا وجوب منه ولا عليه ٢- وقالت المعتزلة : انه (الافادة) بالتوليد كحركة المفتاح بحركة اليد -٣- وقالت الحكماء إنه بطريق الإعداد، فانه يُعد الذهن إعداداً تاماً تفيض عليه النتيجة من مبدأ الفيض وجوباً منه ٤- واختار الإمام الرازي أنه واجب عقبه (عقب النظر أو الاستدلال) و إن لم يكن واجباً منه تعالى ابتداءً، غير متولد منه ؛ لأنه ليس لقدرة العبد تأثير، وهذا اختيار الرازى أشبه (بالصواب) فانّ لزوم بعض الأشياء للبعض مما لا ينكر ألا ترى أن وجود العرض بدون الجوهر، والكلية بدون الأعظمية غير معقول هذا خذ هذا واعلم أنّ أهل البصيرة والخبرة من الناس اتفقوا على أن الفكر والنظر يفيد العلم إما القطعى، وإما الظنى) على حسب الفكر والنظر، وأن الصحف والكتب السماوية أمر الناس بهما، والعلوم المتعلقة بالغيب مبنية على الفكر والنظر، بيد أنهم اختلفوا في كيفية افادة النظر العلم. ۱ - فقال أبو الحسن الأشعري: إنّ افادة النظر والدليل العلم بالشيئ بالعادة (من العود مرة بعد أخرى فإن عادة الله تعالى جارية بالقاء العلم بالمدلول بعد العلم بمقدمات الدليل، وبالقاء العلم بالنتيجة بعد العلم بمقدمات القياس، فترتب العلم بعد العلم بالدليل ومقدماته، كترتب الألم على الضرب وترتب الشبع على الأكل ، وليس للنظر والدليل في حد ذاتهما أي تأثير في حصول العلم أو النتيجة ، فإنه لا مؤثر في حصول العلم بعد استعمال ذرائعه وأسبابه الا الله تعالى بلا وجوب منه تعالى على العباد، ومن غير وجوب من العباد عليه تعالى إذا الوجوب عليه تعالى مناف لاختياره
Then the author mentioned in the second introductory principle of the theological introductions: Regarding how reasoning (nazar) yields the acquisition of the unknown and knowledge of it, and regarding how the evidence (dalil) yields the indicated (madlul), and regarding how analogy (qiyas), induction (istiqra), and example (tamthil) yield the result (natija), there are four schools: The school of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the school of the Mu'tazila, the school of the philosophers, and the school of Imam al-Razi. The schools regarding how evidence yields knowledge: 1) Al-Ash'ari said: The yielding is the yielding of reasoning, evidence, analogy, and everything that indicates something (the result or the indicated) by habit ('ada), i.e., God's habit runs by casting the result or the indicated into the heart at the time of reasoning, inference, and analogy, for there is no effect except God, without obligation from Him or upon Him. 2) The Mu'tazila said: It (the yielding) is by generation (tawlid), like the movement of a key by the movement of a hand. 3) The philosophers said: It is by way of preparation (i'dad). For it prepares the mind a complete preparation, then the result overflows upon it from the source of emanation necessarily from Him. 4) Imam al-Razi chose that it is necessary subsequent (to the reasoning or inference), even if it is not necessary from Him initially, nor generated from it; because the servant's power has no effect. And this choice of al-Razi is more likely (correct). For the necessity of some things for others is undeniable. Do you not see that the existence of an accident without a substance, and totality without greatness [؟] are inconceivable? Take this. And know that people of insight and experience agreed that thought and reasoning yield knowledge – either certain or probable – according to the thought and reasoning, and that the heavenly scrolls and books commanded people to do both, and the sciences related to the unseen are based on thought and reasoning. However, they differed regarding how reasoning yields knowledge. 1) Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: The yielding of reasoning and evidence to knowledge of something is by habit (from repetition). For God's habit runs by casting knowledge of the indicated after knowledge of the premises of the evidence, and by casting knowledge of the result after knowledge of the premises of the analogy. So knowledge is arranged after knowledge of the evidence and its premises, like the arrangement of pain after hitting and the arrangement of satiety after eating. And reasoning and evidence in themselves have no effect on the attainment of knowledge or the result. For there is no effect on the attainment of knowledge after using its means and causes except God, without obligation from Him upon the servants, and without obligation of the servants upon Him, because obligation upon Him is contrary to His choice.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents four theological schools on how reasoning (nazar) yields knowledge of the unknown or the conclusion. The author is summarizing the positions as part of the second theological introduction in the original text. (1) Al-Ash'ari: knowledge comes 'by habit' (al-'ada) – God habitually creates knowledge in the heart upon proper reasoning, without any causal efficacy of the reasoning itself. (2) Mu'tazila: the reasoning generates the knowledge necessarily, analogous to a hand generating movement in a key (tawlid). (3) Philosophers (hukama): reasoning prepares the mind (i'dad), and then knowledge emanates from the Active Intellect necessarily. (4) Imam al-Razi: knowledge is logically necessary subsequent to reasoning (wajib 'aqibahu), but this necessity is not due to generation or divine obligation; it is a necessary concomitance (luzum). The author notes that al-Razi's view is closest to correctness. After listing these schools, the author adds that all people of insight agree that thought and reasoning yield knowledge (certain or probable) and that the heavenly scriptures command their use. The difference is only in the modality of how reasoning produces knowledge. The Ash'ari position is explained in more detail, emphasizing that God alone is the effectual cause (mu'aththir) and that there is no obligation upon God.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'ada): God's habitual way of creating knowledge in the heart without causal necessity.
التوليد (al-tawlid): Generation; the idea that an act necessarily produces its effect without direct divine intervention.
الإعداد (al-i'dad): Preparation; the mind's readiness for emanation of knowledge from the Active Intellect.
الوجوب العقبي (al-wujub al-'aqibi): Subsequent necessity; the view that knowledge follows reasoning as a logical necessary consequence, without being generated by it or requiring divine intervention beyond the first cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which school holds that reasoning prepares the mind for knowledge to flow from an external source?
A) Ash'ari
B) Mu'tazila
C) Philosophers
D) al-Razi

Key Takeaway
The four theological positions on nazar's knowledge production are 'ada, tawlid, i'dad, and wujub 'aqib; the author favors al-Razi, and this ikhtilaf impacts the certitude of qiyas in usul al-fiqh.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced

Four Schools on How Reasoning Yields Knowledge
Exposition of four schools on causality of reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four positions on how reasoning produces knowledge and evaluate their implications for the certainty of ijtihad.

Core Idea
Four schools disagree on how reasoning yields knowledge—habit, generation, emanation, or rational necessity—affecting the certainty of ijtihad.

This lesson examines the classical dispute in usul al-fiqh on the causality of reasoning (nazar). While all schools agree that nazar and dalil produce knowledge, they profoundly disagree on the mechanism—whether by divine habit, generation, emanation, or rational necessity. Understanding this debate is crucial for evaluating the epistemological weight of ijtihad and istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common ground
All schools agree that reasoning (nazar) and evidence (dalil) yield knowledge, either certain (qat'i) or probable (zanni), and that the heavenly scriptures command reasoning. Recognise this consensus before exploring differences.
The agreement is on the fact of knowledge acquisition, not the mechanism.
2
Map the four positions
Learn each school's explanation: Ash'ari says by God's habit ('adah); Mu'tazila says by generation (tawlid); philosophers say by emanation (fayd) upon a prepared mind; al-Razi says by rational necessity (wujub 'aqli) after reasoning.
Al-Razi's view is presented as ‘closer to correctness’ by the author.
3
Understand key terminology
Grasp the technical terms: 'adah (habit), tawlid (generation), i'dad (preparation), wujub 'aqli (rational necessity). Each term encapsulates the causal role of reasoning in each theory.
These terms are central to the dispute and appear in usul works.
4
Note the Hanafi/Maturidi alignment
The passage does not directly state the Hanafi position, but Hanafi usul (following Maturidi theology) aligns with the Ash'ari habit view: God creates knowledge after reasoning without necessary causation.
This alignment means Hanafis accept the probabilistic nature of rational deduction unless supported by qat'i text.
5
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if reasoning generates knowledge necessarily (Mu'tazila), then qiyas yields certainty; if by habit (Ash'ari/Hanafi), it yields only probability. This affects the strength of rulings derived by ijtihad.
In Hanafi usul, qiyas is generally zanni, consistent with the habit view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المذاهب فى إفادة الدليل العلم

۲ - وقال أصحاب الاعتزال : إنما يكون حصول العلم من المقدمات أو النظر بالتوليد والتوليد : هو أن يصدر من الفاعل فعل اختياري حصل منه أمر غير اختیاری، كصدور حركة المفتاح من حركة اليد

ولما وجد في المادة (الذهن) الاستعداد التام وجد فيضان العلم من جانب المبدأ الفياض، وإلّا فيلزم البخل، أو عدم العلم باستعداد الذهن، وكلاهما لا يليقان بشأن الواجب تعالى، والوجوب هنا بمعنى الضرورة العقلية لأنه لو كان شيئ واجباً على الله تعالى ليسئل عنه، ولكن لا يُسئل عنما يفعل ) .
واختلاف الأنظار والمدارك والعلوم بين الناس إنما جاء من اختلاف الأذهان
واستعداد تهم . وقال الشاعر الفارسي :
نقصان ز قابل است وگرنه على الدوام
فيض سعادتش به همه کس برابر است
واختار الامام الرازى وجوب حصول العلم بعد النظر بعادة الله تعالى القاء العلم عقب النظر، وليس لإعداد النظر دخل فيه، وإن لم يكن فيضان العلم واجباً من جانبه تعالى ابتداءً قبل النظر) ولا هو ((العلم) متولد من النظر بعد
The schools concerning the benefit of evidence for knowledge. 1- Al-Ash'ari said that the benefit (of reasoning, evidence, and analogy, and everything that indicates a thing) is the result or the indicated [thing] by habit, meaning God's habit is ongoing by casting the result or the indicated [thing] into the heart upon reasoning, inference, and analogy, for there is no effect except God Almighty, without obligation from Him nor upon Him. 2- The Mu'tazila said that it (the benefit) is by generation, like the movement of a key by the movement of a hand. 3- The philosophers said that it is by way of preparation, for it prepares the mind completely, then the result overflows upon it from the source of emanation, necessarily from it. 4- Imam al-Razi chose that it is necessary after it (after reasoning or inference), even if it is not necessary from Him Almighty initially, nor generated from it; because the slave's power has no effect. And this choice of al-Razi is closer to correctness, for the necessity of some things for others is undeniable. Do you not see that the existence of an accident without a substance, and a whole without its greatest part are inconceivable. Take this. Know that the people of insight and experience among mankind have agreed that thought and reasoning yield knowledge, either certain or conjectural, according to the thought and reasoning, and that the heavenly scriptures commanded people to do so, and the sciences related to the unseen are based on thought and reasoning. However, they differed on how reasoning yields knowledge. 1- Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: that reasoning and evidence yield knowledge of a thing by habit (from returning again and again): for God's habit is ongoing by casting knowledge of the indicated [thing] after knowledge of the premises of the evidence, and by casting knowledge of the result after knowledge of the premises of the analogy. So knowledge follows after knowledge of the evidence and its premises, like the succession of pain after striking and satiety after eating. Reasoning and evidence in themselves have no effect in the acquisition of knowledge or result, for there is no effect in acquiring knowledge after using its means and causes except God Almighty, without any obligation from Him upon the servants, and without any obligation from the servants upon Him, for obligation upon Him contradicts His perfect choice; He does what He wills and chooses, and He does whatever He intends. 2- The people of i'tizal said: the acquisition of knowledge from premises or reasoning is by generation. Generation is that from an agent an optional act occurs from which a non-optional matter arises, like the occurrence of the key's movement from the hand's movement. That is, knowledge is generated and occurs from the premises and evidence after knowing them and by that evidence. They analogized the acquisition of knowledge after knowledge of the evidence to the key's movement occurring after the hand's movement. Choice is only in the hand's movement, not the key's movement. Similarly, the acquisition of knowledge after using premises and evidences is optional, but the acquisition of knowledge or result is non-optional. 3- As for the philosophers, they said: the acquisition of knowledge after the mind's reasoning and knowledge of premises or evidence is rationally necessary. For the emanation of knowledge of the result or the indicated [thing] depends on material preparedness, which is the mind's preparedness, then its preparation and readiness to receive knowledge or result from the overflowing source—which is God Almighty according to Muslims and people of revealed laws, or the active intellect according to the philosophers. When complete preparedness is found in the matter (the mind), the emanation of knowledge from the overflowing source occurs; otherwise, it would entail stinginess or ignorance of the mind's preparedness, both unbefitting the status of the Necessary Being. Necessity here means rational necessity, because if something were obligatory upon God Almighty, He would be questioned, but 'He is not questioned about what He does.' 4- The variation in views, perceptions, and sciences among people arises only from differences in minds and their preparedness. The Persian poet said: 'The deficiency comes from the recipient; otherwise, the emanation of His grace to everyone is always equal.' Imam al-Razi chose the necessity of the acquisition of knowledge after reasoning by God's habit of casting knowledge after reasoning, and the preparation of reasoning has no part in it, even if the emanation of knowledge is not obligatory from His side initially before reasoning, nor is it generated from reasoning afterwards.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the classical dispute in Islamic theology and usul al-fiqh on how reasoning (nazar) and evidence (dalil) produce knowledge ('ilm). The author, commenting on Musallam al-Thubut, enumerates four positions. First, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari holds that the production of knowledge is by divine 'adah (habit): God ordinarily creates knowledge in the heart after the act of reasoning, without any causal efficacy in the reasoning itself. There is no necessary connection; it is purely God's custom. Second, the Mu'tazila hold that knowledge is generated (tawlid) from reasoning: just as the hand's movement generates the key's movement, so reasoning generates knowledge. Third, the philosophers (hukama) hold that knowledge overflows (fayd) from the Active Intellect (or God) onto a prepared mind; the mind's preparedness is necessary for the emanation. Fourth, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi chose a middle position: knowledge is necessary after reasoning, but not generated by it nor obligatory upon God initially. The author seems to favor al-Razi's view, calling it 'closer to correctness'. The passage ends with a note that the diversity of understandings stems from differences in mental preparedness.

Key Terms
الفكر (al-fikr): Thought; the mental process of reasoning.
النظر (al-nazar): Reasoning; reflection on premises to reach a conclusion.
الدليل (al-dalil): Evidence; that which indicates something else (the madlul).
العادة (al-'adah): Divine habit; God's consistent practice of creating knowledge after reasoning, without necessary causation.
التوليد (al-tawlid): Generation; the result (knowledge) arises necessarily from the act of reasoning, analogous to the key's movement from hand movement.
الإعداد (al-i'dad): Preparation; the mind's readiness to receive emanation of knowledge from the active intellect or God.
الوجوب العقلي (al-wujub al-'aqli): Rational necessity; the conclusion necessarily follows the premises by reason, but not as a moral obligation on God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position, how does reasoning produce knowledge?
A) By generating it necessarily from the premises.
B) By God's habit of creating knowledge after reasoning.
C) By the mind's preparedness for emanation.
D) By rational necessity independent of God's action.

Key Takeaway
The mechanism of reasoning's production of knowledge is disputed among schools, but all agree that nazar and dalil yield knowledge, affecting the certainty of ijtihad.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Attaining Knowledge from Reasoning: Three Views and the Hanafi Stance
Comparing three epistemological theories on knowledge from reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three positions on attaining knowledge from reasoning and explain why the Hanafi school prefers the view of divine custom (adah).

Core Idea
Knowledge after reasoning is a divine gift by custom, not generated by man nor intellectually necessary.

This lesson examines a classical usul dispute on epistemology: How does reasoning (nazar) lead to knowledge? The Mu'tazila argue for generation (tawlid), the philosophers for intellectual necessity, while Imam al-Razi and the Hanafi tradition affirm divine custom (adah). Understanding this debate clarifies the role of human effort and divine omnipotence in acquiring knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
Isolate the Mu'tazila, philosopher, and al-Razi views on how knowledge is attained after reasoning. Note the key terminology: tawlid, intellectual necessity, and adah (custom).
2
Analyze the Mu'tazila argument
Examine their analogy of the key and hand: voluntary act (hand) produces involuntary effect (key movement). They claim reasoning produces knowledge similarly. Identify the flaw the author points out: knowledge is not a material effect.
3
Analyze the philosopher argument
Understand their stance: knowledge emanates necessarily from the Emanating Principle when the intellect is prepared. Note their reasoning: otherwise God would be miserly or ignorant. Identify the Hanafi criticism: this imposes necessity on God.
4
Understand al-Razi's view and its support
Al-Razi holds that knowledge occurs by God's custom (adah) after reasoning. He supports this with examples of natural inseparability (pain–hit, satiety–eat) and the Quranic verse that God is not questioned. See how the author endorses this as 'more similar to correctness'.
5
Connect to Hanafi usul
Recognize that the Hanafi school adopts al-Razi's view because it preserves divine omnipotence while accounting for human effort (kasb). The servant's reasoning is a condition (shart), not a cause.

The complete; for He does what He wills and chooses, and He is effective in what He intends. 2 - The people of Mu'tazila said: Knowledge is only attained from premises or reasoning by generation (tawlid). And tawlid is: that an agent performs a voluntary act from which an involuntary matter results, like the movement of a key resulting from the movement of the hand. That is, knowledge is generated and occurs from premises and evidence after knowledge of them, and by that evidence. They analogized the attainment of knowledge after knowledge of the evidence to the movement of the key occurring after the movement of the hand. Choice lies only in the movement of the hand, not the movement of the key. Likewise, the attainment of knowledge after using premises and evidences is voluntary, but the attainment of knowledge or the result is involuntary. 3 - As for the philosophers, they said: The attainment of knowledge after mental contemplation and knowledge of premises or evidence is intellectually necessary. For the emanation of knowledge of the result or the thing indicated depends on material preparation, which is the preparedness of the intellect, then its preparation and readiness to receive knowledge or the result from the Emanating Principle (al-Mabda al-Fayyad) — which is Allah Almighty according to Muslims and adherents of revealed laws, and the Active Intellect according to the philosophers. When complete preparedness exists in the matter (the intellect), the emanation of knowledge occurs from the Emanating Principle; otherwise, miserliness or ignorance of the intellect's preparedness would follow, both unbefitting the Necessary Existent. Necessity here means intellectual necessity, because if something were obligatory upon Allah, He would be questioned about it, but 'He is not questioned about what He does' (Quran 21:23). Differences in views, perceptions, and sciences among people arise only from differences in intellects and their preparedness. The Persian poet said: 'The defect is in the recipient; otherwise, the emanation of His grace is always equal for everyone.' Imam al-Razi chose the necessity of the attainment of knowledge after reasoning by the custom (adah) of Allah Almighty casting knowledge subsequent to reasoning, and the preparation of reasoning has no role in it, even though the emanation of knowledge is not obligatory from His side initially before reasoning. Nor is knowledge generated from reasoning after preparation of the intellect, because the servant's power has no effect on the emanation and casting of knowledge after reasoning; rather it is by His casting and inspiration upon acquisition (kasb) through reasoning and arranging knowns to obtain unknowns. This necessity is a metaphor for not wasting the servant's effort and acquisition. Al-Razi's saying (his choice) is more correct because he affirms the inseparability of knowledge with reasoning. For the inseparability of some things from others (like the inseparability of pain from hitting, satiety from eating, grief from calamity) is undeniable. Do you not see that the existence of an accident without a substance supporting it, and the existence of a whole without it being greater than its part, is not accepted by the intellect? Thus, the existence of reasoning without knowledge following it is unintelligible. This inseparability or customary necessity does not contradict the existence of all things from Him, by His grace and choice. He indicated by his saying 'take this to the correctness of al-Razi's saying' as confirmation of his saying: 'And this is more similar.'
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on how knowledge (ilm) is attained from premises and reasoning (nazar). The author presents three views. First, the Mu'tazila hold that knowledge is generated (tawlid) by the reasoning act: the servant chooses to reason, and knowledge necessarily follows involuntarily, like a key's movement following the hand's movement. Second, the philosophers (hukama) hold that knowledge is intellectually necessary after mental preparation, emanating from the Divine (or Active Intellect) when the intellect is fully prepared; they argue that otherwise it would imply miserliness or ignorance in God. Third, Imam al-Razi selects the view that knowledge after reasoning occurs by God's custom (adah) of casting knowledge after reasoning, not by generation nor intellectual necessity; rather it is a divine gift upon the servant's acquisition (kasb). The author endorses al-Razi's view, noting that inseparability of knowledge from reasoning is like other natural inseparabilities (e.g., pain from hitting), and this customary necessity does not contradict God's omnipotence. This aligns with the Hanafi theological stance that human action and its effect are created by God, and the servant's role is acquisition.

Key Terms
توليد (tawlid): The doctrine that knowledge is generated from the servant's reasoning act involuntarily, analogous to physical effects.
عادة (adah): God's established custom of casting knowledge after reasoning, not by necessity or generation.
نظر (nazar): Intellectual contemplation or reasoning leading to knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which view is more correct regarding the attainment of knowledge after reasoning?
A) The Mu'tazila view of tawlid (generation).
B) The philosophers' view of intellectual necessity.
C) Al-Razi's view of divine custom (adah).
D) The view that knowledge is attained without any reasoning.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, knowledge after reasoning is attained by God's custom (adah), not by human generation (tawlid) nor intellectual necessity.

The Nature of Good and Evil: Rational or Revealed?
Analyzes three schools' positions on rational vs. revealed good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Maturidi, Ash'ari, and Mu'tazili positions on ḥusn and qubḥ, and explain the practical consequence for taklīf before revelation.

Core Idea
Good and evil are rationally knowable, but only revelation imposes obligation – the Maturidi middle ground.

This passage delves into a foundational usul al-fiqh debate: are moral values intrinsic to actions (rational) or merely divine decrees (revealed)? The Maturidi school, followed by the Hanafis, offers a nuanced position: reason can discern good and evil, but this discernment does not create a binding legal obligation until the divine message arrives. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi theory of taklīf (accountability).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Positions
List the Ash'ari, Maturidi, and Mu'tazili views on the source of ḥusn and qubḥ. Note which groups share the premise that good/evil are rational and which do not.
Ash'ari: purely revealed (sharʿī). Maturidi and Mu'tazila: rational (ʿaqlī).
2
Pinpoint the Shared Premise between Maturidis and Mu'tazila
Both hold that good and evil can be known independently of revelation. But the passage indicates a critical difference: does rational knowledge alone impose a ruling (ḥukm) on the servant?
The difference is over legal obligation, not epistemology.
3
Analyze the Maturidi Rebuttal of the Mu'tazila
The Maturidis argue that rational good/evil only create deservability (istiḥqāq) of reward or punishment, not actual obligation. Obligation requires a divine command, which only comes after revelation (balāgh al-daʿwa). Understand the argument that 'if reason alone obligated, then there would be no need for messengers.'
This is the core of the dispute.
4
Examine the Mu'tazili Classification
The passage outlines three categories of good/evil according to the Mu'tazila: necessary (ḍarūrī), theoretical (naẓarī), and purely revelational (sharʿī). Note how even they admit some moral knowledge depends on revelation, which weakens their claim of purely rational obligation.
This classification is used to refine their position.
5
Trace the Thamarah (Practical Consequence)
Apply the dispute to a concrete scenario: a person living before any prophetic message. According to Maturidis, they are not obligated by reason alone; according to Mu'tazila, they are. This affects rulings on the accountability of non-Muslims who never heard Islam.
The Hanafi fatwa position follows the Maturidi view.

He (the author) pointed to the three meanings by his statement: 'And there is no dispute,' and to specifying the disputed meaning by his statement: 'Rather, the dispute is only now.' And he pointed to the difference among the three groups by his statement: 'According to the Ash'aris, it is revealed, i.e., He (God) makes it so. So whatever He commands is good, and whatever He forbids is evil, and if the command were reversed, the matter would be reversed. And according to us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazila, it is rational, i.e., their knowledge does not depend on revelation.' According to the Ash'aris, it is revealed, i.e., only by His making it so. That is, goodness in the sense of deserving praise and reward is only by the Maker's making it so. Likewise, evil in the sense of deserving blame and punishment is revealed by the Maker's making it so. He said in explaining the Maker's making: 'So whatever He commands is good, and whatever He forbids is evil, and if the command were reversed by abrogating the command of what was commanded, and commanding what was forbidden by abrogation, then the matter would be reversed (so that the previously commanded thing becomes evil, and the previously forbidden thing becomes good).' (2) And according to us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazila, the goodness of the good is rational, and likewise the evil of the evil, i.e., the perception of good and evil does not depend on revelation (as the Ash'aris say, rather good and evil are perceived even before the advent of revelation). Explanation of the difference between us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazila after agreeing that they are rational: But according to us, it does not entail a ruling upon the servant, rather it becomes a cause for deserving the ruling from the Wise (God) who does not prefer the inferior. So as long as He has not ruled, there is no ruling. Hence we stipulated that the call (of revelation) reach (the person) for obligation. But according to us, rational good or evil does not entail a ruling upon the servant (before revelation), rather good or evil becomes a cause for deserving the ruling and deserving its necessity by command or prohibition from the Wise (God) who does not prefer the inferior (and does not rule by it). So as long as the Wise (God) has not ruled (after perceiving good or evil rationally), there is no ruling (obligatory by reason alone). And from here (due to the absence of ruling by reason alone) we stipulated that the call (of revelation) reach (the person) for obligation (through a messenger or his deputy to rational legally responsible persons for their responsibility and the necessity of the ruling upon them). The doctrine of the Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Brahmins regarding the obligation of responsibility by reason alone: Contrary to the Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Brahmins, for according to them, it (rational good/evil) necessitates the ruling. So if there were no Revealer and the acts (were rational), the rulings would be obligatory. Contrary to the Mu'tazila, Imami (Shi'a) Karamiyya, and Brahmins, for according to them, rational good or rational evil necessitates the ruling of action and compliance in rational good, and avoidance and abandoning action in rational evil, even if there were no ruling of the Revealer, His command or prohibition. That is, the servant becomes obligated to comply in rational good and obligated to avoid in rational evil. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'So if there were no Revealer, the Prophet had not come, and the acts were good and evil rationally, then rulings would be obligatory upon the servants rationally by complying with rational commands and avoiding rational prohibitions.' ... 'As for the obligation of those commands or the necessity of avoiding those prohibitions by revelation, no one has said that, because these four groups did not act upon those revealed commands nor avoid those revealed prohibitions, even though they are in accordance with their rational doctrines.' Types of good and evil according to the Mu'tazila: (1) They said: Some of good and evil are necessary, like the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the evil of harmful lying, for they are known without acquisition. (2) And some of each are theoretical, like the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the evil of beneficial lying, since they are not perceived without acquisition and reflection. (3) And some of each of good and evil are not perceived except by revelation, like the goodness of
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul al-fiqh dispute regarding the ontological and epistemological status of good (ḥusn) and evil (qubḥ). The author, a Maturidi scholar, presents the positions of three major schools: the Ash'aris, the Maturidis (the author's school), and the Mu'tazila, along with others like the Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Brahmins. The core issue is whether moral values are intrinsic to actions (rational) or merely determined by divine decree (revealed). The author first explains that there is no dispute that some aspects of good and evil are agreed upon; the dispute is over a specific meaning: whether the qualification of an act as good or evil refers to an intrinsic property or is purely a matter of divine command. According to the Ash'aris, good and evil are entirely revealed (sharʿī): God's command makes an action good, and His prohibition makes it evil. If God were to reverse His commands by abrogation, the moral values would flip accordingly. In contrast, the Maturidis (the author's school) and the Mu'tazila hold that good and evil are rational (ʿaqlī), meaning that the intellect can perceive the goodness or evil of an action independently of revelation. However, the passage highlights a crucial difference between these two groups: although both agree that good and evil are rationally knowable, the Maturidis maintain that rational recognition alone does not impose any obligation (ḥukm) upon a person without the arrival of revelation. Instead, rational good or evil merely makes the action deserving of reward or punishment (istiḥqāq), but the actual obligation (wujūb) or prohibition (tahrīm) comes only from the divine command once the prophetic message reaches the individual. This is why the Maturidis stipulate the condition that the call of Islam (balāgh al-daʿwa) must reach a person before they can be held accountable. The Mu'tazila, on the other hand, hold that rational good and evil by themselves impose obligations: a rational good act must be performed, and a rational evil act must be avoided, even if no revelation has come. This is a fundamental point of divergence. The passage then notes that the Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Brahmins all agree on this rational obligation, while the Maturidis reject it. Finally, the author outlines the Mu'tazila classification of good and evil into three categories: necessary (ḍarūrī) — known instinctively (e.g., beneficial truth is good, harmful lying is evil); theoretical (naẓarī) — requires reflection (e.g., beneficial lying or harmful truth); and purely revelational (sharʿī) — cannot be known without revelation (the text cuts off here). This passage is a detailed exposition of a foundational usul dispute that has significant implications for theories of obligation, the nature of divine justice, and the role of reason in Islamic law.

Key Terms
حسن (ḥusn): Goodness, moral beauty; in this context, the quality of an action that makes it praiseworthy and deserving of reward.
قبح (qubḥ): Evil, moral ugliness; the quality that makes an action blameworthy and deserving of punishment.
عقلي (ʿaqlī): Rational; knowable or determined by the intellect independently of revelation.
شرعي (sharʿī): Revealed; dependent on divine law or revelation for its determination.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): Deservability or worthiness; the state of being worthy of reward or punishment for an action, even if no ruling has been issued.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It imposes a binding obligation to act or avoid.
B) It creates a state of deservability (istiḥqāq) but no actual ruling.
C) It has no effect at all until after judgment day.
D) It makes the person sinful for not following reason.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, good and evil are rationally knowable, but binding legal obligation depends on the arrival of divine revelation.

The Mu'tazila Doctrine of Rational Obligation
Presents Mu'tazila position on rational obligation and Hanafi critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Mu'tazila position on rational obligation, classify the three types of good and evil according to them, and evaluate the Hanafi response using the objection and two responses.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot impose legal obligation; revelation is necessary for accountability.

This lesson examines the Mu'tazila claim that reason alone obligates actions and the Hanafi rejection. The Hanafi school requires revelation (da'wa) for taklif, while the Mu'tazila argue that rational good and evil necessitate action or avoidance even without revelation. The passage also classifies rational good/evil into necessary, theoretical, and purely revelational types, and presents an objection about the afterlife being a revealed matter, followed by two Hanafi responses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core question: Does reason alone impose legal obligation (taklif) without revelation? State the Hanafi position (obligation requires reach of revelation) and the Mu'tazila position (reason alone suffices).
2
Analyze the Mu'tazila typology
The Mu'tazila divide rational good/evil into three types: necessary (e.g., beneficial truthfulness), theoretical (e.g., harmful truthfulness), and purely revelational (e.g., fasting last of Ramadan). Note which are known by reason alone and which require revelation.
The third type shows that even Mu'tazila accept some moral knowledge requires revelation.
3
Follow the objection to the first type
The objection: The afterlife (reward/punishment) is a revealed matter, so reason cannot judge it a priori. This challenges the Mu'tazila claim that necessary good/evil entail reward/punishment by reason alone.
The objection targets the necessary type, which is supposed to be a priori.
4
Understand the Hanafi responses
The author presents two responses. First: Justice is rationally obligatory, so recompense is necessary by reason, even if physical details are revealed. Second: Rational good/evil can be understood conditionally: if resurrection exists, then reward/punishment follow; this conditional necessity is sufficient for reason's judgment.
These responses defend the possibility of a priori rational judgment on reward/punishment.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The key difference: A person who has not received revelation is not accountable according to Hanafis, but is accountable by reason alone according to Mu'tazila. This affects the legal status of people living before or without prophetic messages.
The Hanafi position aligns with the Quranic principle of 'We never punish until We send a messenger' (17:15).

مذهب المعتزلة والإمامية والكرامية والبراهمة في وجوب التكليف بمجرد العقل

بخلاف المعتزلة والإمامية والكرامية والبراهمة؛ فإنه عندهم يوجب الحكم، فلولا الشارع وكانت الأفعال لوجبت الأحكام. بخلاف المعتزلة والشيعة الإمامية والكرامية والبراهمة؛ فإن الحسن العقلي أو القبح العقلي عندهم يوجب الحكم العمل والامتثال في الحسن العقلي والاجتناب وترك العمل في القبيح العقلي، ولولم يكن حكم الشارع أمره أو نهيه، أي يصير العبد مكلفاً بالامتثال في الحسن العقلي ومكلفاً بالاجتناب في القبيح العقلي. وهذا معنى قوله فلولا الشارع لم يأت النبي وكانت الأفعال حسنة وقبيحة عقلا لوجبت الأحكام على العباد عقلا بامتثال الأوامر والاجتناب عن النواهي العقلية... أما وجوب تلك الأوامر أو لزوم الاجتناب عن تلك النواهي شرعا فلم يقل به أحد؛ لأن هؤلاء الفرق الأربع لم يعملوا بتلك الأوامر الشرعية ولم يجتنبوا عن تلك النواهي شرعا، مع كونها وفق عقولهم ومذاهبهم العقلية.

انواع الحسن والقبح عند المعتزلة
(1) قالوا منه من الحسن أو القبح ما هو ضروري كحسن الصدق النافع وقبح الكذب الضار، إذ هما يُعلمان بدون الكسب. (2) ومن كل منهما ما هو نظري كحسن الصدق الضار وقبح الكذب النافع، حيث لا يدركان من غير كسب ونظر. (3) ومن كل من الحسن والقبح ما لا يدرك إلا بالشرع كحسن صوم آخر رمضان وقبح صوم أول شوال، فإنه لا سبيل للعقل إليه إلى كل من حسن الصوم وقبحه لكن الشرع كشف عن الحسن الذاتي في صوم آخر رمضان وعن القبح الذاتي في صوم أول شوال.
(1) والقوانين الوضعية في الدول غير الإسلامية والدول الإسلامية المعاصرة مبينة على الحسن والقيح العقليين، فالحسن ما هو حسن عقلا، والقبيح ما هو قبيح عقلا

الإشكال على النوع الأول وجوابه بوجهين

قيل: أمر الآخرة أمر سمعي لا يستقل العقل بإدراكه، فكيف يحكم في الحسن العقلي بالثواب آجلا؟
أقول: العدل واجب عقلاً عندهم، فتجب المجازاة وذلك كاف لحكم العقل، وإن كان خصوصية المعاد الجسماني للمجازاة سمعياً.
على أنه بمعنى لو تحقق لتحقق كاف لحكم العقل، فتدبر.

أما الإشكال فقوله: قيل في الاعتراض على المعتزلة: أمر الآخرة وهو ترتب الثواب الآجل في الحسن والعقاب الآجل في القبيح أمر سمعي يتوقف على السمع من الشرع، فلا يستقل العقل بإدراكه، فكيف يحكم العقل بالثواب أو العقاب آجلا؟ حتى يتم معرفة العقل بداهة كون فعل حسناً وموجباً للثواب وآخر قبيحا وموجباً للعقاب؟ فثبت أن بدهة بعض الحسن والقبح عقلاً باطل.

أما الجواب بالوجه الأول فقوله: أقول: العدل بين الناس في القضاء والمحاكم واجب عقلاً عندهم عند المعتزلة، فتجب المجازاة بإعطاء الثواب بالفعل الحسن وتسليط العقاب بالقبيح في عالم آخر غير هذا العالم، وذلك كاف لحكم العقل على الثواب والعقاب بداهة، وإن كان خصوصية الحياة الجسمانية المركبة من الروح والجسد سمعياً، أي لا يمكن إثبات الحياة الجسمانية للمجازات بالعقل فقط، بل بإثبات المعاد مطلقاً جسمانياً كان أو روحيانياً.

Here, because of not judging by reason alone, we have stipulated the reach of the call in obligation through the messenger or his deputy to the rational beings obligated according to revelation for their obligation and the necessity of judgment upon them.

The school of the Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Barahima on the necessity of obligation by reason alone.

Contrary to the Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Barahima; for according to them reason necessitates judgment, so if there were no Lawgiver and actions existed, judgments would be necessary. Contrary to the Mu'tazila, the Shi'a Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Barahima; for the rational good or rational evil according to them necessitates judgment: action and compliance in rational good, and avoidance and abandonment of action in rational evil, even if there were no command or prohibition from the Lawgiver. That is, the servant becomes obligated to comply in rational good and obligated to avoid in rational evil. And this is the meaning of his statement: 'If there were no Lawgiver, the Prophet would not have come, and actions would be good and evil by reason, so judgments would be obligatory upon servants by reason: compliance with commands and avoidance of rational prohibitions...' As for the obligation of those commands or the necessity of avoiding those prohibitions by revelation, no one has said that; because these four sects did not act upon those revealed commands nor avoid those revealed prohibitions despite them being in accordance with their reason and their rational doctrines.

Types of good and evil according to the Mu'tazila
(1) They said: among good or evil there is what is necessary, like the goodness of beneficial truthfulness and the evil of harmful lying, as both are known without acquisition. (2) And from each there is what is theoretical, like the goodness of harmful truthfulness and the evil of beneficial lying, as they are not perceived without acquisition and consideration. (3) And from each of good and evil there is what is not perceived except by revelation, like the goodness of fasting the last of Ramadan and the evil of fasting the first of Shawwal, for reason has no way to it—to each of the goodness and evil of fasting, but revelation uncovered the intrinsic goodness in fasting the last of Ramadan and the intrinsic evil in fasting the first of Shawwal.
(1) Positive laws in non-Islamic states and contemporary Islamic states are based on rational good and evil: good is what is rationally good, evil is what is rationally evil.

Objection to the first type and its response in two ways

It was said: The matter of the Hereafter is a revealed matter; reason cannot independently perceive it. So how can it judge regarding rational good for reward in the afterlife?
I say: Justice is rationally obligatory according to them, so recompense becomes obligatory, and that is sufficient for reason's judgment, even if the specificity of physical resurrection for recompense is revealed.
Moreover, it is in the sense that if it were realized, it would be realized—sufficient for reason's judgment; so reflect.

As for the objection, his statement: It was said in objection to the Mu'tazila: The matter of the Hereafter—which is the linking of immediate reward for good and immediate punishment for evil—is a revealed matter dependent on hearing from revelation; reason cannot independently perceive it. So how can reason judge reward or punishment in the afterlife? Even reason's a priori knowledge that an act is good and entails reward and another evil and entails punishment? So it is established that the a priori knowledge of some good and evil by reason is false.

As for the response by the first way, his statement: I say: Justice among people in judgment and courts is rationally obligatory according to them (the Mu'tazila), so recompense becomes obligatory by giving reward for the good act and inflicting punishment for the evil in another world besides this world, and that is sufficient for reason's judgment on reward and punishment a priori, even if the specificity of physical life composed of soul and body is revealed. That is, it is not possible to prove physical life for recompense by reason alone, but rather by proving the resurrection absolutely, whether physical or spiritual.

As for the response by the second way, his statement: Moreover, the goodness or evil of an act by reason is in the sense that if the resurrection were realized, entitlement to reward or punishment would be realized, sufficient for reason's judgment on good and evil necessarily a priori, or on reward and punishment in the afterlife, whether that resurrection is only spiritual or
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul al-fiqh discussion of the Mu'tazila position that reason alone can impose obligation. The author first states the Hanafi condition that revelation (da'wa) must reach the obligated person, rejecting pure rational obligation. He then presents the Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karamiyya, and Barahima schools who hold that rational good and evil necessitate action or avoidance even without revelation. He clarifies that these groups do not reject revealed commands but base their obligation on reason. The passage then details the Mu'tazila classification of good and evil into three types: necessary (e.g., beneficial truthfulness), theoretical (e.g., harmful truthfulness), and purely revelational (e.g., fasting last of Ramadan). A footnote notes that modern positive laws also follow rational good/evil. An objection is raised: the afterlife (reward/punishment) is a revealed matter, so reason cannot judge it a priori. The Hanafi response gives two answers. First, justice is rationally obligatory, so recompense is necessary by reason, even if the physical details are revealed. Second, rational good/evil is understood as conditional on the resurrection—if it exists, reward/punishment follow; this conditional necessity is sufficient for reason's a priori judgment on the moral worth of acts, even if the specific afterlife is known by revelation. The commentary follows the author's order: first the Hanafi position, then the Mu'tazila view, the typology, the objection, and the two responses.

Key Terms
الحسن العقلي (al-hasan al-ʻaqlī): Rational good: an act that reason deems good, entailing praise in this world and reward in the afterlife according to Mu'tazila.
القبح العقلي (al-qubh al-ʻaqlī): Rational evil: an act that reason deems evil, entailing blame and punishment according to Mu'tazila.
التكليف بمجرد العقل (al-taklīf bi-mujarrad al-ʻaql): Obligation by reason alone: the doctrine that rational beings are obligated to follow rational good and avoid rational evil without revelation.

B) Theoretical good/evil (e.g., harmful truthfulness)
C) Purely revelational good/evil (e.g., fasting last of Ramadan)
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul affirms that reason can discern moral qualities, but legal obligation and afterlife rewards depend on revelation reaching the individual.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

8 min
Dispute on Rational Good and Evil: Reward in Afterlife
Refuting objection that reward/punishment is only known by revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Mu'tazili argument that reward and punishment are known by reason, and identify the Hanafi/Ash'ari response distinguishing between bodily and absolute resurrection.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are revealed (sam'i), not purely rational; reason only knows the conditional necessity of requital.

This passage from 'Izalat al-Rahbūt' presents a classical usul al-fiqh debate between the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya (with whom Hanafis align) on whether the third meaning of goodness and evil—deserving divine reward and punishment—is known by reason alone. Using the example of fasting on Eid, the author refutes the Mu'tazili claim that reason can independently determine the retributive consequences of acts. Understanding this dispute is essential for grasping the Hanafi epistemology of ethical-legal values and the primacy of revelation in matters of the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed meaning
Recognize that the debate centers on the third meaning of ḥusn/qubḥ: an action's inherent desert of divine praise/reward or blame/punishment in the afterlife, not its worldly suitability or perfection of the agent.
This third meaning is distinct from the first two (perfection/imperfection and worldly benefit/harm).
2
State the Mu'tazili position and proof
The Mu'tazila argue that justice ('adl) is rationally obligatory, and since God is just, He must requite good with reward and evil with punishment. Therefore, reason alone can know that deeds entail retribution in an afterlife.
They do not claim reason knows the specific form (e.g., bodily resurrection), only the necessity of requital.
3
Understand the Ash'ari objection (ishkāl)
The objector (Ash'ari) argues that matters of the afterlife, such as reward, punishment, Paradise, Hell, etc., are purely revealed (sam'ī). Reason cannot perceive them independently. How then can the Mu'tazila claim that the desert of reward/punishment is known intuitively by reason?
The objection is supported by Quran 2:216, which shows human moral judgments can be mistaken.
4
Analyze the Mu'tazila's two responses
First response: Reason necessitates absolute requital in another abode; this is rational, even if the specific bodily resurrection is revealed. Second response: Even if actual resurrection is unknown, reason can judge conditionally: if there is resurrection, then reward/punishment necessarily follow. This conditional judgment is sufficient.
The first response appeals to rational obligation; the second to logical entailment.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi stance
The Hanafi commentator sides with the Ash'ari view: the specific bodily resurrection and the exact details of reward/punishment are sam'ī. Reason may affirm the possibility of requital but cannot independently establish its actuality. The Quranic verse is the decisive proof that moral knowledge requires revelation.
Thus, in Hanafi usul, the third meaning of ḥusn/qubḥ is primarily known through revelation, though reason plays a subsidiary role.

صوم آخر رمضان وقبح صوم أول شوال، فإنه لاسبيل للعقل إليه (إلى كل من حسن الصوم وقبحه لكن الشرع كشف عن الحسن الذاتي ( في صوم آخر رمضان) وعن القبح الذاتي (في صوم أول شوال) الإشكال على النوع الأول وجوابه بوجهين قيل : أمر الآخرة، أمر سمعي لا يستقل العقل بإدراكه، فكيف يحكم في الحسن العقلى بالثواب آجلا ؟ أقول: العدل واجب عقلاً عندهم، فتجب المجازا وذلك كاف لحكم العقل، وإن كان خصوصية المعاد الجسماني (للمحازا[؟]) سمعياً على أنه بمعنى لو تحقق لتحقق كاف لحكم العقل فتدبر أما الإشكال فقوله : قيل ( في الاعتراض على المعتزلة) : أمر الآخرة، (وهو ترتب الثواب الآجل في الحسن، والعقاب الآجل في القبيح أمر سمعي يتوقف على السمع من الشرع، فلا يستقل العقل بإدراكه، فكيف يحكم العقل بالثواب أو العقاب اجلا؟ حتى يتم معرفة العقل ( بداهة) كون فعل حسناً وموجباً للثواب، وآخر قبيحا وموجبًا للعقاب ؟ فثبت أن بدهة بعض الحسن و القبح عقلاً باطل أما الجواب بالوجه الأول فقوله : أقول : العدل بين الناس في القضاء والمحاكم) واجب عقلاً عندهم ( عند المعتزلة) فتجب المجازاة بإعطاء الثواب بالفعل الحسن ويتسليط العقاب بالقبيح في عالم آخر غير هذا العالم وذلك كاف لحكم العقل على الثواب والعقاب بداهة وإن كان خصوصية الحياة الجسمانية المركبة من الروح والجسد) سمعياً، أي لا يكمن إثبات الحياة الجسمانية للمجازات بالعقل فقط - بل بإثبات المعاد مطلقاً جسمانياً كان أو روحيانياً. وأما الجواب بالوجه الثاني، فقوله : على أنه ( حسن الفعل أو قبحه عقلا) بمعنى لو تحقق المعاد لتحقق استحقاق الثواب أو العقاب كاف لحكم العقل بالحسن والقبح ضرورة (بداهة) أو بالثواب والعقاب آجلاً سواء كان ذلك المعاد روحانيا فقط أو روحانيا وجسمانيا، فالمطلوب هو مطلق المعاد ( عند المعتزلة) خلاصة الإشكال : أن المعنى المتنازع فيه بيننا وبين الأشاعرة هو المعنى الثالث "استحقاق مدحه تعالى وثوابه في الحسن، واستحقاق ذمّه تعالى وعقابه في القبح" وهما استحقاق المدح والثواب واستحقاق الذم والعقاب من الأمور الأخروية، كحقية النار، والصراط ووزن الأعمال والجنة ونعيمها فهي ليست من مدركات العقل، فكيف يكون الحسن الموجب لاستحقاق المدح والثواب عقليا؟ وكذلك القبح الموجب للعقاب، كيف يكون عقليا؟ وكيف يكون الشيئ حسناً موجباً للثواب) أو (يكون قبيحاً موجباً للعقاب) بمجرد العقل؟ وقال تعالى : ( عسى أن تكرهوا شيئا وهو خير لكم وعسى أن تحبوا شيئا وهو شر لكم والله يعلم وأنتم لا تعلمون ) وحاصل الجواب : أن الأمور الأخروية لها معنيان : أحدهما ما يتعلق بخصوص المعاد الجسماني، أي يتصور المعاد الجسماني و يثبت له لوازمه، وثانيهما ما يتعلق بمطلق المعاد ودار الجزاء جسمانيا كان أو غيره، فإن كان مراد المعترض المعنى الأول فمسلم أنه سمعي، ولكن لا يضرنا، لأنا لانريد من الأمور الأخروية المعنى الأول، وإن كان مراده المعنى الثاني فلا نسلم أنه سمعي، بل هو عقلي لأن جزاء الأعمال واجب عقلاً عند المعتزلة، فإذا عمل أحد عملاً صالحا حسنا وجب أجره على الله عندهم فإذا لم يثبه الله في الدنيا وجب أن يعطيه أجره في دار الجزاء ضرورة بعد وفاته، فثبت مطلق المعاد، ودار الجزاء من غير لحاظ كون معاده جسمانيا أو روحانيا، هذا حاصل الجواب الأول . وحاصل الجواب الثاني : أن المعنى الثاني للحسن أو القبح، (المتنازع فيه) لا يترتب عليه الثواب أو العقاب آجلا ،بالفعل بل تقديراً، أي على تقدير وجود المعاد المطلق يوجد الثواب أو العقاب، فبهذا المعنى يكون الحسن و القبح عقليين، فإن العقل يحكم بالثواب أو العقاب على تقدير وجود المعاد، وإن لم يوجد بالفعل فهذه ملازمة عقلية فقط.
Fasting at the end of Ramadan and the ugliness of fasting at the beginning of Shawwal, for reason has no way to it (to each of the goodness of fasting and its ugliness) but the Shariah revealed the intrinsic goodness (in fasting at the end of Ramadan) and the intrinsic ugliness (in fasting at the beginning of Shawwal). The objection to the first type and its answer in two ways: It was said: The matter of the Hereafter is a revealed matter; reason cannot independently perceive it. So how can it judge in rational goodness regarding reward in the future? I say: Justice is obligatory by reason according to them, so requital is obligatory, and that is sufficient for reason's judgment, even though the specificity of bodily resurrection (for requital) is revealed. Moreover, it is in the sense that if it were realized, it would be realized; sufficient for reason's judgment, so ponder. As for the objection: His statement: 'It was said' (in objection to the Mu'tazila): The matter of the Hereafter (i.e., the accrual of future reward for good and future punishment for evil is a revealed matter dependent on revelation from the Shariah; reason cannot independently perceive it. So how can reason judge reward or punishment in the future? So that reason's (intuitive) knowledge that an act is good and necessitates reward, or evil and necessitates punishment, is established as false. So it is established that the intuitive nature of some goodness and evil by reason is false. As for the answer by the first way: His statement: 'I say: Justice among people (in judgment and courts) is obligatory by reason according to them (the Mu'tazila), so requital by giving reward for the good act and inflicting punishment for the evil deed in another world besides this world is obligatory, and that is sufficient for reason's judgment regarding reward and punishment intuitively, even though the specificity of bodily life (composed of spirit and body) is revealed, i.e., it is not possible to prove bodily life for requital by reason alone—rather by proving resurrection absolutely, whether bodily or spiritual.' As for the answer by the second way: His statement: 'Moreover, (that the goodness or evil of an act is rational) in the sense that if resurrection were realized, the deserving of reward or punishment would be realized; sufficient for reason's judgment of goodness and evil necessarily (intuitively) or of reward and punishment in the future, whether that resurrection is only spiritual or both spiritual and bodily. So what is required is absolute resurrection (according to the Mu'tazila).' Summary of the objection: That the disputed meaning between us and the Ash'aris is the third meaning: 'Deserving of His praise and reward in good, and deserving of His blame and punishment in evil.' Both deserving of praise and reward and deserving of blame and punishment are matters of the Hereafter, like the reality of the Fire, the Path, the weighing of deeds, Paradise and its bliss; they are not things perceived by reason. So how can goodness that necessitates deserving praise and reward be rational? Similarly, evil that necessitates punishment, how can it be rational? And how can something be good necessitating reward or be evil necessitating punishment by mere reason? And He said: 'It may be that you dislike something while it is good for you, and it may be that you love something while it is evil for you. God knows and you do not know' (Quran 2:216). The summary of the answer: That matters of the Hereafter have two meanings: One is what pertains to the specificity of bodily resurrection, i.e., the conception of bodily resurrection and its necessary concomitants. The second is what pertains to absolute resurrection and the abode of requital, whether bodily or otherwise. If the objector intends the first meaning, it is conceded that it is revealed, but it does not harm us, for we do not intend the first meaning from the matters of the Hereafter. If he intends the second meaning, then we do not concede that it is revealed; rather it is rational, because the requital of deeds is rationally obligatory according to the Mu'tazila. So if someone does a good deed, his reward is incumbent upon God according to them; if God does not reward him in this world, it is necessary that He give him his reward in the abode of requital after his death, of necessity. Thus absolute resurrection and the abode of requital are established without regard to whether his resurrection is bodily or spiritual. This is the summary of the first answer. As for the summary of the second answer: That the second meaning of goodness or evil (the disputed one) does not entail reward or punishment in the future actually, but rather hypothetically, i.e., on the supposition of the existence of absolute resurrection, reward or punishment exists. In this sense, goodness and evil are rational, for reason judges reward or punishment on the supposition of the existence of resurrection, even if it does not exist actually; this is merely a rational concomitant.

Commentary
This passage from 'Izalat al-Rahbūt' deals with the ethical-epistemological dispute between the Mu'tazila and the Ash'ariyya (and by extension the Ahl al-Sunna) concerning whether the goodness (ḥusn) and evil (qubḥ) of actions are known by reason alone ('aql) or require revelation (sam'). The author begins with an example: fasting on the last day of Ramadan is good, while fasting on the first day of Shawwal is evil—but reason cannot know this; only the Shariah reveals the intrinsic goodness or evil. This frames the objection (ishkāl) to the Mu'tazilī view that the third meaning of goodness (entailing divine reward/praise) and evil (entailing punishment/blame) is also known by reason. The objector (the Ash'arī) argues that matters of the afterlife, such as reward and punishment, are purely revealed (sam'ī); reason has no independent access. How then can reason judge such things and claim they are known intuitively (bi-dahā)? The Mu'tazila respond with two answers. First answer: Reason judges that justice is obligatory, which necessitates requital (mujāzāt) in another world. This requirement is rational, even though the specific details (e.g., bodily resurrection) are revealed. Thus reason can know that there will be an abode of requital absolutely, without specifying its form. The second answer: Even if the actual existence of resurrection is not known by reason, reason can judge that if resurrection exists, then reward and punishment follow necessarily. This is a rational conditional (taqdīrī), sufficient for the ethical judgment. The passage then provides a summary: the dispute centers on the third meaning of ḥusn/qubḥ (deserving divine praise/blame and reward/punishment). The objector cites the Quranic verse (2:216) to show that people's intuitive judgments can be wrong. The Mu'tazila's final response distinguishes two meanings of 'matters of the afterlife': (1) specific bodily resurrection (sam'ī), which they concede is revealed, and (2) absolute resurrection and requital, which they maintain is rationally necessary because of the principle of divine justice. They also note that in the second answer, the reward/punishment is not actual but conditional. The author is a Hanafī commentator, and the Mu'tazilī position is discussed from a perspective critical of it, aligning with the Ash'arī/orthodox view that the ethical qualification of acts and their retributive consequences cannot be independently known by reason. The commentary thus serves to clarify the contours of this usul al-fiqh debate.

Key Terms
الحسن الذاتي (al-ḥusn al-dhātī): Intrinsic goodness that the Shariah reveals, not reason.
المعاد (al-ma'ād): The resurrection or return to afterlife; here discussed as bodily and/or spiritual.
الاستحقاق (al-istiḥqāq): Deserving reward or punishment; for Mu'tazila this is a rational obligation, for Ash'aris it is a divine promise/threat known by revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the Mu'tazila's first answer to the Ash'ari objection?
A) Justice is rationally obligatory, therefore requital is necessary, and reason can know that there will be an abode of requital.
B) The Quran explicitly states that all actions will be rewarded or punished.
C) Human beings have an innate desire for reward that leads to rational certainty.
D) The consensus of the Companions proves the existence of the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the third meaning of goodness/evil—deserving divine reward/punishment—is primarily known through revelation, not reason alone, though reason can recognize the general principle of justice.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
The Rational Knowledge of Good and Evil: Mu'tazilite vs Ash'ari/Hanafi
Summary of objection and Mu'tazilite responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Mu'tazilite argument for rational knowledge of husn and qubh and critique it using the Hanafi/Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Good and evil in the sense of deserving reward/punishment are known through revelation, not reason.

This passage presents the central debate between the Mu'tazilites and the Ash'aris/Hanafis on whether the third meaning of husn and qubh—desert of reward and punishment—is known rationally or through revelation. The Mu'tazilites argue that justice necessitates reward for good deeds, even if not given in this world, so the afterlife must exist rationally. The Ash'ari/Hanafi response is that the afterlife is not rationally necessary; it is known only through revelation. Reason can err, as the Quran indicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third meaning of husn/qubh
Recognize that the contested meaning is desert of praise/reward (husn) and blame/punishment (qubh) in the afterlife.
This is distinct from the other two meanings (beauty/ugliness and conformity to object/desire).
2
State the Ash'ari/Hanafi objection
How can the desert of afterlife reward be rational when the afterlife itself is beyond reason's perception?
3
Analyze the first Mu'tazilite response
Distinguish between specific physical resurrection and absolute resurrection; the latter is rationally necessary because justice demands reward for good deeds.
This response assumes that the afterlife is rationally obligatory as a requirement of justice.
4
Analyze the second Mu'tazilite response
Rational judgment about reward/punishment is merely hypothetical (taqdiri), based on the assumption of resurrection, not actual existence.
This avoids the need to prove the actual afterlife rationally, but only a correlation.
5
Evaluate the weakness of the second response
The commentator points out that this only proves resurrection hypothetically, not definitively, contradicting the Mu'tazilite creed that the afterlife is rationally necessary.
This reveals the Mu'tazilite position as internally inconsistent.
