Removal of Fright from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut
Spiritually and physically, so what is sought is absolute resurrection (according to the Mu'tazilites)
Summary of the objection: That the contested meaning between us and the Ash'aris is the third meaning: "deserving His praise and reward in good, and deserving His blame and punishment in evil." These two—deserving praise and reward and deserving blame and punishment—are matters of the afterlife, like the reality of the Fire, the Bridge, the weighing of deeds, Paradise and its bliss. They are not things perceptible by reason. So how can the good that necessitates deserving praise and reward be rational? Likewise, the evil that necessitates punishment, how can it be rational? And how can something be good necessitating reward or evil necessitating punishment by mere reason?
And He said, the Exalted: "Perhaps you dislike something which is good for you and perhaps you love something which is bad for you. And Allah knows while you do not know."
The sum of the answer: That afterlife matters have two meanings:
One of them: what pertains specifically to physical resurrection, i.e., physical resurrection is conceived and its necessary consequences are established. The second: what pertains to absolute resurrection and the abode of recompense, whether physical or otherwise. If the objector intends the first meaning, it is granted that it is revelational, but it does not harm us, because we do not intend the first meaning from afterlife matters. If he intends the second meaning, we do not grant that it is revelational; rather it is rational, because the reward of deeds is rationally obligatory according to the Mu'tazilites. So if someone does a good righteous deed, his reward is obligatory upon God according to them; if God does not reward him in this world, it becomes necessary that He give him his reward in the abode of recompense necessarily after his death. Thus absolute resurrection and the abode of recompense are established without considering whether his resurrection is physical or spiritual. This is the gist of the first answer.
And the gist of the second answer: That the second meaning of good or evil (the contested one) does not entail reward or punishment in the afterlife actually, but hypothetically; i.e., on the supposition of the existence of absolute resurrection, reward or punishment would exist. In this sense, good and evil are rational, for reason judges reward or punishment on the supposition of the existence of resurrection, even if it does not actually exist. This is merely a rational correlation.
Removal of Fright from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut
His saying: "So ponder": Therein is an indication of the weakness of the second answer, because on this supposition (positing recompense by positing the existence of the Hereafter and resurrection) the existence of the Hereafter is not established definitively; rather the existence of resurrection and the Hereafter is established hypothetically and suppositionally, and this contradicts the belief of the Mu'tazilites.
The disagreement of the Mu'tazilites regarding what necessitates good and evil in actions. Then they disagreed: The ancients said: For the essence of the action; the later scholars said: Rather for a real attribute that necessitates it in both; and a group said: For a real attribute in evil only, and good is the absence of evil. And al-Jubba'i said: It is not a real attribute, but rather considerations. And the truth according to us is the absolute general meaning, so abrogation does not apply to us.
Know that the Mu'tazilites (after their agreement that good and evil in the third sense are rational) disagreed regarding what necessitates good and evil: What is it?
1. The ancients (of the Mu'tazilites) said: Good and evil are for the essence of the action, not for an attribute in the action that necessitates good or evil.
2. The later scholars among them said: Good and evil are not for the essence of the action, but rather for a real attribute that necessitates good or evil in both (in the good and the evil).
- A group (of the Mu'tazilites, like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and those who followed him) said: Evil is for a real attribute that exists in the evil only; as for good, it is the absence of evil, so it has no cause, neither essence nor real attribute nor attributive [attribute]. So good is that in which the attribute of evil does not exist, making good non-existent.
4. Al-Jubba'i (the chief of the Mu'tazilites and their leader) said: There is no essence or real attribute for good or evil, but rather aspects and considerations due to which good and evil differ, like striking an orphan: it can be for discipline or for torture. The first is good due to the goodness of discipline, the second is evil due to the evil of torture. The meaning of this statement is that good and evil have no universal principle; rather good and evil differ in one action according to different aspects or different considerations, as in the given example.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a point from the matn 'Muslim al-Thubut' regarding the Mu'tazilite position on the rational basis of good (husn) and evil (qubh). The author first presents an objection from the Ash'aris: how can the third meaning of husn/qubh—which concerns deserving reward/punishment in the afterlife—be rational when the afterlife is beyond reason? The Mu'tazilites respond with two answers. First, they distinguish between specific physical resurrection and absolute resurrection; the latter is rationally necessary because justice demands reward for good deeds, even if not given in this world. Second, they argue that rational judgment about reward/punishment is only hypothetical (taqdiri), not actual, so it is a rational correlation. The commentator then notes a weakness in the second answer: it only proves resurrection hypothetically, not definitively, contradicting the Mu'tazilite creed. Following this, the passage details the internal Mu'tazilite disagreement on what causes an act to be good or evil: (1) essence of the act (ancients), (2) a real attribute (later scholars), (3) a real attribute only for evil, good being its absence (Abu al-Husayn al-Basri), and (4) considerations/aspects (al-Jubba'i). The author concludes by stating the Hanafi position: the absolute general meaning (al-itlaq al-amm), which avoids the problem of abrogation. The passage thus serves to refute the Mu'tazilite rationalist view and present the Ash'ari/Hanafi stance.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): The quality of an act that makes it deserving of praise and reward (third meaning).
القبح (al-qubh): The quality of an act that makes it deserving of blame and punishment (third meaning).
المعاد (al-ma'ad): Resurrection; either physical or absolute (mortal or immortal).

The Hanafi school holds that moral desert in the afterlife is known through revelation, not reason.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Internal Mu'tazilite Disagreement on the Cause of Good and Evil
Internal Mu'tazilite disagreement and Hanafi resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Mu'tazilite positions on what causes an act to be good or evil and explain the Hanafi position of al-itlaq al-amm.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites disagreed on the cause of husn/qubh; the Hanafis hold a general absolute meaning that avoids abrogation.

After affirming that good and evil in the third sense are rational, the Mu'tazilites split on what in the act necessitates its moral quality: the essence, a real attribute, absence of evil, or considerations. The author presents these views and then states the Hanafi/Ash'ari position: the absolute general meaning (al-itlaq al-amm), which avoids the problem of abrogation (naskh) and is consistent with revelation.

Guided Path
1
Recall the agreed rational basis
All Mu'tazilites agree that husn/qubh in the third sense are rationally known.
2
State the position of the ancients
They held that good and evil are due to the essence (dhat) of the act itself, not an attribute.
3
State the position of the later scholars
They held that a real attribute (sifah haqiqiyyah) in the act necessitates its goodness or evil.
4
State the position of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers
Evil is due to a real attribute; good is merely the absence of that attribute.
This makes good a privation (adami).
5
State the position of al-Jubba'i
Good and evil are not inherent or attributes but considerations (i'tibarat) based on aspects like purpose.
E.g., striking an orphan can be good or evil depending on intent.
6
Explain the Hanafi position
The truth according to us (ahl al-sunnah) is the absolute general meaning: good and evil are determined by revelation without specifying a cause, which avoids the problem of abrogation.
Abrogation (naskh) would be problematic if good/evil were inherent (essence/attribute) because something cannot change.

Removal of Fright from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut
Spiritually and physically, so what is sought is absolute resurrection (according to the Mu'tazilites)
Summary of the objection: That the contested meaning between us and the Ash'aris is the third meaning: "deserving His praise and reward in good, and deserving His blame and punishment in evil." These two—deserving praise and reward and deserving blame and punishment—are matters of the afterlife, like the reality of the Fire, the Bridge, the weighing of deeds, Paradise and its bliss. They are not things perceptible by reason. So how can the good that necessitates deserving praise and reward be rational? Likewise, the evil that necessitates punishment, how can it be rational? And how can something be good necessitating reward or evil necessitating punishment by mere reason?
And He said, the Exalted: "Perhaps you dislike something which is good for you and perhaps you love something which is bad for you. And Allah knows while you do not know."
The sum of the answer: That afterlife matters have two meanings:
One of them: what pertains specifically to physical resurrection, i.e., physical resurrection is conceived and its necessary consequences are established. The second: what pertains to absolute resurrection and the abode of recompense, whether physical or otherwise. If the objector intends the first meaning, it is granted that it is revelational, but it does not harm us, because we do not intend the first meaning from afterlife matters. If he intends the second meaning, we do not grant that it is revelational; rather it is rational, because the reward of deeds is rationally obligatory according to the Mu'tazilites. So if someone does a good righteous deed, his reward is obligatory upon God according to them; if God does not reward him in this world, it becomes necessary that He give him his reward in the abode of recompense necessarily after his death. Thus absolute resurrection and the abode of recompense are established without considering whether his resurrection is physical or spiritual. This is the gist of the first answer.
And the gist of the second answer: That the second meaning of good or evil (the contested one) does not entail reward or punishment in the afterlife actually, but hypothetically; i.e., on the supposition of the existence of absolute resurrection, reward or punishment would exist. In this sense, good and evil are rational, for reason judges reward or punishment on the supposition of the existence of resurrection, even if it does not actually exist. This is merely a rational correlation.
Removal of Fright from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut
His saying: "So ponder": Therein is an indication of the weakness of the second answer, because on this supposition (positing recompense by positing the existence of the Hereafter and resurrection) the existence of the Hereafter is not established definitively; rather the existence of resurrection and the Hereafter is established hypothetically and suppositionally, and this contradicts the belief of the Mu'tazilites.
The disagreement of the Mu'tazilites regarding what necessitates good and evil in actions. Then they disagreed: The ancients said: For the essence of the action; the later scholars said: Rather for a real attribute that necessitates it in both; and a group said: For a real attribute in evil only, and good is the absence of evil. And al-Jubba'i said: It is not a real attribute, but rather considerations. And the truth according to us is the absolute general meaning, so abrogation does not apply to us.
Know that the Mu'tazilites (after their agreement that good and evil in the third sense are rational) disagreed regarding what necessitates good and evil: What is it?
1. The ancients (of the Mu'tazilites) said: Good and evil are for the essence of the action, not for an attribute in the action that necessitates good or evil.
2. The later scholars among them said: Good and evil are not for the essence of the action, but rather for a real attribute that necessitates good or evil in both (in the good and the evil).
- A group (of the Mu'tazilites, like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and those who followed him) said: Evil is for a real attribute that exists in the evil only; as for good, it is the absence of evil, so it has no cause, neither essence nor real attribute nor attributive [attribute]. So good is that in which the attribute of evil does not exist, making good non-existent.
4. Al-Jubba'i (the chief of the Mu'tazilites and their leader) said: There is no essence or real attribute for good or evil, but rather aspects and considerations due to which good and evil differ, like striking an orphan: it can be for discipline or for torture. The first is good due to the goodness of discipline, the second is evil due to the evil of torture. The meaning of this statement is that good and evil have no universal principle; rather good and evil differ in one action according to different aspects or different considerations, as in the given example.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a point from the matn 'Muslim al-Thubut' regarding the Mu'tazilite position on the rational basis of good (husn) and evil (qubh). The author first presents an objection from the Ash'aris: how can the third meaning of husn/qubh—which concerns deserving reward/punishment in the afterlife—be rational when the afterlife is beyond reason? Second, they argue that rational judgment about reward/punishment is only hypothetical (taqdiri), not actual, so it is a rational correlation. The commentator then notes a weakness in the second answer: it only proves resurrection hypothetically, not definitively, contradicting the Mu'tazilite creed. Following this, the passage details the internal Mu'tazilite disagreement on what causes an act to be good or evil: (1) essence of the act (ancients), (2) a real attribute (later scholars), (3) a real attribute only for evil, good being its absence (Abu al-Husayn al-Basri), and (4) considerations/aspects (al-Jubba'i). The author concludes by stating the Hanafi position: the absolute general meaning (al-itlaq al-amm), which avoids the problem of abrogation. The passage thus serves to refute the Mu'tazilite rationalist view and present the Ash'ari/Hanafi stance.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): The quality of an act that makes it deserving of praise and reward (third meaning).
القبح (al-qubh): The quality of an act that makes it deserving of blame and punishment (third meaning).
المعاد (al-ma'ad): Resurrection; either physical or absolute (mortal or immortal).
الإطلاق الأعم (al-itlaq al-amm): The absolute general meaning; the principle that moral qualities are determined by general revelatory meanings without specifying a cause.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Basis of Rational Good and Bad: Mu'tazilite vs. Maturidi Views
Exposition of four Mu'tazilite positions and the Maturidi fifth view with naskh rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Mu'tazilite positions on the basis of rational good and bad, explain the Maturidi (Hanafi) position of al-itlaq al-a'mm, and apply the argument from naskh to evaluate why the Maturidi view is preferred.

Core Idea
Rational good and bad (husn/qubh) can change; the Maturidi view allows abrogation, unlike most Mu'tazilite views.

This lesson examines a foundational usul dispute about the ontological basis of rational moral evaluation. The Mu'tazilites debated whether good and bad are intrinsic to actions, due to real attributes, or merely conventional. The Hanafi school, following the Maturidi theology, adopts the most general absolute (al-itlaq al-a'mm), which allows moral status to change through abrogation (naskh). Understanding this dispute clarifies why certain rulings can transform across Shari'as.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What exactly is being debated? The passage deals with the basis (mu'jib) of rational good (husn) and bad (qubh) in actions.
This is a theological-legal question: are these moral qualities intrinsic or extrinsic to the act?
2
List the four Mu'tazilite positions
Read the passage and extract each group's view: (1) ancients: essence, (2) later ones: real attribute in both, (3) some: real attribute only in bad, good is its absence, (4) al-Jubba'i: considerations.
Each position represents a different theory of moral ontology.
3
State the Maturidi (Hanafi) fifth position
The passage declares 'the truth is the most general absolute' (al-itlaq al-a'mm). Understand that this means husn/qubh can be due to essence, real attribute, or conventional attribute, whichever applies.
This is the operative position in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the naskh argument
The author argues that if husn/qubh were intrinsic (essence or real attribute), they could never change. Yet the Shari'a contains abrogations like visiting graves and the 'idda period, which changed from bad to good and vice versa. This supports the Maturidi view.
The argument is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on observed Shari'a rulings.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Hanafi view, a mufti can recognize that moral statuses can vary across contexts and times, while under many Mu'tazilite views, this would be impossible.
This impacts istinbat when considering previous Shari'as or changed circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والقول الخامس (وهو الحق) عند (الماتريدية) هو الإطلاق الأعم من الذات و الصفة الحقيقية والاعتبارية، فلايرد علينا الماتريديين عدم إمكان النسخ في النصوص، لأجل أن الحسن و القبح، إذا كانا لذات الفعل أو لصفته الحقيقية التي لا تنفك عنه لا يزول الحسن أو القبح عن محله أبدا، لأن ما بالذات أو بالصفة الحقيقية لا تزول قط، فكيف يصير الحسن بالنسخ قبيحا، ويصير القبيح بالنسخ حسنا؟ كما أن زيارة القبور كانت قبيحة وممنوعة قبل النسخ، وصارت حسنة وجائزة بعد النسخ، فلو كان القبح لذات الزيارة أو لصفته الحقيقية فما نسخت قط، وكذلك عدة المتوفي عنها زوجها إلى عام كانت سنة وكانت حسنا، ثم نسخت بأربعة أشهر وعشرا، فلم يبق حسنها السابق، ولو كان الحسن لذات العدة في نسخت، قط .

The disagreement of the Mu'tazila regarding what necessitates good and bad in actions: they disagreed. The ancients said: due to the essence of the act. The later ones said: rather due to a real attribute necessitating it in both. Some said: due to a real attribute in bad only, and good is the absence of bad. Al-Jubba'i said: not a real attribute, but considerations. And the truth with us is the most general absolute, so abrogation does not apply to us. Know that the Mu'tazila (after agreeing that good and bad in the third sense are rational) disagreed about what necessitates good and bad, what is it? - The ancients (of the Mu'tazila) said: good and bad are due to the essence of the act, not due to an attribute in the act that necessitates good or bad. - The later ones among them said: good and bad are not due to the essence of the act, but due to a real attribute necessitating good or bad in both (in good and bad). - Some (of the Mu'tazila, like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his followers) said: bad is due to a real attribute that exists in the bad only, and as for good, it is the absence of bad, so it has no cause, neither essence, nor real attribute, nor conventional attribute; good is that in which the attribute of bad is not present, so good is privative. - Al-Jubba'i (the head and imam of the Mu'tazila) said: (good or bad is) not due to the essence of the act nor due to a real attribute, but rather (aspects) and considerations by which good and bad differ, like slapping an orphan: it may be for discipline or for torture. The first is good due to the goodness of discipline, the second is bad due to the badness of torture. The meaning of this statement is that good and bad have no universal rule, but rather good and bad differ in one act according to two different considerations or various considerations, as in the mentioned example. And the fifth opinion (which is the truth) according to the Maturidis is the most general absolute of essence, real attribute, and conventional attribute, so the impossibility of abrogation in texts does not apply to us (Maturidis), because if good and bad were due to the essence of the act or due to its real attribute that is inseparable from it, then good or bad would never leave its place, because what is by essence or by real attribute never disappears. So how could good become bad by abrogation, and bad become good by abrogation? For example, visiting graves was bad and prohibited before abrogation, then became good and permissible after abrogation. If the badness were due to the essence of visiting or due to its real attribute, it would never have been abrogated. Similarly, the waiting period of a widow for a year was a sunna and was good, then it was abrogated by four months and ten days, so its previous goodness did not remain. If the goodness were due to the essence of the waiting period, it would never have been abrogated. The problem of abrogation only applies to the Mu'tazila who assert causation by essence or real attribute.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh concerning the basis of the rational categories of good (husn) and bad (qubh) in actions. The author presents four Mu'tazilite positions, then states the Maturidi (Hanafi) position and defends it against the objection of abrogation. The first Mu'tazilite view (the ancients) holds that good and bad are intrinsic to the act itself (dhat al-fi'l). The second view (later ones) says they are due to a real attribute (sifa haqiqiyya) in the act. The third view (some like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri) restricts the real attribute to bad only, while good is merely the absence of bad. The fourth view (al-Jubba'i) denies both essence and real attribute, saying it is based on considerations (i'tibarat) that vary. The Maturidi fifth view is 'the most general absolute' (al-itlaq al-a'mm), meaning that husn/qubh can be based on essence, real attribute, or conventional attribute as the case may be. This broad definition avoids the problem of naskh (abrogation): if husn/qubh were intrinsic or by a real attribute inseparable from the act, then an act could never change from good to bad or vice versa by abrogation. The author gives examples: visiting graves was prohibited (bad) then later permitted (good); the widow's 'idda was one year (good) then changed to four months and ten days. Both would be impossible if husn/qubh were fixed by essence or real attribute. Thus the Maturidi position saves the possibility of naskh, while the Mu'tazilite views (except al-Jubba'i's) face the naskh objection. The passage also implicitly shows that the Hanafi school follows the Maturidi theological position on this point.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): The quality of being good in a rational sense (not necessarily Shari'a-based), as used in the dispute over the basis of moral evaluation.
القبح (al-qubh): The quality of being bad in a rational sense; the passage discusses its basis in essence or attribute.
الإطلاق الأعم (al-itlaq al-a'mm): The most general absolute: the Maturidi position that husn/qubh can be due to essence, real attribute, or conventional attribute, whichever applies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi position stated in the passage, why does the abrogation of visiting graves (from prohibited to permitted) pose a problem for the Mu'tazilite view that bad (qubh) is due to a real attribute?
A) Because the real attribute of bad would need to change, but real attributes are inseparable.
B) Because visiting graves was always good, not bad.
C) Because the Mu'tazila do not believe in abrogation.
D) Because the Maturidi view also denies abrogation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi (Maturidi) view of al-itlaq al-a'mm preserves the possibility of naskh and is preferred over the Mu'tazilite positions that tie husn/qubh to immutable essences or attributes.

Reason's Independence in Hanafi Usul: The Maturidi View
Presentation of Hanafi views on reason's independent knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position that rational good and bad can independently obligate rulings and apply it to the case of a rational minor.

Core Idea
Some Hanafis: reason can independently obligate faith for rational minors — rational good/bad are a basis for ruling.

This passage reveals an important nuance within the Hanafi school: while many think the school relies solely on revelation, a group of Hanafis, including Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, affirm that reason can independently know certain fundamental rulings, such as the obligation of faith and prohibition of disbelief. This is based on a statement of Abu Hanifa himself, implying that rational evidence can lead to knowledge of the Creator. The author qualifies that this applies after a period of reflection, varying by intellect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Note the key claim: some Hanafis hold that reason can be independent (yastaqillu) in perceiving some of Allah's rulings, specifically obligating faith and prohibiting disbelief for a rational minor.
The term 'yastaqillu' implies self-sufficiency without revelation.
2
Locate the proof
Find the narration from Abu Hanifa: 'There is no excuse for anyone in ignorance of his Creator, due to what he sees of evidences.' This is the core textual evidence for the position.
This is considered a qawl of the Imam, used as a proof within the madhhab.
3
Understand the condition
The commentator adds that this applies after a period of reflection (ta'mmul), which varies according to intellectual capacity. Reflection serves as a substitute for the prophetic call.
Thus, the rational minor is not instantly accountable; he must have time to reflect.
4
Recognize the internal disagreement
The phrase 'some Hanafis' indicates that this is not the unanimous position within the school. Other Hanafis likely hold that revelation is necessary.
This intra-madhhab ikhtilaf shows the range of Hanafi thought.
5
Realize the implication
The conclusion: rational good and bad can entail rulings (hukm) in limited cases, such as basic faith and disbelief, even for those not reached by a prophet.
This is a key usul principle with implications for taklif of minors and non-Muslims.

Commentary
This passage discusses a group of Hanafis who hold that rational good (husn aqli) and rational bad (qubh aqli) can themselves entail a ruling (hukm) from Allah, meaning that reason can independently know some obligations and prohibitions without revelation. Specifically, some Hanafis say that reason can perceive the obligation of faith (iman) and the prohibition of disbelief (kufr) for a rational minor (sabi 'aqil) who understands the evidence. This is based on a narration from Abu Hanifa: 'There is no excuse for anyone in ignorance of his Creator, due to what he sees of evidences.' The author (commentator) adds a qualification: this applies after a period of reflection (tamul), which varies according to intellectual capacity, and this reflection serves like the call of prophets in awakening the heart. Then the author mentions another group, specifically Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and some Iraqi Hanafi scholars, who similarly hold that reason can independently know some rulings, obligating faith and prohibiting disbelief for the rational minor who knows the goodness of faith and badness of disbelief. The proof cited is again the statement of Abu Hanifa. This position is significant in Hanafi usul as it affirms that rational good/bad can serve as a source of knowledge for certain fundamental obligations even in the absence of revelation, though this is limited to basic matters like faith in the Creator.

Key Terms
الحسن العقلي (al-husn al-'aqli): Rational goodness: the quality of being good that reason can perceive independently of revelation, used here as a basis for obligation.
القبح العقلي (al-qubh al-'aqli): Rational badness: the quality of being bad that reason can perceive independently, used here as a basis for prohibition.
الصبي العاقل (al-sabi al-'aqil): A minor who has reached intellectual maturity (tamyiz) but not legal majority; such a person can understand rational good and bad.
التأمل (al-ta'mmul): Reflection: the period of intellectual contemplation needed to arrive at knowledge of the Creator through evidence.

Maturidi Proof for Inherent Moral Values: Universal Consensus of Rational People
Maturidi rational proof using universal consensus for inherent good and evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Maturidi proof for inherent moral values, explain the example of beneficence, and evaluate the Ash'ari objection based on general benefit (maṣlaḥa).

Core Idea
Maturidis prove inherent good/evil through universal rational agreement on beneficence, even by atheists.

In the previous discussion, we examined the ruling on a person who never receives revelation, highlighting the debate between those who hold reason obligates and those who require revelation. Now, the passage presents the Maturidi school's rational proof that some actions are inherently good or evil, known independently of revelation. The proof rests on a striking example: all rational beings, including those who deny God and prophets, agree that beneficence is good and repaying kindness with harm is evil. This agreement, the Maturidis argue, demonstrates that these moral values are intrinsic to the acts themselves, not merely derived from scripture or social convention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maturidi claim
The Maturidis assert that some actions are inherently good or evil (ḥusn/qubḥ dhātī), meaning their moral value is an intrinsic property knowable by reason alone, independent of revelation.
This claim is more limited than the Mu'tazila position that all actions have inherent moral value.
2
Examine the proof from universal consensus
The Maturidis cite the universal agreement of rational people ('uqalā') that beneficence (iḥsān) is good and reciprocating beneficence with harm is evil. This consensus includes even atheists like the Brahmins, who reject revelation entirely.
The proof is an ijmā' of rational people, not technical scholarly consensus.
3
Analyze the first Ash'ari objection
The Ash'aris object that this consensus may stem from recognition of general benefit (maṣlaḥa ʿāmma) rather than inherent essence. People agree beneficence is good because it brings social harmony, not because the act is intrinsically good.
Ash'aris argue that without revelation, we cannot know if something is truly good in God's sight.
4
Analyze the Maturidi response
The author counters that even if the consensus arises from benefit, the benefit itself is rationally recognized as good. Thus reason can independently identify good, which is the core Maturidi claim. The claim is not that all good is essential, but that reason can know moral value without revelation.
The Maturidi response shifts the focus from essence to epistemological independence of reason.

From the rational proofs for His existence and oneness. When the author saw that this statement and this narration entail that rational good and evil necessitate a ruling, as the four aforementioned groups (Mu'tazila, Imamiyya, Karramiyya, and Brahmins) said, he wanted to respond to this statement and its proof narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa and said: I say: perhaps the meaning of the narration of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) is that no one has an excuse after a period of reflection on the proofs, because reflection in the possible period is like the prophet's call in awakening the heart, and that period has no specific limit but differs due to differences in intellects, for intellects vary so the period varies accordingly. According to the Ash'aris and the majority of Hanafis, he will not be punished because the ruling of obligation of faith and action, and then punishment for abandoning it, is only by revelation, and it is assumed he did not receive it. Were each of them inherent, they would not have agreed upon them. Their agreement is proof that good and evil may be inherent, not dependent on revelation. The gist of the Maturidi claim is that the goodness and evil of actions may be due to the essence of the act (like the mentioned example) and may be due to an attribute in it, whether real or considered. Their first proof is what the author mentioned saying: 'For us' etc. The answer that it may be for a general benefit does not harm us, because observing general benefit is necessarily good. It would only harm us if we claimed it is for the essence of the act, but our claim is non-dependence on revelation. And the denial of consensus that it is the basis for His ruling does not touch us, for we do not say it necessitates a ruling from Him; rather that necessitation is by revelation. The Ash'aris objected in two ways: First: It is possible that the goodness of beneficence and evil of reciprocating with harm are not due to their essence, but goodness and evil in them and the agreement on them are for a general benefit, to prevent closing the door of benefit and opening the door of harm, so they are not necessarily inherent to the act. The author answered: That they are good and evil for observing benefit does not harm our claim, because observing general benefit is necessarily good (by intuition). It would only harm us if we claimed that each is good/evil for the essence of the act, and this is not our claim. Rather our claim is that knowledge of good and evil in actions does not depend on the arrival of revelation. Second: We do not accept that consensus of rational people indicates that the goodness of beneficence and evil of reciprocating it...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a theological-legal dispute (ikhtilaf) between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools on the epistemological basis of moral values (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ). The author first clarifies a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa about the obligation to reflect on proofs before revelation reaches a person. The discussion then branches into the famous case (mas'ala) of an adult who lives on a high mountain peak and never receives any prophetic call. The Mu'tazila and a minority of Hanafis hold that such a person will be punished for not believing and acting, because reason alone obligates these. The Ash'aris and majority Hanafis hold that he will not be punished, since obligation and punishment come only through revelation (Shar'). The author cites the Quranic verse: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). The core of the passage is the Maturidi proof that some actions are inherently good or evil, recognized universally even by atheists (Brahmins). The example is the goodness of beneficence (iḥsān) and the evil of repaying kindness with harm. The Maturidis argue that universal rational consensus (ijmā' al-'uqalā') proves these values are intrinsic to the acts themselves, not dependent on revelation. However, the Maturidis' claim is not that all moral values are inherent, but that some are. The Ash'aris raise two objections: first, agreement may be due to general expediency (maṣlaḥa) rather than inherent value. The author responds that expediency itself is considered rationally good, which still supports the claim that reason can know good without revelation. The second objection denies the probative value of consensus. The author replies that the Maturidis do not assert that rational good necessitates a divine ruling; that necessitates revelation only. This dispute has direct implications for uṣūl al-fiqh: if good and evil are rationally known, then reason can serve as a source of law (aql) alongside revelation, affecting rulings on acts before revelation and obligations of minors, etc. The majority Hanafi position (madhhab) as followed in fiqh is Ash'ari on this point, i.e., that moral values are known only through Shar'.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ): Moral goodness and evil attributed to actions; the Maturidis argue these can be intrinsic and known by reason.
الذاتي (al-dhātī): Inherent in the essence of an act, not from external factors like revelation or benefit.
المصلحة العامة (al-maṣlaḥa al-ʿāmma): General public benefit; Ash'aris argue that rational consensus may stem from this rather than inherent essence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Prayer and fasting
B) Beneficence (iḥsān) and reciprocating kindness with harm
C) Truthfulness and honesty
D) Justice and injustice

Key Takeaway
The universal rational consensus on beneficence being good and harm being evil is the Maturidis' strongest evidence that some moral values are inherent, defended against Ash'ari objections that the consensus may be due to benefit.

The First Maturidi Proof: Universal Agreement on Good and Evil
First Maturidi proof and Ash'ari rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the first Maturidi proof from universal agreement of rational people and evaluate the Ash'ari objection and Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Universal agreement among rational people proves that goodness and evil are intrinsic and rationally known.

In this lesson, we examine the first Maturidi argument that goodness and evil are intrinsic to actions and knowable by reason, independent of revelation. The proof rests on universal consensus, even among those who reject revelation, like the Brahmins. We will follow the dialectic between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris, and understand how the Hanafi school maintains that moral knowledge is rational while reward and punishment remain revealed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maturidi claim
State clearly what the Maturidis assert: that goodness and evil can be intrinsic (dhātī) and known by reason, not dependent on revelation.
The claim is about knowledge, not about reward/punishment.
2
Analyze the first proof (Ijma)
Examine the proof from universal agreement: rational people universally agree that beneficence is good and harming benefactors is evil. This agreement would not occur if these values were not intrinsic.
The proof cites even non-believers (Brahmins) to show independence from revelation.
3
Understand the first Ash'ari objection
The Ash'aris object that the agreement could be due to general benefit, not intrinsic worth. Understand this objection.
They argue that the consensus might be based on utility, not on intrinsic moral qualities.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The author responds: even if based on benefit, that benefit is still rationally known, so the claim that moral knowledge requires revelation is defeated.
The Maturidis do not claim that reward/punishment follow rationally, only that we can know good/evil by reason.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: according to Hanafi/Maturidi, one can know that justice is good without revelation, while Ash'aris hold that nothing is known as good or evil until revelation declares it.
This affects the basis of legal obligation (taklīf) and the role of reason in ijtihad.

Source Text

وَمِنْ من استدل عليه بقوله : لنا (للماتريدية) أن حسن الإحسان وقبح مقابلته بالإساءة مما اتفق عليه العقلاء، حتى من لا يقول ( ولا يعتقد) بإرسال الرسل كالبراهمة (عابدي البرهمن معبود (الهنود فلولا أن كل واحد منهما ذاتي لم يكن كذلك (لم يتفقوا عليها)، فاتفاقهم دليل على أن الحسن و القبح قد يكونان ذاتيين غير متوقفين على الشرع. و حاصل دعوى الماتريدية: أن حسن الأفعال وقبحها قد يكونان لذات الفعل كالمثال المذكور) وقد يكونان لصفة فيه سواء كانت ذاتية أو اعتبارية)، ودليلهم الأول هو ما ذكره المصنف بقوله : (لنا) الخ والجواب بأنه يجوز أن يكون لمصلحة عامة لا يضرنا؛ لأن رعاية المصلحة العامة حسن بالضرورة ، وإنما يضرنا لو ادعينا أنه لذات الفعل، بل الدعوى عدم التوقف على الشرع ومنع الاتفاق على أنه مناط لحكمه تعالى لا يمسنا ؛ فإنا لا نقول باستلزامه حكماً منه تعالى بل ذلك الاستلزام بالسمع واعترض عليه الأشاعرة بوجهين : الأول : أنه يمكن أن لا يكون حسن الإحسان وقبح مقابلته بالإساءة لأجل ذاتها، بل كان الحسن والقبح فيهما والاتفاق عليهما، لأجل مصلحة عامة لئلا ينفتح باب المصلحة، ولا ينفتح باب المفسدة، فلا يلزم كونها ذاتيين للفعل . فأجاب عنه المصنف : بأن كونها حسنا وقبيحا لرعاية المصلحة لايضر في دعوانا، لأن رعاية المصلحة العامة حسن بالضرورة بالبداهة)، وإنما يضرنا رعاية المصلحة لو ادعينا أن كلاً من الحسن و القبح لذات الفعل، وليس هذا دعوانا، بل دعوانا أن معرفة الحسن و القبح في الأفعال لا تتوقف على ورود الشرع. والثاني : أنه لا نسلم أن اتفاق العقلاء يدل على أن حسن الإحسان وقبح مقابلته بالإساءة علة لوجوب الثواب في الأول، ووجوب العقاب في الثاني، كما تقولون أنتم فأجاب عنه المصنف بقوله : " ومنع الاتفاق اتفاق العقلاء) على أن كل واحد من الحسن و القبح مناط ( علة ) حكمه تعالى بالثواب والعقاب، ولا يمتنا منع الاتفاق ولا يضرنا، فإنا لا نقول باستلزام الحسن الثواب عقلاً واستلزام القبيح العقاب عقلاً بل ترتب وجوب الثواب والعقاب بالسمع فقط. وحاصل الجواب أن الاتفاق المذكور كان على كون الحسن والقبح عقليين، لا على كون الحسن علة للثواب والقبح علة للعقاب . واستدل (بأنه) إذا استوى الصدق والكذب في المقصود أثر العقل الصدق والدليل الثاني للماتريدية هو قوله (واستدل الخ) وحاصل الدليل على كونهما عقليين أنه إذا ستوى الصدق والكذب في حصول مقصود من المقاصد يختار العقل الصدق لحسنه، ويترك الكذب لقبحه كنجاة اسير وخلاصه بالطريقين ( بالصدق والكذب) فعلم أن الحسن و القبح عقليان . وفيه أنه لا استواء في نفس الأمر؛ لأن لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض، فهو تقدير مستحيل فيمتنع الإيثار على ذلك التقدير وأجاب عنه المصنف بقوله : (وفيه انه الخ). وتفصيل الجواب : أن لا استواء بين الصدق والكذب في نفس الأمر، وترجيح الصدق على الكذب في المثال المذكور، لأجل ذلك الفرق النفس الأمرى، لأن لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض غير لوازم وعوارض الآخر، واختلاف اللوازم والعوارض دليل على اختلاف الملزوم والمعروض ففرض الاستواء بين الصدق والكذب هو فرض محال، والمحال (وهو ذلك الفرض) يستلرم محالاً آخر، وهو ترجيح الصدق لأجل الاستواء مع الكذب، مع أنه
And from the proofs: He said: For us (the Maturidis) that the goodness of beneficence and the evil of reciprocating it with harm is something on which rational people agree, even those who do not believe in the sending of messengers like the Brahmins (worshippers of Brahma, the idol of the Hindus). So if each of them were not intrinsic (dhātī) it would not be so (they would not agree on it). Their agreement is evidence that goodness and evil may be intrinsic, not dependent on revelation. The summary of the Maturidi claim: Goodness and evil of actions may be due to the essence of the act (like the mentioned example) and may be due to a quality in it, whether essential or accidental. Their first proof is what the author mentioned: "For us" etc. The answer: It is possible that it is for a general benefit, which does not harm us; because observing general benefit is necessarily good, and it would harm us only if we claimed it is for the essence of the act. Rather our claim is non-dependence on revelation. And denying consensus that it is the basis for His judgment does not harm us; for we do not say it entails a ruling from Him, but that entailment is through revelation. The Ash'aris objected with two points: First: It is possible that the goodness of beneficence and evil of reciprocating harm is not due to their essence, but the goodness and evil therein and the agreement upon them is for a general benefit, so that the door of benefit is not opened, and the door of harm is not opened; thus they are not necessarily intrinsic to the act. The author answered: That they are good and evil for observing benefit does not harm our claim, because observing general benefit is necessarily good, and it would harm us only if we claimed each of goodness and evil is for the essence of the act, and that is not our claim. Rather our claim is that knowledge of goodness and evil in actions does not depend on the advent of revelation. Second: We do not agree that the agreement of rational people indicates that the goodness of beneficence and evil of reciprocating with harm is a cause for reward in the first and punishment in the second, as you claim. The author answered: "And denying consensus (of rational people) that each of goodness and evil is the basis for His judgment of reward and punishment does not harm us; for we do not say that goodness entails reward by reason or that evil entails punishment by reason; rather the entailment of reward and punishment is only through revelation." The summary of the answer: The mentioned consensus was on goodness and evil being rational, not on goodness being a cause for reward and evil a cause for punishment. And he argued: If truth and falsehood are equal in purpose, reason prefers truth. The second proof of the Maturidis is his statement: "And he argued (etc.)". The summary of the proof on their being rational: If truth and falsehood are equal in achieving some purpose, reason chooses truth for its goodness and leaves falsehood for its evil, like saving a prisoner by both means (truth and falsehood); thus it is known that goodness and evil are rational. And in it (the objection): There is no real equality; because each has its own necessary concomitants and accidents; it is an impossible assumption, so preference cannot occur on that assumption. The author answered: "And in it (etc.)". Detail of the answer: There is no equality between truth and falsehood in reality, and the preference of truth over falsehood in the mentioned example is due to that real difference, because each has its own necessary concomitants and accidents different from the other, and difference of concomitants and accidents indicates difference of the subject and attribute. So assuming equality between truth and falsehood is an impossible assumption, and the impossible requires another impossibility, namely preferring truth for the sake of equality with falsehood, while in fact it is not equal.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute between the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools on whether goodness (ḥusn) and evil (qubḥ) are intrinsic (dhātī) and knowable by reason, or purely revealed. The Maturidis (followed by the Hanafis in usul) argue that reason can independently recognize moral qualities. Their first proof: universal agreement on the goodness of beneficence and evil of harming benefactors, even among non-believers like Brahmins. If these judgments were not intrinsic, such agreement would not obtain. The Ash'aris counter: the agreement may stem from consideration of general benefit, not intrinsic morals. The author (Badakhshani) replies: benefit-based goodness is still rationally known, so the point stands—knowledge of goodness does not require revelation. The second Ash'ari objection: consensus does not prove that goodness is a cause (ʻilla) for reward or evil for punishment. The author clarifies: the Maturidis only claim rational knowledge of good and evil, not that reward/punishment follow rationally; that requires revelation. The second Maturidi proof: when truth and falsehood both achieve the same purpose, reason prefers truth for its goodness. The Ash'ari objection: such equality is impossible in reality due to differing consequences. The author concedes but argues the preference still shows rational judgment of goodness and evil. This debate is foundational in Hanafi usul, affirming that reason has a role in knowing moral values, while reward and punishment remain revealed. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that some acts are good or bad by nature and reason can know this, though legal obligation (taklīf) requires revelation.

Key Terms
حسن (ḥusn): The quality of an act that makes it praiseworthy by reason or revelation.
قبح (qubḥ): The quality of an act that makes it blameworthy by reason or revelation.
ذاتي (dhātī): Intrinsic to the essence of the act, not dependent on external factors.
عقلي (ʿaqlī): Knowable by reason without need for revelation.
سمعي (samʿī): Derived from revelation (Quran and Sunnah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Maturidi first proof from universal agreement?
A) That all rational people believe in God.
B) That goodness and evil are known by reason, not just revelation.
C) That reward and punishment are determined by reason.
D) That the Brahmins have correct moral beliefs.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi/Maturidi school affirms that reason can independently know some moral values, while reward and punishment remain dependent on revelation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

The Second Maturidi Proof: Reason's Preference for Truth
Second Maturidi proof and Ash'ari objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the second Maturidi proof from reason's preference for truth over falsehood and evaluate the Ash'ari objection and Hanafi response.

Core Idea
When truth and falsehood achieve the same purpose, reason prefers truth—proving that reason can recognize moral qualities.

The second Maturidi argument is that if truth and falsehood are equally effective in achieving a goal, reason still chooses truth, indicating that truth is inherently good and falsehood inherently evil. The Ash'aris object that such equality is impossible in reality. We will see how the author defends the rational basis of moral distinction even in a hypothetical scenario.

Guided Path
1
State the second proof
The Maturidis argue: if truth and falsehood are equal in achieving a purpose (e.g., saving a prisoner), reason prefers truth for its goodness and avoids falsehood for its evil. This shows that goodness and evil are rationally known.
The example of saving a prisoner by truth or falsehood is used.
2
Understand the Ash'ari objection
The Ash'aris object that such equality is impossible in reality because truth and falsehood have different consequences and attributes.
They argue that the scenario is a false assumption, so it cannot prove anything.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author concedes that equality is impossible, but argues that even in a hypothetical scenario, reason's preference for truth shows that truth is recognized as good. The objection does not refute the existence of a rational judgment; it only denies the scenario's actuality.
The response uses the concept of 'impossible assumption' (al-fard al-muḥāl) to show that the proof still works.
4
Identify the underlying usul principle
The proof relies on the principle that reason can discern intrinsic moral qualities (ḥusn and qubḥ dhātiyyān). The preference for truth is seen as evidence of such discernment.
This distinguishes Hanafi usul from Ash'ari, which denies such rational discernment.
5
Evaluate the strength of the proof
Consider whether the proof holds despite the concession that perfect equality is impossible. Does the hypothetical scenario still support the claim?
The proof is not about an actual scenario but about the logical implication that if equality were possible, reason would choose truth. The impossibility of equality does not negate the rational judgment itself.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Rationality of Good and Evil: Maturidi vs Ash'ari
debate over second Maturidi proof and first Ash'ari proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Maturidi proof for the rationality of good and evil, identify the Ash'ari counter-argument, and explain the Maturidi response of committing the lesser evil.

Core Idea
For the Maturidi, good and evil are intrinsic; for the Ash'ari, they are assigned by revelation.

This passage tackles a foundational usul al-fiqh dispute: Can reason independently know good and evil, or must we rely solely on revelation? The Maturidi school (followed by Hanafis) defends the rationalist view, while the Ash'aris argue that moral values are determined by divine law. We will examine one key proof from each side and the Maturidi reply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maturidi second proof
Find the scenario where truth and falsehood equally achieve a goal. Reason chooses truth for its goodness and avoids falsehood for its evil. This shows reason perceives good and evil intrinsically.
The proof assumes a hypothetical equality to isolate rational preference.
2
Examine the author's critique of the proof
The author argues that true equality is impossible because truth and falsehood have different consequences. Thus the choice is based on real differences, not pure rationality. Understand the objection.
This is a subtle point: the proof's premise is unrealistic.
3
State the Ash'ari first proof
If good/evil were intrinsic, they would never be absent. Yet lying (normally evil) becomes obligatory and thus good; truth becomes forbidden and thus evil. So moral values must be assigned by revelation.
The key term 'takhalluf' (absence) is central.
4
Explain the Maturidi response
The Maturidi replies that obligatory lying does not become good intrinsically; rather, it is the lesser of two evils. Lying remains evil in itself (qabīḥ li-dhātih) but is chosen to avoid a greater evil.
This distinguishes intrinsic evil from extrinsic beneficial effect.
5
Assess the thamarah al-khilaf
The dispute affects whether qiyās and istiḥsān can be based on rational considerations. Hanafis rely on rational perception of good/evil in deriving rulings.
This links the theological dispute to practical fiqh methodology.

والدليل الثاني للماتريدية هو قوله (واستدل الخ) وحاصل الدليل على كونهما عقليين أنه إذا استوى الصدق والكذب في حصول مقصود من المقاصد يختار العقل الصدق لحسنه، ويترك الكذب لقبحه كنجاة اسير وخلاصه بالطريقين ( بالصدق والكذب) فعلم أن الحسن و القبح عقليان . وفيه أنه لا استواء في نفس الأمر؛ لأن لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض، فهو تقدير مستحيل فيمتنع الإيثار على ذلك التقدير وأجاب عنه المصنف بقوله : (وفيه انه الخ). وتفصيل الجواب : أن لا استواء بين الصدق والكذب في نفس الأمر، وترجيح الصدق على الكذب في المثال المذكور، لأجل ذلك الفرق النفس الأمرى، لأن لكل منهما لوازم وعوارض غير لوازم وعوارض الآخر، واختلاف اللوازم والعوارض دليل على اختلاف الملزوم والمعروض ففرض الاستواء بين الصدق والكذب هو فرض محال، والمحال (وهو ذلك الفرض) يستلرم محالاً آخر، وهو ترجيح الصدق لأجل الاستواء مع الكذب، مع أنه غير ممكن، إذ في الصدق مطابقة الواقعي والنفس الأمر، وفي الكذب عدم المطابقة وخلاف النفس الأمر، وهذا معنى قول المصنف : فيمنع الإيثار (إيثار الصدق وترجيحه ) على ذلك التقدير ( تقدير استوائه بالكذب). دلائل الأشاعرة الخمسة على كونهما شرعيين - قالوا أولاً : لوكان ذاتياً لم يتخلف، وقد تخلف، فإن الكذب مثلاً يجب العصمة نبي وإنقاذ بري عن سقاك والجواب أن ههنا ارتكاب أقل القبيحين، لا أن الكذب صار حسناً قيل: يرد عليه أنّ هذا الكذب واجب، فيدخل في الحسن اقول : الحسن لغيره لا ينافي القبح لذاته ، وهذا معنى قولهم: الضرورات تبيح المحظورات ، غاية الأمر أنه يلزم القول بأن كلا منهما كما أنه بالذات كذلك بالغير، ولعلهم يلتزمونه ،وبه أمكن لهم التخلص عن النسخ على أنه لا يتم على الجبائية ولا علينا قالوا (الأشاعرة) في إنكار عقلية الحسن و القبح ونفي كونهما لذات الفعل : - أولاً : لو كان كل واحد منهما ذاتيا لذات الفعل) لم يتخلف ولم ينفك الحسن عن الحسن والقبح عن القبيح، لأن ما بالذات يكون باقيا مادامت الذات باقية، وقد وجد التخلف (الانفكاك) في بعض المواضع، فإن الكذب لإنقاذ برى عن سفاك (قتال) واجب، فيكون حسنا)، والصدق حرام فيكون (قبيحا فهنا وجد الكذب بدون القبح، والصدق بدون الحسن، ولوكانا ذاتيين لم يكونا كذلك ( لم ينفكا) . (وأما مثال وجوب الكذب لعصمة ( حفظ ) نبي فلا جدوى فيه، ولا يناسب، فإن سلسلة الأنبياء، قد انقطعت بعد وفاة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، فلا يوجد في الأرض نبي، حتى وجب الكذب لأجل عمصته وحفظه عن السفاك). والجواب من جانب الماتريدية والمعتزلة) أن ههنا في محل وجوب الكذب) ارتكاب أقل القبيحين، وهو الكذب في مقابلة ترك قتل البرئ، لا أن الكذب صار
And the second proof of the Maturidis is his statement (and he argued etc.) The summary of the proof on them being rational is that if truth and falsehood are equal in achieving a goal, reason chooses truth for its goodness and leaves falsehood for its evil, like saving a captive and his release by both paths (truth and falsehood). So it is known that good and evil are rational. But there is no equality in reality; because each has necessary consequences and accidents, so it is an impossible estimation, and preference is prevented on that estimation. And the author answered with his statement (and in it is that etc.). And the detailed answer: that there is no equality between truth and falsehood in reality, and the preference of truth over falsehood in the mentioned example is due to that real difference, because each has necessary consequences and accidents different from the other's, and the difference in consequences and accidents indicates a difference in the subject and object. So assuming equality between truth and falsehood is an impossible assumption, and the impossible (that assumption) entails another impossible, which is preferring truth because of equality with falsehood, while that is not possible, for in truth there is conformity with reality and the real, and in falsehood there is lack of conformity and opposition to reality. And this is the meaning of the author's statement: 'so preference is prevented (preference of truth and its prioritization) on that estimation (the estimation of its equality with falsehood).' The five proofs of the Ash'aris on them being religious - they said first: If it were intrinsic it would not be absent, but it has been absent. For example, lying is obligatory to protect a prophet and save an innocent from a killer. The answer: here there is committing the lesser of the two evils, not that lying becomes good. It was said: it is objected that this lying is obligatory, thus it enters into good. I say: good for other does not negate evil in itself, and this is the meaning of their statement: necessities permit prohibitions. The only point is that it necessitates saying that each of them is both intrinsic and extrinsic, and perhaps they accept it, and thereby they can be free from abrogation, but it is not valid for the Jabaiyya nor for us. The Ash'aris said, in denying the rationality of good and evil and negating them being intrinsic to the act: First: If each were intrinsic to the act, it would not be absent and good would not separate from good and evil from evil, because what is intrinsic remains as long as the essence remains, but absence (separation) has occurred in some places. For lying to save an innocent from a killer (murderer) is obligatory, so it becomes good, and truth becomes forbidden so it becomes evil. Here lying was found without evil, and truth without good. If they were intrinsic they would not be thus (would not separate). (As for the example of obligatory lying to protect (preserve) a prophet, there is no benefit in it, nor is it appropriate, because the chain of prophets has ended after the death of Muhammad, peace be upon him, so there is no prophet on earth, so that lying would be obligatory for his protection and preservation from the killer). The answer from the side of the Maturidis and Mu'tazila: here, in the place of obligatory lying, there is committing the lesser of the two evils, which is lying in exchange for leaving the killing of the innocent, not that lying becomes good.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents a classical theological dispute between the Maturidi (Hanafi) school and the Ash'ari school regarding the basis of good (husn) and evil (qubh). The Maturidis, together with the Mu'tazila, hold that goodness and evil are rational (ʿaqliyyān) — that is, reason can independently perceive them, and they are intrinsic to actions. The Ash'aris maintain that they are religious (sharʿiyyān) — determined solely by divine revelation. The author, a Maturidi, defends the rationalist position.

The passage begins with the second Maturidi proof: if truth and falsehood are equal in achieving a goal (e.g., saving a captive), reason prefers truth due to its inherent goodness — showing that reason recognizes good and evil intrinsically. The author (the commentator) responds by arguing that true equality between truth and falsehood is impossible in reality because each has distinct consequences and properties. Hence, the choice is based on real differences, not pure rationality.

Then the passage presents the first Ash'ari proof: if good and evil were intrinsic, they would never be absent from an action. Yet we find instances where lying, normally evil, becomes obligatory (e.g., to save an innocent life) and thus good, while truth becomes forbidden and thus evil. Therefore, good and evil cannot be intrinsic but are assigned by revelation.

The Maturidi answer to this is that in such a case, we are not saying lying becomes good in itself. Rather, we commit the lesser of two evils: lying is still evil in itself (qabīḥ li-dhātih), but it is chosen because it prevents a greater evil (killing an innocent). This is the concept of 'good for other' (ḥasan li-ghayrih) not negating 'evil in itself'. The author also notes that the example of lying to protect a prophet is irrelevant since there are no prophets after Muhammad (PBUH).

The author concludes by acknowledging that this view implies that an act can be both intrinsically evil and extrinsically good, which the Ash'aris might accept, but he notes it does not apply to the Jabaiyya (a Mu'tazili subgroup) nor to his own school (Maturidi).

Key Terms
الحسن و القبح (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ): Goodness and evil; moral qualities attributed to actions. The dispute is whether they are intrinsic to actions (rational) or assigned by revelation (religious).
عقلي (ʿaqlī): Rational — perceived by reason independently of revelation.
شرعي (sharʿī): Religious — determined by divine law (sharʿ), not reason.
ذاتي (dhātī): Intrinsic — belonging to the essence of the act itself.
التخلف (al-takhalluf): Non-occurrence or absence; used in Ash'ari argument: if good/evil were intrinsic, they would never be absent from actions, but they are absent in cases like obligatory lying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi response to the Ash'ari argument, what is the status of obligatory lying to save an innocent?
A) Lying becomes good in itself (ḥasan li-dhātih).
B) Lying remains evil in itself but is the lesser of two evils.
C) Lying becomes religiously neutral (mubāḥ).
D) Lying is not considered evil at all.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view maintains that good and evil are intrinsic to actions but can be overridden by extrinsic considerations, allowing for rational moral reasoning.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Ashʿarī-Māturīdī Dispute on Intrinsic Good and Evil
Presents five Ashʿarī proofs that ḥusn/qubḥ are Sharʿī, with Māturīdī rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ashʿarī proof that good and evil are not intrinsic and evaluate the Māturīdī response using the distinction between qabīḥ li-dhātihi and ḥasan li-ghayrihi.

Core Idea
Acts can be evil in themselves but good for another—intrinsic morality remains unchanged.

The nature of moral good and evil (ḥusn and qubḥ) is a foundational dispute in uṣūl al-fiqh. The Ashʿarī school argues that these are purely determined by revelation (Sharʿī), while the Māturīdī school (adopted by Ḥanafīs) holds that some acts are intrinsically good or evil. This lesson examines the first Ashʿarī proof—the alleged detachment of moral qualities from acts in exceptional cases—and the Māturīdī rebuttal through the concepts of bi al-dhāt (by essence) and bi al-ghayr (by another). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing rulings that involve necessity, such as lying to save a life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine what the Ashʿarīs are denying: that ḥusn and qubḥ are intrinsic (dhātī) to acts. They assert these are purely Sharʿī.
2
Analyze the Ashʿarī proof
The Ashʿarīs argue: if moral qualities were intrinsic, they would never detach. Yet we find cases like lying to save a life, which becomes obligatory (good), and truthfulness that becomes forbidden (evil). Thus, they claim, good and evil cannot be intrinsic.
This is a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) based on observed exceptions.
3
Understand the Māturīdī counter-argument
The Māturīdīs respond by distinguishing between the act's intrinsic nature and its effect. The act remains qabīḥ li-dhātihi (evil in itself) but becomes ḥasan li-ghayrihi (good for another) due to the beneficial outcome. Thus, intrinsic evil does not disappear.
Key: ḥasan li-ghayrihi does not negate qabīḥ li-dhātihi.
4
Examine the objection and rebuttal
An objection claims that since the lying is obligatory (wājib), it must be good, so its intrinsic evil must have vanished. The author (al-Badakhshānī) rebuts: 'good for another' does not negate 'evil in itself'; the act is still evil intrinsically but permitted due to necessity.
This mirrors the juristic maxim: 'Necessities permit prohibitions.'
5
Connect to the issue of abrogation (naskh)
The passage explains that the division into bi al-dhāt and bi al-ghayr allows the Māturīdīs to avoid the objection that abrogation would be impossible if moral qualities were only intrinsic. Those aspects that are bi al-ghayr can change, resolving the problem.
Ashʿarīs also avoid this problem by denying intrinsic values entirely.

(وأما مثال وجوب الكذب لعصمة ( حفظ ) نبي فلا جدوى فيه، ولا يناسب، فإن سلسلة الأنبياء، قد انقطعت بعد وفاة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، فلا يوجد في الأرض نبي، حتى وجب الكذب لأجل عمصته وحفظه عن السفاك).

الإشكال على هذا الجواب : وأورد على هذا الجواب إشكال، وهو أن هذا الكذب الكذب لإنقاذ برئ) واجب فيدخل في الحسن، لأن كل واجب يكون حسنًا، فهذا الواجب أيضًا حسن، فصار الكذب حسنًا، وزال عنه القبح، فعلم أنه (القبح) ليس لذات الكذب. ( فأجاب عنه المصنف من جانب الماتريدية وقال أقول: الحسن لغيره لا ينافي القبح لذاته، وحاصل كلامه أن الكذب في هذا المثال قبيح لذاته كما كان، ولكن صار حسنًا لأجل غيره، وهو إنقاذ البرئ عن السفاك، فالحسن لغيره لا ينافي القبح لذاته، فلم يلزم التخلف والانفكاك ، حتى يلزم كون قبح الكذب أو صدق الحسن غير ذاتي. وهذا (كون القبيح لذاته يصير حسناً لغيره (لأجل الضرورة) معنى قول الفقهاء : الضرورات تبيح ( وتجعل ) المحظورات حسنة " التي تكون قبيحة وممنوعة . ونهاية الأمر والتغير الذي يلزم من هذا التأويل، هو أنه يلزم القول بأن كلاً من الحسن والقبح قديكون بالذات وقديكون بالغير، فمن الممكن أن يكون فعل قبيحا بالذات وحسنًا بالغير، ويكون فعل حسنًا بالذات وقبيحا لأجل غيره، كالكذب النافع والصدق الضار في المثال المذكور، ولعل الماتريدية يلتزمون به أي يعتقدون تقسيم الحسن والقبيح إلى هذاين النوعين (بالذات وبالغير) ويعدونه لازما عليهم . ثم قال في الجواب عن إشكال النسخ : و به بهذا التقسيم إلى الحسن بالذات و بالغير، والقبيح كذلك) أمكن لهم (للماتريدية) الخلاص عن لزوم الإشكال بالنسخ إذا كانا ذاتيين فقط، وأما في صورة كونهما لذاته ولغيره فلا يلزم الإشكال، لأن الحسن أو القبيح لغيره يقبل النسخ . وقال في الجواب الثاني عنه : على أن هذا الإشكال لا يتم على الجبائية، لأنهم يقولون: إنما يكونان (الحسن و القبح لوجوه اعتبارية ، لالذات الفعل، حتى يرد عليهم النسخ. وكذا لايتم إشكال النسخ علينا (الماتريدية) لأنا نقول بأعم من ذات الفعل
Not possible, for in truth there is conformity to reality and the thing-in-itself, and in falsehood there is non-conformity and opposition to the thing-in-itself. This is the meaning of the author's statement: 'so it prevents preferring (preferring truth and giving it precedence) over that estimation (the estimation of its equality with falsehood).' The five proofs of the Ash'aris that they (ḥusn and qubḥ) are Sharʿī: They said first: If they were intrinsic (dhātī), they would not be detached, but they have been detached. For example, lying is obligatory for the protection of a prophet and saving an innocent from a killer. The answer is that here there is committing the lesser of the two evils, not that lying has become good. It was said: It is objected to this that this lying is obligatory, so it enters into good. I say: Good for another (ḥasan li-ghayrihi) does not negate evil in itself (qabīḥ li-dhātihi). This is the meaning of their statement: Necessities permit prohibitions. The upshot is that it necessitates saying that each of them, just as it is by essence, so too by other, and perhaps they commit to it, and by it they were able to escape from abrogation, but it is not valid against the Jabā'iyya nor against us. They (the Ash'aris) said in denying the rationality of good and evil and negating that they are intrinsic to the act: First: If each of them were intrinsic to the essence of the act, they would not detach and good would not separate from good and evil from evil, because what is intrinsic remains as long as the essence remains, yet detachment (separation) has occurred in some places. For lying to save an innocent from a killer is obligatory, so it becomes good, and truthfulness becomes forbidden, so it becomes evil. So here lying is found without evil, and truthfulness without good. If they were intrinsic, they would not be so (they would not separate). (As for the example of the obligation of lying for the protection (preservation) of a prophet, there is no benefit in it, nor is it appropriate, for the chain of prophets has been cut off after the death of Muḥammad صلى الله عليه وسلم, so there is no prophet on earth until lying becomes obligatory for his protection and preservation from a killer.) The answer from the side of the Māturīdiyya and Mu'tazila: Here, in the place where lying is obligatory, there is committing the lesser of the two evils, namely lying in exchange for abandoning killing the innocent, not that lying has become good and its evil has disappeared, nor that truthfulness has become forbidden so its goodness disappeared. Thus, detachment and separation do not occur, and they (good and evil) remain as they were. The objection to this answer: An objection was raised against this answer, namely that this lying (lying to save an innocent) is obligatory, so it enters into good, because every obligatory is good. So this obligatory is also good, so lying became good, and its evil disappeared. So it is known that evil is not intrinsic to lying. The author answered from the side of the Māturīdiyya, saying: I say: Good for another does not negate evil in itself. The summary of his statement is that lying in this example is evil in itself as it was, but it became good for another, namely saving the innocent from the killer. So good for another does not negate evil in itself. Thus, detachment and separation do not occur such that it necessitates that the evil of lying or the good of truthfulness is not intrinsic. This (the fact that what is evil in itself becomes good for another (due to necessity)) is the meaning of the jurists' statement: 'Necessities permit (make) prohibitions good' which are evil and forbidden. The end result and the change that follows from this interpretation is that it necessitates the statement that each of good and evil may be by essence and may be by another, so it is possible for an act to be evil in itself and good for another, and for an act to be good in itself and evil for another, like beneficial lying and harmful truthfulness in the mentioned example. Perhaps the Māturīdiyya commit to this, i.e., they believe in the division of good and evil into these two types (by essence and by another) and consider it necessary for them. Then he said in answering the objection of abrogation: And by this division into good by essence and by another, and evil likewise, they (the Māturīdiyya) were able to escape from the necessity of the objection of abrogation if they were only by essence. But in the case of them being by essence and by another, the objection does not arise, because good or evil for another is subject to abrogation. And he said in the second answer about it: Moreover, this objection is not valid against the Jabā'iyya, because they say that they (good and evil) are only due to considerational aspects, not intrinsic to the act, so that abrogation would be an objection against them. Likewise, the objection of abrogation is not valid against us (the Māturīdiyya) because we say with something more general than the essence of the act.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute (ikhtilāf) between the Ashʿarī school and the Māturīdī (alongside Muʿtazila) on the nature of ḥusn (good) and qubḥ (evil). The Ashʿarīs argue that these are Sharʿī (revealed) categories, not intrinsic (dhātī) to acts. They present a rational proof: if good and evil were intrinsic, they would never separate from their acts. Yet we find cases where lying (normally evil) becomes obligatory and good (e.g., to save an innocent life), and truthfulness (normally good) becomes forbidden and evil. This proves that good and evil are not intrinsic but determined by divine command. The Māturīdīs respond by distinguishing between 'good/evil in itself' (bi al-dhāt) and 'good/evil for another' (li al-ghayr). In the case of lying to save a life, the act remains evil in itself but becomes good for another (the beneficial outcome). Thus, there is no detachment; the intrinsic quality remains, but an accidental goodness appears. The objector asks: if the lying is obligatory, it must be good, so why still say it is evil in itself? The author (al-Badakhshānī) explains that ḥasan li-ghayrihi (good for another) does not negate qabīḥ li-dhātihi (evil in itself). This is the juristic principle 'necessities permit prohibitions' — the act remains prohibited in itself but is permitted due to necessity. The passage also addresses the issue of naskh (abrogation): if good and evil were only intrinsic, abrogation would be impossible because what is intrinsic cannot change. But by accepting that good and evil can also be extrinsic (li al-ghayr), abrogation becomes possible for those aspects. The author notes that the Jabā'iyya (a group within Muʿtazila) avoid this objection entirely because they deny intrinsic moral values altogether, considering them subjective. The Māturīdīs, however, maintain a middle position: acts have both intrinsic and extrinsic moral qualities.

Key Terms
ذاتي (dhātī): Intrinsic to the act itself, inseparable from its essence.
حسن لغيره (ḥasan li-ghayrihi): Good for another reason or end, not good in itself.
قبيح لذاته (qabīḥ li-dhātihi): Evil in itself due to its own nature.
شرعي (Sharʿī): Determined by divine revelation rather than reason or intrinsic nature.
بالذات (bi al-dhāt): By essence; intrinsic.
بالغير (bi al-ghayr): By another; extrinsic.
الضرورات تبيح المحظورات (al-ḍarūrāt tubīḥ al-maḥẓūrāt): Necessities permit prohibitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī response, why does lying to save an innocent life not prove that good and evil are purely Sharʿī?
A) Because lying is always evil and cannot become good under any circumstance.
B) Because the act is evil in itself but good for another, so the intrinsic quality remains.
C) Because the Ashʿarī argument is based on a false premise.
D) Because the example is invalid since there are no prophets today.

Key Takeaway
The Māturīdī distinction between qabīḥ li-dhātihi and ḥasan li-ghayrihi allows for acts to retain intrinsic moral status while being permissible under necessity, reconciling exceptions with a stable moral framework.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Maturidi Distinction Between Intrinsic and Extrinsic Goodness/Badness
Defense of dual nature of husn/qubh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Maturidi position on al-husn wa al-qubh and defend it against the Ash'ari objection using the example of lying to save an innocent.

Core Idea
An act can be intrinsically bad yet extrinsically good due to its purpose.

In this passage, the author defends the Maturidi (Hanafi) view that goodness and badness of acts can be both intrinsic (dhati) and extrinsic (ghairi). The Ash'ari school argues that these qualities are purely legal (shar'i) and not intrinsic, raising paradoxes like the oath 'I will surely lie tomorrow.' The Maturidi response distinguishes between the act's inherent nature and its outcome or purpose, allowing for acts like lying to save an innocent to be intrinsically bad but extrinsically good. This distinction also resolves issues about abrogation (naskh).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Positions
State the Maturidi (Hanafi) and Ash'ari positions on whether goodness and badness are intrinsic to acts or only legal (shar'i). The Maturidi holds they can be both intrinsic and extrinsic; the Ash'ari holds they are only legal.
2
Understand the Objection: Lying to Save an Innocent
The objector claims that if lying to save an innocent is wajib (obligatory), then it must be good. But if lying is intrinsically bad, how can it become good? This sets up the need for a distinction.
This objection challenges the coherence of intrinsic badness.
3
Apply the Maturidi Distinction
Analyze how the author uses the phrase 'الحسن لغيره لا ينافي القبح لذاته' (goodness for another does not contradict badness in itself). The act remains intrinsically bad (qabih bi-dhatih) but becomes good due to its purpose (hasan bi-ghayrih).
This is the core of the Maturidi response.
4
Evaluate the Ash'ari Oath Paradox
The Ash'aris argue that if truth is intrinsically good and lying intrinsically bad, then the oath 'I will surely lie tomorrow' would force the act to be both good and bad, a contradiction. The author rebuts by denying that the necessary (the act) always shares the ruling of its necessitating (the oath).
Use the analogy of jihad and extreme cold: the act's consequence may reverse its moral valence.
5
Connect to Naskh (Abrogation)
Explain why this distinction matters for the possibility of abrogation. If goodness/badness were only intrinsic, abrogation would change the nature of acts. Since they can be extrinsic, naskh can apply to the extrinsic aspect without contradiction.
This practical implication shows the usul importance of the debate.
