( وقالت الأشاعرة) ثانيا : في الدليل على عدم كونهما لذات الفعل، بل هما شرعيان لو كان كل واحد منهما ذاتيا لاجتمع النقيضان في مثل قول القائل : لأكذبن غدًا، فإن صدقه بأن قال الحالف في الغد قولاً صادقا، يستلزم الكذب التكلم بكلام كاذب وبالعكس وكذبه بأن يتكلم غذا بكلام كاذب يستلزم صدقه، وفي الكذب لاحسن، وفي الصدق حسن، فاجتمع النقيضان، وللملزوم (الحسن والقبيح) حكم اللازم (الحسن والقبيح) فاجتمع الحسن الذي ليس بقبيح والقبيح الذي ليس بحسن وهما نقيضان . أو الملزوم التكلم بكلام كاذب غدًا ولازمه الصدق أو عدم التكلم بذلك الكلام، ولازمه عد الوفاء بحلفه، وفيه كذب في كلامه، فاستلزم كل واحد نقيضه ثم أجاب المصنف عن دليلهم الثاني، وقال : وربما يمنع ذلك (أي كون الملزوم في حكم اللازم كما أن الجهاد في نفسه قتل النفوس وتخريب البيوت وإهلاك الأموال، وهو ملزوم ، وفي لازمه ( و هو إعلاء كلمة الله وإزالة الشرك ورفع الظلم وتنفيذ أحكام الله في أرضه خير كثير، أعظم من جميع الخيرات والحسنات . ثم أشار المصنف إلى دليل الرد وقال: ألا ترى أن المفضي (الموصل) إلى الشر لا يكون شراً بالذات، بل بالعرض واللزوم، مثل البرد الشديد فإنه يفضي إلى هلاك الثمار وضياعها، وليس البرد شراً في نفسه، بل قديكون خيراً من جانب آخر قد تعلمه
It became good and its badness departed, and truth became forbidden so its goodness departed, so neither delay nor detachment occurred, and they (goodness and badness) remain as they were. An objection was raised to this answer: that this lie (to save an innocent person) is obligatory, thus it enters into goodness, because every obligatory thing is good. So this obligatory act is also good, so the lie became good and its badness departed, and it became known that badness is not intrinsic to lying. So the author answered from the Maturidi side saying: 'I say: goodness for another does not contradict badness in itself.' The summary of his words is that lying in this example is bad in itself as it was, but it became good for the sake of another, namely saving the innocent from the oppressor. So goodness for another does not contradict badness in itself, so no delay or detachment occurs that would necessitate that the badness of lying or the goodness of truth is non-intrinsic. This (that the intrinsically bad becomes good for another due to necessity) is the meaning of the jurists' statement: 'Necessities permit (and make) prohibitions good' which are bad and prohibited. The end result and the change necessitated by this interpretation is that one must say that each of goodness and badness can be intrinsic and can be extrinsic. So it is possible for an act to be bad intrinsically and good extrinsically, and for an act to be good intrinsically and bad for the sake of another, like beneficial lying and harmful truth in the mentioned example. Perhaps the Maturidis adhere to this, i.e., they believe in dividing goodness and badness into these two types (intrinsic and extrinsic) and consider it necessary for them. Then he said in response to the objection of abrogation: 'And by this division into intrinsic and extrinsic goodness and likewise badness, the Maturidis can escape the objection of abrogation if they were only intrinsic. But in the case of being both intrinsic and for another, the objection does not arise, because goodness or badness for another is subject to abrogation.' And he said in the second response: 'Moreover, this objection does not apply to the Jabaiyyah, because they say that goodness and badness are only based on considerations, not on the essence of the act, so that abrogation would apply to them. Nor does the objection of abrogation apply to us (Maturidis), because we speak of something more general than the essence of the act and its real and attributed qualities. Rather, that objection applies only to the earlier Mu'tazilites who claim only intrinsic goodness and badness.' Secondly, if it were intrinsic, then two opposites would meet in something like 'I will surely lie tomorrow.' For if he is truthful, it necessitates lying, and vice versa, and the necessary has the ruling of the necessitating. But perhaps that is prevented. Do you not see that what leads to evil is not evil in itself? The teacher said in al-Isharat: 'Evil enters into capacity by accident.' I say: This guides you to the aforementioned adherence, so understand. 2 - (And the Ash'aris said) secondly: In the proof that they are not intrinsic to the act, rather they are legal. If each of them were intrinsic, then two opposites would meet in something like one's saying: 'I will surely lie tomorrow.' For if he is truthful by uttering a truthful statement tomorrow, it necessitates lying (uttering a false statement), and vice versa. And in lying there is no goodness, and in truth there is goodness, so the two opposites meet, and the necessary (goodness and badness) has the ruling of the necessitating (goodness and badness), so the goodness that is not bad and the badness that is not good meet, and they are opposites. Or the necessitating is uttering a false statement tomorrow, and its necessary is truth or not uttering that statement, and its necessary is failing to fulfill his oath, and in that there is a lie in his speech. So each necessitates its opposite. Then the author answered their second proof and said: 'Perhaps that is prevented' (i.e., the necessary having the ruling of the necessitating). Just as jihad in itself is killing souls, destroying houses, and ruining wealth, and it is a necessitating, and in its necessary (which is exalting the word of Allah, removing shirk, lifting oppression, and implementing Allah's laws on His earth) there is much good, greater than all good deeds. Then the author pointed to a proof of the rebuttal and said: Do you not see that what leads (conveys) to evil is not evil in itself, but accidentally and by concomitance, like extreme cold, for it leads to the destruction and loss of fruits, yet cold is not evil in itself, but may be good from another aspect that you may know.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a theological and legal dispute (ikhtilaf) between the Maturidi (Hanafi) school and the Ash'ari school concerning the nature of al-husn wa al-qubh (goodness and badness). The Maturidi position, defended by the author, holds that these qualities can be both intrinsic (dhati) and extrinsic (ghairi). The discussion arises from an earlier argument about a lie told to save an innocent person. The objector claims that if the lie is obligatory, then it must be good, contradicting its intrinsic badness. The author replies by distinguishing between intrinsic and acquired goodness: the lie remains intrinsically bad but becomes good due to its noble purpose (extrinsic goodness). This resolves the paradox and aligns with the juristic maxim 'necessity permits prohibitions.' The author then addresses the problem of naskh (abrogation). If goodness and badness were purely intrinsic, abrogation would be problematic; but because they can be extrinsic, abrogation is possible for the extrinsic aspect. The Ash'aris present a second argument based on the oath 'I will surely lie tomorrow.' If lying is intrinsically bad and truth intrinsically good, then the oath would entail a contradictory mix of the two. The author rebuts by denying that the necessary always shares the ruling of its necessitating. He uses the example of jihad: the act of killing is intrinsically bad, but its higher purpose (exalting Allah's word) makes it good overall. Similarly, extreme cold causes harm but is not evil in itself; it may be beneficial from another perspective. Thus, the Maturidi view that goodness and badness can be both intrinsic and extrinsic is established, and the Ash'ari objections are answered.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): Goodness of an act; can be intrinsic (dhati) or extrinsic (ghairi).
القبح (al-qubh): Badness of an act; can be intrinsic or extrinsic.
بالذات (bi-dhatih): Intrinsic; inherent to the act itself regardless of context.
بالغير (bi-ghayrih): Extrinsic; acquired from external factors like purpose or necessity.
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling; possible for extrinsic goodness/badness but not intrinsic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main Ash'ari argument against intrinsic goodness/badness presented in the passage?
A) That the Qur'an requires all goodness to be from revelation.
B) The oath 'I will surely lie tomorrow' would require both goodness and badness in the same act if they were intrinsic.
C) That scholars have consensus on goodness being only legal.
D) That abrogation is impossible if goodness is intrinsic.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic goodness/badness resolves apparent paradoxes and allows for a coherent theory of moral qualities that supports Hanafi usul on necessity and abrogation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Resolving the Ash'ari Liar's Paradox: Intrinsic and Accidental Good and Evil
Ash'ari second argument using liar's paradox and Hanafi response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the malzūm-lāzim relationship in the Ash'ari liar's paradox and apply the Hanafi distinction between intrinsic and accidental ethical qualities to resolve the contradiction.

Core Idea
An action can be intrinsically good and accidentally evil, and vice versa—resolving the Ash'ari liar's paradox.

The Ash'aris argued that if good and evil were intrinsic to actions, then a person swearing to lie tomorrow would necessitate a contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument and the Hanafi-inspired response: actions can have both intrinsic and accidental ethical qualities, so no actual contradiction occurs. Understanding this distinction is key to the usul al-fiqh debate on ḥusn and qubḥ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari malzūm and lāzim
In the liar's paradox, when someone swears to lie tomorrow, the malzūm (the act) is either truthfulness or lying, and its lāzim (necessary consequence) is the opposite. Determine which is which in each scenario.
The Ash'ari argument assumes that the malzūm carries the ruling of the lāzim.
2
Understand the paradox
If the person tells the truth, the malzūm is truthfulness (good) but the lāzim is lying (evil) because he swore to lie. If he lies, the malzūm is lying (evil) and the lāzim is truthfulness (good). Thus good and evil appear to coexist, a contradiction if they are intrinsic.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author denies that the malzūm always has the ruling of the lāzim. He gives examples of jihad (destruction as malzūm, elevation of Allah's word as lāzim) and severe cold (harmful as malzūm, but not intrinsically evil). The ruling of the lāzim applies accidentally, not intrinsically.
This shows that an action can be good intrinsically and evil accidentally, or vice versa.
4
Apply the principle to the liar's paradox
Truthfulness is good intrinsically but evil accidentally (because it leads to lying via the oath). Lying is evil intrinsically but good accidentally (if it fulfills the oath). No actual contradiction; just different aspects.
Thus the paradox is resolved without denying intrinsic values.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الأشاعرة ثانيا : في الدليل على عدم كونهما لذات الفعل، بل هما شرعيان لو كان كل واحد منهما ذاتيا لاجتمع النقيضان في مثل قول القائل : لأكذبن غدًا، فإن صدقه بأن قال الحالف في الغد قولاً صادقا، يستلزم الكذب التكلم بكلام كاذب وبالعكس وكذبه بأن يتكلم غذا بكلام كاذب يستلزم صدقه، وفي الكذب لاحسن، وفي الصدق حسن، فاجتمع النقيضان، وللملزوم (الحسن والقبيح) حكم اللازم (الحسن والقبيح) فاجتمع الحسن الذي ليس بقبيح والقبيح الذي ليس بحسن وهما نقيضان . أو الملزوم التكلم بكلام كاذب غدًا ولازمه الصدق أو عدم التكلم بذلك الكلام، ولازمه عد الوفاء بحلفه، وفيه كذب في كلامه، فاستلزم كل واحد نقيضه ثم أجاب المصنف عن دليلهم الثاني، وقال : وربما يمنع ذلك (أي كون الملزوم في حكم اللازم كما أن الجهاد في نفسه قتل النفوس وتخريب البيوت وإهلاك الأموال، وهو ملزوم ، وفي لازمه (و هو إعلاء كلمة الله وإزالة الشرك ورفع الظلم وتنفيذ أحكام الله في أرضه خير كثير، أعظم من جميع الخيرات والحسنات . ثم أشار المصنف إلى دليل الرد وقال: ألا ترى أن المفضي (الموصل) إلى الشر لا يكون شراً بالذات، بل بالعرض واللزوم، مثل البرد الشديد فإنه يفضي إلى هلاك الثمار وضياعها، وليس البرد شراً في نفسه، بل قديكون خيراً من جانب آخر قد تعلمه وقد لا نعلمه، وعلى هذا لا يخلوا شر عن خير إلا ما شاء الله ثم نقل قول الشيخ (الفلسفي الباطنى) في إشاراته “ (الذي شرحه الطوسى والرازي) فإنه قال فيه : الشر داخل في القدر (في تقدير الله للخلق والإيجاد بالعرض. و معنى كلام ابن سيناء أن الله يخلق الخير بالذات والشر بالعرض واللزوم، مع أنه خالق كل شئ قصداً وبالذات وهو على كل شيئ وكيل . والحق أن يقال إن الشرور أمور إضافية، تكون شرا بالنسبة إلى أحد، أو شيئ وتكون خيرا بالنسبة إلى آخر، فلايكون كل ملزوم في حكم لازمه . فايد جوابه بقول الشيخ وقال: أقول هذا يرشدك (أي قول الشيخ يرشدك) إلى الالتزام المذكور سابقا، من أنهما كما يكونان بالذات يكونان بالعرض أيضًا، فحكم اللازم وإن لم يكن للملزوم بالذات في كل موضع لكنه يكون بالعرض، فهذا الصدق حسن بالذت وقبيح بواسطة لازمه الذي هو الكذب، وكذلك الكذب، قديكون قبيحا بالذات وحسنا باعتبار لازمه الذي هو الصدق . وقوله : "فافهم " : تاكيد لفهم المسألة وإشارة إلى دقتها .
And the Ash'aris said secondly: As evidence against them being [intrinsic] to the act itself, rather they are Shar'i: If each of them were intrinsic, then two opposites would combine in the example of someone saying: 'I will definitely lie tomorrow.' For if he is truthful (i.e., the swearer says a truthful statement tomorrow), it necessitates lying (speaking a false statement), and vice versa. If he is false (i.e., he speaks falsely tomorrow), it necessitates truthfulness. And in lying there is no good, and in truthfulness there is good, so the two opposites combine. And for the concomitant (al-malzum) [good and evil] there is the ruling of the necessary (al-lazim) [good and evil], so good that is not evil and evil that is not good combine, and they are opposites. Or the concomitant is speaking a false statement tomorrow, and its necessary is truthfulness or not speaking that statement, and its necessary is failure to fulfill his oath, and there is lying in his speech, so each one necessitates its opposite. Then the author responded to their second proof and said: 'And perhaps that is prevented (i.e., the concomitant having the ruling of the necessary, as jihad itself is killing souls, destroying houses, and wasting wealth, and it is a concomitant, and in its necessary (which is elevating the word of Allah, removing shirk, lifting oppression, and implementing Allah's rulings in His land) there is much good, greater than all goods and virtues.' Then the author pointed to the proof of the refutation and said: 'Do you not see that that which leads to evil is not evil intrinsically, but rather accidentally and by necessity (billuzum), like severe cold: it leads to the destruction and waste of fruits, but cold is not evil in itself; rather, it may be good from another aspect which we may know or not know. Based on this, no evil is devoid of good except what Allah wills.' Then he quoted the saying of the Shaykh (the philosophical esoteric) in his 'Isharat' (which al-Tusi and al-Razi explained), for he said in it: 'Evil is included in [divine] decree (in Allah's estimation of creation and bringing into existence) accidentally.' And the meaning of Ibn Sina's statement is that Allah creates good intrinsically and evil accidentally and by necessity, while He is the creator of everything intentionally and intrinsically, and He is over all things a guardian. And the truth is that evils are relative matters; they are evil in relation to one person or thing and good in relation to another. Thus, not every concomitant has the ruling of its necessary. So he strengthened his response with the saying of the Shaykh and said: 'I say: This (i.e., the saying of the Shaykh) guides you to the commitment mentioned previously: that they (good and evil), just as they are intrinsic, they also are accidental. So the ruling of the necessary, even if it is not for the concomitant intrinsically in every case, is nevertheless accidental. So this truthfulness is good intrinsically and evil due to its necessary which is lying. Similarly, lying may be evil intrinsically and good in consideration of its necessary which is truthfulness.' And his statement 'fafham' indicates emphasis on understanding the issue and a reference to its subtlety.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a detailed presentation of the second Ash'ari proof against the Mu'tazili claim that good and evil are intrinsic to actions (ḥusn and qubḥ dhātiyyān). The Ash'aris argue that if good and evil were intrinsic, then in a case where a person swears 'I will definitely lie tomorrow,' a contradiction would arise: if he then tells a lie, it is evil; but his oath to lie forces him to lie, so lying becomes necessary, and if he tells the truth, he breaks his oath, which is also evil? The argument uses the concept of lāzim (necessary consequence) and malzūm (the thing on which it is consequent). The response by the author (al-muṣannif) denies that the malzūm always has the ruling of the lāzim. He gives jihad as an example: jihad involves killing and destruction (malzūm), but its consequence (lāzim) is great good. Similarly, severe cold causes destruction but is not intrinsically evil; it might be good in another respect. The author then quotes Ibn Sina to support the idea that evil is accidental, not intrinsic, and concludes that good and evil can be both intrinsic and accidental depending on the aspect. This refutes the Ash'ari argument by showing that the same action can have both intrinsic and accidental ethical values, so no actual contradiction arises.

Key Terms
حسن وقبح (ḥusn wa qubḥ): Ethical qualities of good and evil attributed to actions.
ذاتي (dhātī): Intrinsic; an attribute belonging to the essence of an action.
شرعي (shar‘ī): Determined by divine law (Sharī‘ah), not by reason alone.
عرضي (‘araḍī): Accidental; a quality that attaches to an action externally, e.g., due to its consequences.
لزوم (luzūm): Necessary consequence; the relationship between a malzūm (thing) and its lāzim (necessary outcome).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The malzūm always has the ruling of the lāzim.
B) The malzūm does not always have the ruling of the lāzim.
C) Good and evil are entirely accidental.
D) Liar's paradox proves intrinsic values.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari liar's paradox is resolved by recognizing that actions can have both intrinsic and accidental ethical qualities, so no contradiction arises.

Ash'ari Argument: Compulsion Precludes Intrinsic Ethics
Ash'ari third argument against intrinsic ethical values using compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Ash'ari argument from compulsion (iḍṭirār) against intrinsic ethical values and evaluate its logical structure.

Core Idea
If an action is compelled, it cannot be freely chosen, thus not intrinsically good or evil.

This lesson examines a key Ash'ari argument used in uṣūl al-fiqh to deny that actions have intrinsic moral value knowable by reason. Understanding this argument is essential for grasping the Māturīdī-Hanafi response and the broader debate over the basis of Sharīʿah rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
The Ash'aris claim: 'The action of the servant is compelled.' Understand what 'compelled' (iḍṭirārī) means: the action occurs by necessity, not free choice.
Compulsion here is ontological, not merely physical.
2
Identify the major premise (implied)
The text says 'this is the minor premise; the major premise is omitted.' The omitted major premise is: 'A compelled action cannot be intrinsically good or evil rationally.'
This is the link to the ethical conclusion.
3
Analyze the supporting reasoning
Why is the action compelled? Because before existence it is merely possible (mumkin) and requires a preponderant factor (murjīḥ) to exist; giving preponderance without cause is impossible, so the act becomes necessary and thus compelled.
This is based on the principle that for a possible thing to exist, a preponderant factor must tip the balance.
4
Contrast with the Hanafi-Māturīdī position
Hanafis (following Māturīdī theology) affirm human acquisition (kasb) and a degree of free will, so they reject the full compulsion argument. They would respond that actions are acquired, not purely compelled, and thus can be ethically evaluated.
This ikhtilaf has implications for the role of reason in uṣūl al-fiqh.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
The author comments that this argument is 'better and more concise' than what is in al-Mukhtasar. Consider its logical validity and its implications: if accepted, it undermines rational ethics entirely, leaving revelation as the sole source of moral knowledge.
Hanafis would challenge the minor premise or the transition from necessity to compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
-٣- وثالثاً أن فعل العبد اضطراري، فإن الممكن مالم يترجح لم يوجد وترجيح المرجوح محال، فمالم يجب لم يوجد فلا يكون حسنا ولا قبيحاً عقلاً إجماعاً، وهذا أحسن وأخصر مما في "المختصر". -٣- وقالوا ثالثاً : في دليل إنكار كونهما ذاتيين : إن فعل العبد اضطراري، فإن الفعل الممكن مالم يترجح لم يوجد، وترجيح المرجوح محال . فإذا لم يجب لم يوجد، فلا يكون حسنا ولا قبيحاً عقلاً إجماعاً. وحاصل الكلام : أن فعل العبد اضطراري و هذه صغرى والكبرى محذوفة
Thirdly: That the action of the servant is compelled. For the possible act, unless it is given preponderance, it does not exist, and giving preponderance to the unlikely [without cause] is impossible. So unless it is necessitated, it does not exist. Thus, it is not good nor evil rationally, by consensus. And this is better and more concise than what is in al-Mukhtasar. Thirdly: And they said: in the proof of denying that they are intrinsic: The action of the servant is compelled. For the possible act, unless it is given preponderance, it does not exist, and giving preponderance to the unlikely is impossible. So if it is not necessitated, it does not exist. Thus, it is not good nor evil rationally, by consensus. The gist of the discourse: The action of the servant is compelled, and this is the minor premise; the major premise is omitted.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the third Ash'ari argument against intrinsic good and evil, based on determinism (jabr). The argument states that human actions are compelled (iḍṭirārī) because, before an action occurs, it is merely possible (mumkin). For it to come into existence, a preponderant factor (murjīḥ) must tip the scales; without it, the action cannot occur. Since giving preponderance without cause is impossible, the action must become necessary (wājib) before existing. Therefore, the action is not a free choice of the agent but a necessary result of external causes. Consequently, actions cannot be intrinsically good or evil by rational judgment because they are not freely chosen. The author notes that this argument is better and more concise than what is found in al-Mukhtasar (likely a reference to another uṣūl work). The conclusion is that the action is compelled—this is the minor premise; the major premise (that compelled actions cannot be ethically evaluated rationally) is implied.

Key Terms
اضطراري (iḍṭirārī): Compelled; an action that occurs without free choice due to necessity.
ممكن (mumkin): Possible; that which may or may not exist, requiring a preponderant factor to exist.
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): Giving preponderance; the act of preferring one possibility over another.
مرجوح (marjūḥ): Unlikely; the opposite of preponderant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because good and evil are known only through revelation.
B) Because the action is compelled by necessity, not free choice.
C) Because humans cannot know the consequences of their actions.
D) Because all actions are morally neutral.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari compulsion argument challenges the possibility of rational intrinsic ethics, but the Hanafi response through kasb reasserts human responsibility and the role of reason.

The Nature of Evil: Essential or Accidental?
Refutation of philosophical view that evil is created accidentally

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Maturidi position on the nature of evil and explain the distinction between essential (bi al-dhat) and accidental (bi al-'arad) attribution in divine creation.

Core Idea
Evil is not created essentially by Allah but accidentally; it is relative, not absolute.

This passage addresses a philosophical objection that evil must be part of God's plan either essentially or accidentally. The author presents Ibn Sina's view and then offers the Maturidi response, clarifying that goodness and evil can be attributed both essentially and accidentally, and that evils are relative (idafiyya). Understanding this distinction is crucial for reconciling divine omnipotence with the existence of evil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophical claim
Read the opening lines: Ibn Sina argues that evil enters God's decree accidentally (bi al-'arad) and necessarily, while good is created essentially (bi al-dhat). This implies a limitation on divine will—that evil is an unavoidable byproduct.
The phrase 'بالعرض واللزوم' indicates both accidental and necessary.
2
Analyze the Maturidi response
The author counters that good and evil are relative (idafiyya): what is evil for one may be good for another. Moreover, the ruling (hukm) of a necessary consequent (lazim) can be accidental (bi al-'arad) even if not essential. Thus, evil is not essential in creation.
The example of truthfulness and lying illustrates how the same act can be good essentially and evil accidentally through its consequence.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
Consider truthfulness: it is good essentially (bi al-dhat). But if telling the truth leads to a harmful outcome (e.g., disclosing a secret that causes harm), it becomes evil accidentally (bi al-'arad) by virtue of its consequence. Similarly, lying is evil essentially but can be good accidentally if it saves a life.
This shows that moral attributes are not fixed in all contexts.

The outline of the syllogism: the action of the servant is contingent, and every contingent does not exist unless its existence is preponderated over its non-existence. So the action of the servant, unless its existence is preponderated, does not exist. So the minor premise is apparent, and the proof of the major premise is his statement 'and preponderating the less likely is impossible'. For the contingent, its non-existence and existence are equal. Its existence is only preponderated after the attachment of Allah's will to its bringing into existence. So the non-existent that has not been attached to it Allah's will for its existence is impossible. This is the meaning of his statement: and preponderating the less likely is impossible, i.e., the existence of the contingent non-existent that has not been attached to it Allah's will is impossible and prevented. So unless the contingent becomes necessary and its existence becomes necessary, it does not exist. And this is the necessary through another, i.e., through the attachment of Allah's will. Then the author said, praising his speech: This explanation of the third proof in my style is better and more concise than what is in the Mukhtasar of Ibn al-Hajib. I left out mentioning the expression of the Mukhtasar for brevity. And the answer: that necessity through choice does not necessitate involuntariness, by the necessity of the difference between voluntary movement and tremor. Moreover, it is refuted by the act of Allah Almighty. The author intended the answer from the Maturidi side and said: And the answer: that necessity through choice does not necessitate involuntariness. This is a detailed refutation by refuting their minor premise, for we do not concede that the action of the servant is involuntary, because it becomes necessary and its existence is necessitated by his choice. And that which is necessary through choice is not involuntary, because necessity through choice does not negate choice, so it does not cause involuntariness. If necessity through choice caused involuntariness, no difference would remain between voluntary movement and tremulous movement, while the difference between them is self-evident. For if something is lost by a voluntary hand movement, the mover of the hand is liable for compensation, whereas if lost by a trembling hand movement, nothing is upon him. This is the aspect of the difference between them.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage from 'Izalat al-Rahbut' is a commentary on 'Muslim al-Thubut' dealing with two related issues: the nature of evil in divine creation and the status of human action. The author first presents the philosophical view (Ibn Sina) that evil enters God's decree accidentally (bil 'arad), not essentially. He then gives a Maturidi response: evil is relative (idafiyya), and the relationship between an antecedent (malzum) and its consequent (lazim) does not always hold essentially but may be accidental. The example of truthfulness and lying illustrates how the same act can be good or evil depending on its consequence. Then, the author presents the third argument of the opponents—that human action is involuntary (id tirari) and therefore cannot be rationally described as good or evil. The argument is a syllogism: (1) human action is contingent, (2) contingent things require a preponderating factor (murajjih) to exist, which is God's will, and (3) thus, human action becomes necessary (wajib bil-ghair) and involuntary. Consequently, moral attributes (good/evil) do not apply rationally. The author defends the Maturidi position by responding that necessity through choice (wujub bil-ikhtiyar) does not negate voluntariness. The self-evident distinction between a voluntary hand movement (haraka ikhtiyariyya) and a tremulous involuntary movement (haraka ri'shiyya) proves that human actions are voluntary; liability for compensation (gharama) in one case but not the other demonstrates the legal relevance. Thus, human actions are subject to rational and Shari'a-based moral evaluation.

Key Terms
بالذات (bi al-dhat): Essentially; something that is true by its own nature.
بالعرض (bi al-'arad): Accidentally; something true through an external cause.
إضافية (idafiyya): Relative; something that is not absolute but depends on relation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Both are essentially good and accidentally evil.

Key Takeaway
Good and evil can be attributed both essentially and accidentally; evil is relative, not absolute, and this preserves divine wisdom.

Human Action: Involuntary or Voluntary?
Refutation of argument that human action is involuntary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Maturidi argument that necessity through choice (wujub bil-ikhtiyar) does not negate voluntariness, and apply the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement to determine legal liability.

Core Idea
Necessity through choice preserves voluntariness; a voluntary act incurs liability, an involuntary one does not.

Opponents argue that human action is involuntary (id tirari) because contingent existence requires a preponderating factor (murajjih), which is God's will, making the action necessary and thus not subject to rational moral judgment. The author provides the Maturidi answer: necessity through choice does not eliminate choice. The self-evident difference between a voluntary hand movement and a trembling hand movement—one incurs liability, the other does not—proves that human actions are voluntary.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' syllogism
The opponents reason: (1) Human action is contingent (possible). (2) Contingent things require a preponderating factor to come into existence. (3) That preponderating factor is God's will. (4) Therefore, human action is necessary through another (wajib bil-ghair) and involuntary. (5) Involuntary actions are not subject to rational moral evaluation. Hence, human action is neither good nor evil rationally.
The syllogism has a hidden major premise: 'Everything involuntary is not rationally good or evil.'
2
Locate the Maturidi rebuttal
The author responds by denying the minor premise that human action is involuntary. He argues that necessity through choice (wujub bil-ikhtiyar) does not make an action involuntary. The action becomes necessary because of one's own choice, not external compulsion.
Key phrase: 'الوجوب بالاختيار لا يوجب الاضطرار'.
3
Apply the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement
The author points to the self-evident (badihi) difference between a voluntary hand movement (haraka ikhtiyariyya) and a tremulous movement (haraka ri'shiyya). If someone voluntarily moves their hand and causes damage, they are liable for compensation (gharama). If the hand trembles involuntarily and causes damage, there is no liability. This legal distinction proves that human actions are voluntary.
This is a rational proof from legal practice (dalil aqli/mu'amalat).
4
Derive the legal implication
The ruling on liability (damages) is based on the presence of choice. Thus, human actions are subject to Shari'a rulings precisely because they are voluntary. The same principle applies to moral evaluation: actions can be good or evil because they are chosen.
This is consistent with the Hanafi/Maturidi position on human free will (kasb).

الجواب من جانب الماتريدية وقال : والجواب أن الوجوب بالاختيار لايوجب الاضطرار، وهذا نقض تفصيلى بنقض صغرى دليلهم، بأنا لانسلم أن فعل العبد اضطرارى، لأنه يجب ويلزم وجوده باختياره، والواجب بالاختيار لا And we may not know it. Upon this, no evil is free from good except what Allah wills. Then he quoted the saying of the philosopher (the esoteric) in his 'Isharat' (which al-Tusi and al-Razi explained) where he said: Evil is included in decree (in Allah's estimation of creation and bringing into existence accidentally). And the meaning of Ibn Sina's statement is that Allah creates good essentially and evil accidentally and necessarily, while He is the Creator of everything intentionally and essentially, and He is over all things Disposer. The truth is to say that evils are relative matters, being evil for one and good for another, so not every necessary consequent is in the ruling of its antecedent. Evil arises so that good may be the mother. So he benefited from the answer by the shaykh's saying, and he said: I say this guides you (i.e., the shaykh's saying guides you) to the commitment mentioned previously, that just as they are essentially, they are also accidentally. Thus the ruling of the necessary consequent, even if it is not for the antecedent essentially in every place, it is accidentally. For this, truthfulness is good essentially and evil by virtue of its necessary consequent which is lying, and similarly lying may be evil essentially and good by virtue of its necessary consequent which is truthfulness. And his saying 'Understand': an emphasis on understanding the issue and an indication of its subtlety. 3. Thirdly: that the action of the servant is involuntary, for a contingent thing does not exist unless it is preponderated, and preponderating the less likely is impossible. So unless it becomes necessary, it does not exist, and thus it is not good or evil rationally by consensus. This is better and more concise than what is in 'al-Mukhtasar'. 3. And they said thirdly, in the proof of denying that they (good and evil) are essential: that the action of the servant is involuntary, for a contingent action does not exist unless it is preponderated, and preponderating the less likely is impossible. So unless it becomes necessary and its existence becomes necessary, it does not exist, and thus it is not good or evil rationally by consensus. The gist of the statement: that the action of the servant is involuntary, and this is the minor premise, and the major premise is omitted. And the major premise is: everything involuntary is not good or evil rationally by consensus. The word 'by consensus' was added to establish the major premise, meaning they have agreed that the involuntary act is not good or evil rationally. So the conclusion is that the action of the servant is not good or evil rationally. And his saying 'for the contingent action' etc.: this is the major premise, and the minor premise is omitted. The outline of the syllogism: the action of the servant is contingent, and every contingent does not exist unless its existence is preponderated over its non-existence. So the action of the servant, unless its existence is preponderated, does not exist. So the minor premise is apparent, and the proof of the major premise is his statement 'and preponderating the less likely is impossible'. For the contingent, its non-existence and existence are equal. Its existence is only preponderated after the attachment of Allah's will to its bringing into existence. So the non-existent that has not been attached to it Allah's will for its existence is impossible. This is the meaning of his statement: and preponderating the less likely is impossible, i.e., the existence of the contingent non-existent that has not been attached to it Allah's will is impossible and prevented. So unless the contingent becomes necessary and its existence becomes necessary, it does not exist. And this is the necessary through another, i.e., through the attachment of Allah's will. Then the author said, praising his speech: This explanation of the third proof in my style is better and more concise than what is in the Mukhtasar of Ibn al-Hajib. I left out mentioning the expression of the Mukhtasar for brevity. And the answer: that necessity through choice does not necessitate involuntariness, by the necessity of the difference between voluntary movement and tremor. Moreover, it is refuted by the act of Allah Almighty. The author intended the answer from the Maturidi side and said: And the answer: that necessity through choice does not necessitate involuntariness. This is a detailed refutation by refuting their minor premise, for we do not concede that the action of the servant is involuntary, because it becomes necessary and its existence is necessitated by his choice. And that which is necessary through choice is not involuntary, because necessity through choice does not negate choice, so it does not cause involuntariness. If necessity through choice caused involuntariness, no difference would remain between voluntary movement and tremulous movement, while the difference between them is self-evident. For if something is lost by a voluntary hand movement, the mover of the hand is liable for compensation, whereas if lost by a trembling hand movement, nothing is upon him. This is the aspect of the difference between them.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage from 'Izalat al-Rahbut' is a commentary on 'Muslim al-Thubut' dealing with two related issues: the nature of evil in divine creation and the status of human action. The author first presents the philosophical view (Ibn Sina) that evil enters God's decree accidentally (bil 'arad), not essentially. He then gives a Maturidi response: evil is relative (idafiyya), and the relationship between an antecedent (malzum) and its consequent (lazim) does not always hold essentially but may be accidental. The example of truthfulness and lying illustrates how the same act can be good or evil depending on its consequence. Then, the author presents the third argument of the opponents—that human action is involuntary (id tirari) and therefore cannot be rationally described as good or evil. The argument is a syllogism: (1) human action is contingent, (2) contingent things require a preponderating factor (murajjih) to exist, which is God's will, and (3) thus, human action becomes necessary (wajib bil-ghair) and involuntary. Consequently, moral attributes (good/evil) do not apply rationally. The author defends the Maturidi position by responding that necessity through choice (wujub bil-ikhtiyar) does not negate voluntariness. The self-evident distinction between a voluntary hand movement (haraka ikhtiyariyya) and a tremulous involuntary movement (haraka ri'shiyya) proves that human actions are voluntary; liability for compensation (gharama) in one case but not the other demonstrates the legal relevance. Thus, human actions are subject to rational and Shari'a-based moral evaluation.

Key Terms
اضطراري (id tirari): Involuntary; an action without choice, like a tremor.
الوجوب بالاختيار (al-wujub bi al-ikhtiyar): Necessity through choice; an action that becomes necessary because of one's own free choice.
الحركة الاختيارية (al-haraka al-ikhtiyariyya): Voluntary movement; movement resulting from conscious choice.
الحركة الرعشية (al-haraka al-ri'shiyya): Tremulous movement; involuntary shaking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key evidence that human actions are voluntary?
A) The fact that actions are contingent.
B) The consensus of scholars.
C) The difference in liability between voluntary and involuntary hand movements.
D) The impossibility of preponderating the less likely.

Key Takeaway
Necessity through choice does not negate voluntariness; the legal principle of liability for voluntary acts confirms that human actions are chosen and therefore subject to moral and Shari'a evaluation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Hanafi Refutation of the Necessity of Human Action
Maturidi and Hanafi responses to the proof that human action is necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Maturidi responses to the determinist syllogism, and apply the distinction between wujub bi al-ikhtiyar and idtirar to refute the claim that human acts are compulsory.

Core Idea
Necessity by choice does not negate choice; the difference between intentional and tremorous motion proves it.

This passage is a theological-legal dispute within usul al-fiqh concerning the nature of human action and its moral evaluation. The author presents a determinist syllogism that would make human action compulsory (idṭirārī), thereby denying its rational moral value. He then provides two Maturidi responses: first, that necessity by choice (wujūb bi al-ikhtiyār) does not entail compulsion; second, that the same syllogism would apply absurdly to God's own acts. These responses defend the Hanafi-Maturidi position that human acts are voluntary and morally evaluable, a crucial foundation for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the determinist syllogism
The author states the major premise: 'the possible act requires a preponderance to exist, which is God's will.' The minor premise (human act is possible) is implied. Trace how this leads to the conclusion that human acts are compulsory (idṭirārī).
The syllogism is a rational proof (qiyas 'aqli) used by determinists.
2
Analyze the first Maturidi response: necessity by choice
The author rebuts the minor premise: human acts are not compulsory because they are necessitated by choice. The voluntary movement vs. tremor example demonstrates the intuitive difference: liability differs.
This response preserves voluntary moral responsibility.
3
Analyze the second Maturidi response: reductio ad absurdum
The same syllogism applied to God's actions would make them compulsory, which is false. Thus the determinist argument is self-defeating.
This is a total rebuttal (naqd ijmalī) of the entire proof.
4
Understand the broader usul implication
If human acts were compulsory, they could not be rationally good or evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ), undermining the basis for moral obligation before revelation. The Maturidi response preserves rational evaluation.
This connects to the Hanafi acceptance of rational goodness and badness as a source of law.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text clearly sides with the Maturidi response, presenting it as the correct answer. The determinist view is refuted.
The author praises his own presentation as better than Ibn al-Hajib's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أن كل ماهو اضطرارى فهو لا يكون حسنًا ولا قبيحا عقلاً إجماعا، وزاد لفظ إجماعاً" لإثبات الكبرى، أي وقد أجمعوا أن الفعل الاضطراري لا يكون حسنًا ولا قبيحا عقلاً، فالنتيجة أن فعل العبد لا يكون حسنًا ولا قبيحا عقلاً. وقوله : فإن الفعل الممكن الخ: هذه كبرى والصغرى محذوفة، وأصل القياس أن فعل العبد ممكن ، وكل ممكن مالم يترجح وجوده على عدمه لم يوجد، ففعل العبد مالم يترجح وجوده لم يوجد فالصغرى ظاهرة، ودليل الكبرى قوله وترجيح المرجوح محال، فإن الممكن عدمه ووجوده سواء، وإنما يترجح وجوده بعد تعلق إرادة الله تعالى بإيجاده، فالمعدوم الذي لم يتعلق به إرادة الله تعالى وجوده محال. وهذا معنى قوله : وترجيح المرجوح محال أي وجود المعدوم الممكن الذي لم يتعلق به إرادة الله تعالى محال وممتنع، فمالم يجب الممكن ولم يصر وجوده ضروريا لم يوجد، وهذا هو الواجب بالغير أي بسبب تعلق إرادة الله تعالى، ثم قال المصنف مادحا لكلامه : وهذا البيان للدليل الثلاث بأسلوبي أحسن وأوجز مما في مختصر ابن الحاجب تركت ذكر عبارة المختصر للاختصار والجواب أن الوجوب بالاختيار لا يوجب الاضطرار، ضرورة الفرق بين حركة الاختيار والرعشة ، على أنه منقوض بفعل البارى تعالى أراد المصنف الجواب من جانب الماتريدية وقال : والجواب أن الوجوب بالاختيار لايوجب الاضطرار، وهذا نقض تفصيلى بنقض صغرى دليلهم، بأنا لانسلم أن فعل العبد اضطرارى، لأنه يجب ويلزم وجوده باختياره، والواجب بالاختيار لا يكون اضطراريًا، لأن الوجوب بالاختيار لاينافي الاختيار، فلايوجب الاضطرار، ولوكان الوجوب بالاختيار موجبًا للاضطرار لم يبق الفرق بين الحركة الاختيارية والحركة الرعشية، مع أن الفرق بينهما بديهى، فلوضاع شيئ بحركة اليد الاختيارية لزمت الغرامة على محرك اليد، ولوضاع بحركة اليد المرتعشة ليس عليه شيئ وهذا هو وجه الفرق بينهما والجواب الثاني هو قول المصنف على أنه منقوض بفعل البارى تعالى، وهذا نقض إجمالي بنفض الدليل بجميع مقدماته (صغراه وكبراء كلتيهما) وتفصيل النقض أن دليلكم يجرى بجميع مقدماته على فعل البارى تعالى، و يتخلف عنه مدلوله؛ لأنه يلزم أن يكون فعل البارى تعالى اضطرارياً؛ لأن فعله تعالى ممكن ليس بممتنع والممكن مالم يترجح لم يوجد، ففعل الله مالم يترجح بالاختيار لم يجد، فيكون اضطراريًا، فالتالي باطل والمقدم مثله.
And it is that everything that is compulsory is not good nor bad rationally by consensus. And he added the word 'consensus' to prove the major premise, meaning they agreed that the compulsory act is not good nor bad rationally. So the result is that the act of the servant is not good nor bad rationally. And his saying: 'for the possible act...' this is the major premise and the minor premise is omitted. The basis of the syllogism is that the act of the servant is possible, and every possible that does not have its existence preferred over its non-existence does not exist. So the act of the servant, as long as its existence is not preferred, does not exist. So the minor premise is clear. And the proof of the major premise is his saying: 'and preferring the improbable is impossible.' For the possible is equal in non-existence and existence, and its existence is only preferred after the attachment of the will of Allah to bring it into existence. So the non-existent to which the will of Allah is not attached is impossible. This is the meaning of his saying: 'and preferring the improbable is impossible,' meaning the existence of the possible non-existent to which the will of Allah is not attached is impossible and prevented. So as long as the possible is not necessitated and its existence does not become necessary, it does not exist. And this is the necessitated by another, i.e., due to the attachment of the will of Allah. Then the author said, praising his speech: 'And this explanation of the three proofs in a better and more concise way than what is in Mukhtasar Ibn al-Hajib; I left mentioning the phrase of the Mukhtasar for brevity.' And the answer is that necessity by choice does not necessitate compulsion, due to the necessary difference between the movement of choice and tremor. Moreover, it is rebutted by the act of Allah Most High. The author intended the answer from the side of the Maturidiyya and said: 'And the answer is that necessity by choice does not necessitate compulsion.' This is a detailed rebuttal by rebutting the minor premise of their proof: we do not concede that the act of the servant is compulsory, because it is necessitated and its existence follows by his choice, and that which is necessitated by choice is not compulsory. For necessity by choice does not negate choice, so it does not necessitate compulsion. If necessity by choice necessitated compulsion, no difference would remain between the voluntary movement and the trembling movement, whereas the difference between them is self-evident. If something is lost by the voluntary movement of the hand, the guarantor is liable for the mover of the hand, and if lost by the trembling movement of the hand, nothing is upon him. And this is the aspect of difference between them. The second answer is his statement: 'Moreover, it is rebutted by the act of Allah Most High.' This is a total rebuttal by rebutting the entire proof with all its premises (both minor and major). The detail of the rebuttal is that your proof applies with all its premises to the act of Allah Most High, yet its conclusion fails; because it would follow that the act of Allah Most High is compulsory; for His act is possible, not impossible, and the possible, as long as it is not preferred, does not exist. So the act of Allah, as long as it is not preferred by choice, does not exist, so it becomes compulsory. Thus the consequent is false, and so is the antecedent.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a theological dispute in the context of usul al-fiqh regarding the nature of human action and its moral evaluation. The author presents a syllogism that would make human action necessary (idṭirārī), leading to the conclusion that it has no moral value rationally. The syllogism is: human action is possible; every possible requires a preponderance to exist; that preponderance comes from God's will; hence human action is necessitated by God and thus compulsory. The author then gives two Maturidi responses. The first refutes the minor premise of necessity by distinguishing between necessity by choice (which is voluntary) and compulsion. The difference is shown by the example of intentional movement versus tremor: compensation is due for intentional damage but not for involuntary tremor. The second response refutes the whole syllogism by applying it to God's own actions, which would also become compulsory, an absurd conclusion. Thus the proof fails. The author follows the Hanafi-Maturidi theological tradition, defending human freedom within divine omnipotence.

Key Terms
اضطرار (idṭirār): Compulsion; an act that occurs without any choice or capacity to refrain.
الوجوب بالاختيار (al-wuǧūb bi al-ikhtiyār): Necessity that arises through one's own choice; the act is necessitated by the will of the agent, not by external compulsion.
ممكن (mumkin): Possible; something whose existence and non-existence are equally conceivable prior to God's creative will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi response, what is the key difference between an intentional hand movement and a trembling hand movement?
A) Both are necessary and thus no compensation is required.
B) The intentional movement is caused by God, while the trembling is not.
C) The intentional movement is voluntary, so if it damages property, compensation is required; for trembling, it is not.
D) The intentional movement is possible, while trembling is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Maturidi position affirms that human actions are voluntary despite being necessitated by divine will, preserving moral responsibility and rational evaluation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Five Schools on Human Choice (Ikhtiyār)
Enumeration of five theological positions on human free will and acquisition (kasb)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five theological schools on human choice (Jabriyya, Mu'tazila, Ash'ariyya, Hanafiyya, and the author's synthesis) based on their views on kasb and capacity, and apply the Hanafi definition of kasb to analyze moral responsibility.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school affirms a real role for human intention in action without attributing creation to humans — a middle path between jabr and tafwīḍ.

This passage presents a fā'ida (benefit) enumerating five theological schools on the issue of human choice and acquisition (kasb). The author classifies the positions from extreme determinism to human creative power, placing the Hanafi school as the balanced, correct view. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping the Hanafi foundation for taklīf (moral obligation) and the role of intention in ibādāt and muʿāmalāt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five schools and their core claims
Read through the five positions: Jahmiyya (no capacity), Mu'tazila (human creates acts), Ash'ariyya (capacity without effect), Hanafiyya (capacity influences intention), and the author's synthetic view. Note the key terms: qudra, kasb, qaṣd muṣamman.
2
Analyze the Hanafi definition of kasb
Isolate the Hanafi position: 'ṣarf al-qudra al-makhlūqa ilā al-qaṣd al-muṣamman ilā al-fiʿl.' Note that the capacity has an effect on the intention, and God creates the act subsequently by custom. Contrast with Ash'ari view where capacity has no entry.
The internal Hanafi debate on the nature of the intention (ḥāl vs. existent) is secondary but shows the school's commitment to a real role for human choice.
3
Compare Hanafi and Ash'ari views on kasb
Both schools use the term kasb, but Ash'aris deny the capacity any influence on the act or intention. The passage calls the Ash'ari view 'equivalent to compulsion' (kufw li-l-jabr). Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: the Hanafi view allows rational moral evaluation (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ) while Ash'ari relies on revelation.
4
Understand the author's synthetic position
The author concludes with his chosen view: man is free in particular bodily perceptions but compelled in universal intellectual sciences. This is an attempt to reconcile partial freedom with divine omniscience.
This view is not fully elaborated here; reference to al-Fiṭra al-Ilāhiyya.
5
Apply the Hanafi concept to a concrete scenario
Consider a person who intends to pray and then performs the prayer. According to the Hanafi position, the human's directing of capacity towards the intention to pray influences the intention, and God creates the actual prayer acts. This justifies reward for the intention and act.

فائدة فى بيان المذاهب الخمسة في مسألة الاختيار فائدة : (۱) عند الجهمية الذين هم الجبرية حقاً لا قدر في العبد أصلاً، بل هو كالجماد وهذا سفسطة (۲) و عند المعتزلة له قدر مؤثر في أفعاله ، وهم مجوس هذه الأمة، وما فهموا أن الإمكان ليس من شانه إفاضة الوجود (۳) و عند أهل الحق له قدر كاسبة ، لكن عند الأشعرية ليس معنى ذلك إلا وجود قدر متوهمة مع الفعل بلا مدخلية أصلاً ، قالوا ذلك كاف في التكليف ، والحق أنه كفو للجبر (٤) وعند الحنفية الكسب صرف القدرة المخلوقة إلى القصد المصمم إلى الفعل، فلها تأثير في القصد المذكور، ويخلق الله سبحانه الفعل المقصود عقب ذلك بالعادة ، (۱) فقيل ذلك القصد من الأحوال غير موجود ولا معدوم ، فليس بخلق ، وليس الإحداث كالخلق ، بل هو أهون ، (۲) وقيل : بل (هو) موجود ، فيجب تخصيص القصد المصمم من عموم الخلق بالعقل، لأنه أدنى ما يتحقق به فائدة خلق القدر ويتجه به حسن التكليف ، وهذا كأنه واسطة بين الجبر والتفويض وفيه مافيه (ه) وعندي مختار بحسب الإدراكات الجزئية الجسمانية ، ومجبور بحسب العلوم الكلية العقلية ، وشرح ذلك في الفطرة الإلهية ، وإنها
A benefit: Explanation of the five schools on the issue of choice. Benefit: (1) According to the Jahmiyya, who are the true Jabriyya, there is no capacity in the servant at all; rather he is like an inanimate object, and this is sophistry. (2) According to the Mu'tazila, he has an effective capacity in his actions, and they are the Magians of this nation, and they did not understand that possibility is not capable of bestowing existence. (3) According to the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq), he has an acquiring capacity (qudra kāsiba). However, according to the Ash'ariyya, that means nothing but the existence of an imagined capacity with the act without any entry at all; they said that is sufficient for obligation, but the truth is it is equivalent to compulsion. (4) According to the Hanafiyya, kasb is directing the created capacity (qudra) to the firm intention towards the act, so it has an effect on that intention. Allah Most High creates the intended act subsequently by custom. So some said that intention is among the states (aḥwāl), neither existent nor non-existent; it is not creation (khalq), and bringing about (iḥdāth) is not like creation; rather it is easier. (2) And some said rather it is existent, so it is necessary to specify the firm intention from the generality of creation by reason, because it is the least by which the benefit of creating capacity is realized and by which the goodness of obligation is directed. And this is like an intermediary between compulsion and delegation. And in it there is what is in it. (5) And according to my chosen view, he is free with respect to particular bodily perceptions, and compelled with respect to universal intellectual sciences. And its explanation is in the Divine Disposition (al-Fitrat al-Ilāhiyya).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a fā'ida (benefit) listing five theological positions on human free will (ikhtiyār) and the nature of kasb (acquisition). The author classifies them as: (1) Jahmiyya (extreme determinists) who deny any human capacity, likening humans to inanimate objects. (2) Mu'tazila who affirm an effective capacity that independently creates actions, which the author criticizes as excessive. (3) Ash'ariyya who affirm an 'acquiring capacity' but without any real effect on the act; the act is created by God, and the capacity merely accompanies it, which the author equates to compulsion. (4) The Hanafiyya (followers of Abu Hanifa) define kasb as directing the created capacity (qudra) towards a firm intention (qaṣd muṣamman). The capacity influences the intention, and God creates the act subsequently by custom (bi al-ʿāda). There is an internal Hanafi debate: some say the intention is a 'state' (ḥāl) neither existent nor non-existent, while others say it is existent and must be exempted from the generality of God's creation by reason. This view is presented as intermediate between jabr and tafwīḍ. (5) The author's own preferred view that man is free in bodily particular perceptions but determined in universal intellectual knowledge, which he elaborates elsewhere.

Key Terms
كسب (kasb): The human's directing of the created capacity (qudra) towards a firm intention (qaṣd muṣamman) to perform an act, with God creating the act thereafter.
قدرة كاسبة (qudra kāsiba): An acquiring capacity that does not create but is used to direct intention towards an act.
القصد المصمم (al-qaṣd al-muṣamman): The firm, resolved intention that the human directs towards a specific act; it is influenced by the human's directing of capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the role of the human capacity (qudra) in the act of kasb?
A) It directly creates the act.
B) It directs the intention towards the act, influencing that intention.
C) It merely accompanies the act without any effect.
D) It is non-existent and purely metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines kasb as the human directing of created capacity towards a firm intention, giving a real but non-creative role to human choice — a middle path between jabr and tafwīḍ.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Five Schools on Human Agency: Jabr, Kasb, and Choice
Explains five madhahib on human agency to prevent conflating Ash'ari with Jabri

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five schools on human agency, identify the Hanafi position on kasb, and evaluate the refutation of the Ash'ari argument based on God's own action.

Core Idea
Human agency is neither absolute jabr nor absolute tafwīḍ; the Hanafi kasb positions human power as influencing intention without creating the act.

A common error is to conflate the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb with pure Jabrism, but the author clarifies that Ash'aris affirm an acquisitive power, however nominal. To prevent this confusion, he enumerates five distinct schools on human agency, each with a different understanding of power (qudra) and acquisition (kasb). This lesson unpacks these positions, focusing on the Hanafi middle path and its rational refutation of both extremes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the refutation of the Ash'ari argument
The passage begins by rebutting an argument that would imply God's actions are compelled, using a reductio: if the Ash'ari reasoning is applied to God, it leads to absurdity. Identify the logical structure and why the author considers it a valid refutation.
This sets up the framework for discussing human power.
2
List the five schools and their core claims
Extract from the passage the five positions: (1) Jahmi/Jabri – no power at all; (2) Mu'tazili – effective power creating actions; (3) Ahl al-Haqq (Ash'ari/Maturidi) – acquisitive power, but Ash'ari says imaginary; (4) Hanafi – kasb as direction of created power towards intention; (5) Author's own – partial free will, universal compulsion.
Pay attention to the descriptors used for each school.
3
Differentiate Ash'ari and Hanafi kasb
Both schools use the term kasb, but the passage states that for Ash'aris it is 'imaginary power with no entrance', while Hanafis give it a real effect on intention. Note the author's critique that Ash'ari kasb is 'equivalent to jabr'.
This distinction is crucial for accurate fatwa and usul analysis.
4
Analyze the author's own synthesis
The author proposes a dual-aspect view: free will in partial bodily perceptions, compulsion in universal intellectual matters. Consider how this attempts to reconcile human responsibility with divine omnipotence.
This is a personal ijtihad and may not be widely adopted.
5
Apply the taxonomy to a usul argument
When encountering a proof about human power (istitāʿa), identify which school's premise is being used and respond with the correct Hanafi position. This equips you to dismantle flawed arguments in usul and iftaa.
The Hanafi position is the operative one for fatwa.

This is a general refutation by dismantling the evidence with all its premises (both its minor and major premises). Their evidence is the apparent meaning of the verse 'And Allah is the Creator of all things' (39:62), so no creation remains for the servant at all, and the apparent meaning of the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' (37:96). 2. According to the Mu'tazila, he has an effective power in his actions, and they are the Magians of this ummah (as the Prophet ﷺ said in a hadith narrated by Ibn Majah regarding their saying of Allah's creating and their own creating of their actions, like the Magians who say Allah's creating and the creating of Ahriman), and they did not understand that the possible is not such as to bring existence, so how do they bring their actions into existence? 3. The author said in his commentary on this expression: 'From here (because of that) it was said in
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a detailed rebuttal and clarification of an objection raised against the Ash'ari argument for the necessity of divine action, followed by an enumeration of the five main schools of thought on human free will and acquisition (kasb). The author begins by explaining that the second answer to the objection is that the argument is refuted by applying it to God's own actions; this leads to the absurd conclusion that God's actions would be compelled, which is false. Then, to prevent misunderstanding that the Ash'ari position (that human action is compelled) is identical to pure Jabrism, the author interpolates a discussion listing five schools: (1) Jahmi/Jabri – absolute determinism, no human capacity; (2) Mu'tazili – humans create their own actions effectively, which the author criticizes as akin to Magian dualism; (3) Ahl al-Haqq (Ash'ari and Maturidi) – humans have an acquisitive power, but for Ash'aris this power is only imaginary without actual effect; (4) Hanafi – kasb means directing created power towards a firm intention, which influences that intention, and Allah then creates the act by habit; this is intermediate between jabr and tafwid; (5) the author's own view – partial free will in bodily perceptions, compulsion in universal intellectual matters. The passage stresses that these distinctions are crucial for understanding the nature of divine and human agency.

Key Terms
جبرية (Jabriyya): The school that denies any human power or choice; actions are entirely from God.
كسب (kasb): In Hanafi thought, the direction of created power towards a firm intention, which influences that intention, after which Allah creates the act.
اختيار (ikhtiyar): Choice; the human capacity to intend and decide actions within the framework of divine creation.
قدرة (qudra): Power or capability; for humans it is created by God and is not effective in creating actions but accompanies them.
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation; the Mu'tazili view that humans have full power over their actions independent of God.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion; the belief that humans have no real choice or power.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, what is the role of human intention (qasd) in the act of kasb?
A) The intention directly creates the act.
B) The intention influences the act, but Allah creates the act subsequently.
C) The intention is irrelevant; Allah creates both intention and act.
D) The intention is imaginary and has no effect.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi doctrine of kasb provides a middle path between jabr and tafwīḍ, preserving human responsibility while affirming God's sole creative power.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
Five Schools on Human Action and Kasb
Digression refuting misconception that Ash'aris are Jabriyya, expounding five schools on capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five schools on human action, distinguish the Hanafi/Maturidi position on kasb from the Ash'ari position, and explain why the author considers the Ash'ari view equivalent to jabr.

Core Idea
The Hanafi/Maturidi kasb requires real effect on intention; Ash'ari kasb is merely imaginary capacity, indistinguishable from compulsion.

This passage is a digression where the author corrects a potential misunderstanding that the Ash'ari school is the same as the Jabriyya. He presents five distinct schools on the nature of human action and capacity, with special emphasis on the Hanafi/Maturidi definition of kasb and a sharp critique of the Ash'ari definition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for usul al-fiqh because it affects the attribution of actions to the servant and the basis of moral accountability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five schools and their core views
List the five schools mentioned: Jahmiyya (Jabriyya), Mu'tazila, Ash'ari, Maturidi/Hanafi, and the author's own view (al-Muslim). Summarize each position on the servant's capacity.
The author's own view is not elaborated further.
2
Compare the extreme positions
Contrast Jahmiyya (no capacity at all) with Mu'tazila (the servant creates his own actions). Note that both are rejected by Ahl al-Sunnah.
The Mu'tazila are called 'Magians of this Ummah' for positing two creators.
3
Examine the Ash'ari definition of kasb
Ash'aris define kasb as the appearance of eternal capacity in the locus of temporal capacity, with no real effect. The author cites their claim that an imaginary capacity suffices for taklif.
The author considers this equivalent to jabr.
4
Examine the Hanafi/Maturidi definition of kasb
Hanafis define kasb as directing the created capacity to a firm intention (qasd musammam). The capacity has an effect on the intention, and then Allah creates the act. This preserves human responsibility without claiming creation.
Key distinction: khalq (creation) is exclusive to Allah; ihdath (bringing about a new state) is possible for the servant.
5
Understand the author's critique and the responses to objections
The author refutes the Ash'ari position and addresses two objections to the Hanafi view: (1) that the intention is not an independent substance but a state, so it is not creation; (2) that the intention must be created by the servant's capacity, otherwise there is no difference between capable and incapable. This rational specification of general texts is a Hanafi usul principle.
The second response uses the idea that the firm intention is the least benefit of capacity, otherwise capacity would be useless.
