The Rational Impossibility of Deficiency in Allah
Explaining why attributing lying to Allah is rationally impossible and its implication for punishing the obedient

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rational principle of istihalah al-naqs and evaluate the Hanafi/Maturidi and Ash'ari positions on ta'dhib al-ta'i'.

Core Idea
Attributing deficiency to Allah is rationally impossible; thus punishing the obedient cannot be permissible.

This passage from Badakhshani's commentary tackles a foundational usul principle: the impossibility of ascribing any deficiency to Allah. The author then applies this to a debated issue—whether Allah could punish an obedient servant—revealing a key divergence between the Hanafi/Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how rational proofs (dalil aqli) operate in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
What rational principle does the author invoke at the beginning? Note the phrase 'what contradicts necessary existence' and identify the types of deficiency mentioned (psychological attributes like joy/grief, and actions like lying).
2
State the Hanafi/Maturidi Position
According to the text, what is the Hanafi/Maturidi and Mu'tazilite position on punishing the obedient servant? Why do they hold this view?
3
Present the Ash'ari Argument
How do the Ash'aris defend the permissibility of punishing the obedient? Identify the two aspects they distinguish and the Quranic verse they cite.
4
Analyze the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
What is the concrete fiqh difference that results from this dispute? Consider the nature of divine reward and obligation.
5
Evaluate the Reasoning
Based on the principle of istihalah al-naqs, which position seems more consistent? Does the Ash'ari dual-aspect argument successfully avoid attributing deficiency?

هذا المعنى أشار المصنف بقوله : " لأن ما ينافي الوجوب الذاتي كيفاً كان (كالصفات النفسية مثل الفرح والحزن) أو فعلاً (كالكذب)، إسناده إليه تعالى من الاستحالات العقلية". ولذلك لأجل منافاته الوجوب الذاتي اثبته (اسناد الكذب إليه تعالى الحكماء من الاستحالات العقلية، مع أنهم لا يقولون بدين من الأديان السماوية . بيان ما يلزم على الأشاعرة من الإشكال لكن يلزم على الأشاعرة امتناع تعذيب الطائع كما هو مذهبنا، ومذهب المعتزلة ، فإنه نقص يستحيل عليه تعالى ولكن يلزم على هذا الأصل، وهو أن إسناد النقص إليه تعالى مستحيل) على الأشاعرة (القائلين بجواز تعذيب الله المطيع) امتناع تعذيب الطائع، كما أن الامتناع مذهبنا الماتريدية) و مذهب المعتزلة ، فإن تعذيب الطائع (ظلم) و نقص يستحيل عليه تعالى. ويمكن الجواب من جانبهم بأن يقولوا : إن تعذيب الطائع له جهتان : فمن حيث إنه مطيع تعذيبه ظلم ونقص، ومن حيث إنه عبد الله تعالى وملكه ليس في تعذيبه أي نقص واستحالة، ((إن تعذبهم فإنهم عبادك)) ومن أجل هذه الآية قالوا بجواز وإمكان تعذيب الطائع فلا يلزم عليهم شيئ.
The author pointed to this meaning by his saying: 'Because what contradicts necessary existence in mode (such as psychological attributes like joy and grief) or action (such as lying), its attribution to Him is among intellectual impossibilities.' Therefore, due to its contradicting necessary existence, the wise affirmed (the attribution of lying to Him) as an intellectual impossibility, even though they do not profess any revealed religion. Exposition of what follows for the Ash'aris from the problem: But it follows for the Ash'aris (the impossibility of punishing the obedient, as is our doctrine and that of the Mu'tazila, because it is a deficiency impossible for Him) upon this principle (that attributing deficiency to Him is impossible). For the Ash'aris who say that Allah's punishing the obedient is permissible, it entails the impossibility of punishing the obedient, just as impossibility is our (Maturidi) doctrine and the Mu'tazila's, for punishing the obedient is injustice and deficiency impossible for Him. And a response from their side could be that they say: Punishing the obedient has two aspects: insofar as he is obedient, punishing him is injustice and deficiency; insofar as he is the servant of Allah and His property, there is no deficiency or impossibility in punishing him - 'If You punish them, they are Your servants' (Quran 5:118). Because of this verse, they said that punishing the obedient is permissible and possible, so no problem follows for them.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a theological and usul principle: the impossibility of attributing deficiency to Allah. It begins by clarifying that attributes such as joy, grief, or lying—which contradict necessary existence—cannot be ascribed to Allah rationally. The author then addresses a problem: the Ash'ari position that Allah may punish the obedient servant (ta'dhib al-ta'i'). If it is impossible to attribute any deficiency to Allah, then punishing the obedient (which appears unjust) would be a deficiency and hence impossible. The Maturidi (Hanafi) and Mu'tazilite position is that such punishment is indeed impossible. However, the Ash'aris defend their view by noting that punishment has two aspects: from the servant's obedience it is unjust, but from the servant's status as Allah's property (milk) it is not; they cite the Qur'anic verse 'If You punish them, they are Your servants' (5:118) to argue that it is within Allah's dominion and thus not a deficiency. Thus, they claim no logical problem follows.

Key Terms
الوجوب الذاتي (al-wujub al-dhati): Necessary existence, a property of Allah that cannot be contradicted.
الكذب (al-kadhib): Lying, an attribute deficient and impossible for Allah.
تعذيب الطائع (ta'dhib al-ta'i): Punishing the obedient servant, debated between Ash'ari and Maturidi/Mu'tazila.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership, property; used by Ash'aris to argue that punishing one's property is not a deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi/Maturidi position in this passage, why is punishing the obedient servant (ta'dhib al-ta'i) impossible?
A) Because the servant deserves reward, not punishment.
B) Because it would attribute deficiency (naqs) to Allah, which is rationally impossible.
C) Because the Quran explicitly forbids such punishment.
D) Because the Ash'ari argument is flawed and illogical.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of attributing deficiency to Allah (istihalah al-naqs) leads the Hanafi/Maturidi school to deny that Allah could punish an obedient servant, a position contested by the Ash'ari school using the concept of divine ownership.

Rational Obligation of Gratitude: Ash'ari Argument and Hanafi Refutation
Presenting the Ash'ari argument and its partial refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Ash'ari argument against the rational obligation of gratitude and explain the Hanafi/Maturidi refutation using the method of tanzil.

Core Idea
Gratitude to the Benefactor is not rationally obligatory — even conceding reason entails rulings — because the Ash'ari proof over-reaches and hardship does not negate benefit.

This passage from Badakhshani's hashia on Musallam al-Thubut addresses a classic usul dispute: is gratitude to God rationally obligatory before revelation? The author uses the method of tanzil (condescension) to temporarily adopt the Mu'tazilite premise that rational good entails rulings, yet still argues that gratitude is not obligatory. He then refutes the Ash'ari proof, showing its flaws. In this lesson, you will dissect the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the tanzil method
Understand that the author temporarily sets aside his own Maturidi view and adopts the opponent's framework to argue. This is the 'condescension' (tanzil) approach.
Tanzil is a common rhetorical strategy in usul to argue on the opponent's premises.
2
Analyze the Ash'ari proof
The Ash'ari argues that obligation requires a benefit. Since there is no benefit for God (He is self-sufficient) nor for the servant (worldly gratitude is hardship, and the Hereafter is beyond reason), gratitude cannot be obligatory.
The proof rests on the premise that obligation is always for a benefit (fā'ida).
3
Examine the first part of the refutation
The author shows that if the Ash'ari reasoning were sound, it would negate all obligation universally, which is absurd. Therefore the argument proves too much.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Evaluate the second point: hardship and benefit
Hardship does not preclude benefit; indeed, divine gifts often come through hardship. The author cites Quran 29:69 to support that striving leads to guidance.
This counters the claim that worldly hardship negates benefit.
5
Understand the significance for the Hanafi/Maturidi position
Despite the tanzil, the author's actual view (the high view) is that rational good does not entail obligation. However, even on the opponent's premises, the Ash'ari proof fails. The mufta bihi position is that gratitude is rationally obligatory.
The Hanafi school, following Maturidi theology, affirms rational obligation of gratitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة وجوب شكر المنعم عقلا على التنزل شكر المنعم ليس بواجب عقلاً، خلافاً للمعتزلة ، استدل بأنه لو وجب لوجب لفائدة ، ولا فائدة له تعالى ، لتعاليه عنها ، ولا للعبد ، أمّا في الدنيا فلأنه مشقة، وأمّا في الأخر ، فلأنه من الغيب الذي لا مجال للعقل في ذلك واعلم أنه قد مرّ في الدلائل الخمسة للأشاعرة أن الحسن والقبح بالمعنى الثالث المتنازع فيه ليسا بعقليين، والآن يريد المصنف الإشارة إلى مسألة أخرى، وهي أن الحسن و القبح العقليين ، هل يفيدان الحكم؟ (وهو الوجوب العقلي في الحسن والحرمة العقلية في القبح أم لا؟ ثم أخذ الأشعرى المسألة الجزئية، وهي حسن شكر المنعم يبحث عنه، هل هو واجب عقلاً أم لا؟ فذكر المصنف قوله على سبيل التنزل من رأيه العالى (وهو عدم إفادة الحسن والقبح العقليين (الحكم إلى رأي المعتزلة والماتريدية (السافل بأنهما يفيدان الحكم وقال : قال الأشعرى) على التنزل بأن الحسن و القبح العقليين يفيدان الحكم، وأن شكر المنعم حسن عقلاً، فشكر المنعم ليس بواجب عقلاً (أي لايفيد هذا الحسن العقلي حكم الوجوب) خلافاً للمعتزلة (القائلة بوجوب شكر المنعم عقلاً). ثم ذكر المصنف دليل الأشعرى وقال : استدل الأشعرى) بأنه لو وجب (شكر المنعم لوجب لفائدة، ولا فائدة (فيه) لا للمنعم، ولا للشاكر المنعم عليه، ولا فائدة له تعالى (وهو المنعم ) لتعإليه عنها وعدم حاجته إليها، ولا فائدة للعبد الشاكر لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة، أما في الدنيا فلأنه (الشكر) مشقة، وأما في الآخرة فلا مجال للعقل في ذلك في إدارك أمور الآخرة وطريق الشكر فيها). رد المصنف ذلك الاستدلال بوجوه ثلاثة : أقول : بعد تسليم ما ادعاه المعتزلة، كماهو معنى التنزل القول بأنه لا مجال للعقل مشكل على أنه لو تم هذا لاستلزم عدم الوجوب مطلقاً، والظاهر أن الكلام في الخاص بعد تسليم المطلق، مع أن المشقة لا تنفى الفائدة ، فإن العطايا على متن البلايا، قال الله تعالى (والذين جاهدوا فينا لنهدينهم سبلنا فقال في الردّ الأول : أقول بعد تسليم ما ادعاه المعتزلة (من كونهما مفيدين للحكم عقلا كما هو معنى التنزل ( وهو النزول من الرأي العالي (وهو عدم إفادة الحسن
Issue: The obligation of gratitude to the Benefactor rationally. On the basis of condescension (tanzil), gratitude to the Benefactor is not rationally obligatory, contrary to the Mu'tazila. He argued that if it were obligatory, it would be for a benefit, and there is no benefit for Him, may He be exalted, because He is above benefits, nor for the servant: in this world, because it is hardship, and in the Hereafter, because it is from the unseen which the intellect has no scope for. And know that it has passed in the five proofs of the Ash'aris that good and evil in the third disputed meaning are not rational. Now the author wants to point to another issue: whether rational good and evil entail a ruling (i.e., rational obligation for good and rational prohibition for evil) or not? Then the Ash'ari took the subsidiary issue: the goodness of gratitude to the Benefactor – is it rationally obligatory or not? So the author mentioned his statement on the basis of condescension from his high view (which is that rational good and evil do not entail a ruling) to the low view of the Mu'tazila and Maturidis (that they entail a ruling) and said: The Ash'ari said, on the basis of condescension, that rational good and evil entail a ruling, and that gratitude to the Benefactor is rationally good, so gratitude to the Benefactor is not rationally obligatory (i.e., this rational goodness does not entail the ruling of obligation), contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that gratitude to the Benefactor is rationally obligatory. Then the author mentioned the Ash'ari's proof and said: The Ash'ari argued that if it (gratitude to the Benefactor) were obligatory, it would be for a benefit, and there is no benefit in it neither for the Benefactor nor for the grateful recipient. There is no benefit for Him, may He be exalted (the Benefactor), because He is exalted above them and has no need of them, and there is no benefit for the grateful servant in this world or the Hereafter: in this world, because gratitude is hardship, and in the Hereafter, because the intellect has no scope in understanding the matters of the Hereafter and the way of gratitude in it. The author refuted this proof in three ways: I say: after conceding what the Mu'tazila claim, as is the meaning of condescension, the statement that the intellect has no scope is problematic, for if this were sound, it would entail absolute non-obligation, and the apparent meaning is that the discussion is about the specific after conceding the general. Moreover, hardship does not negate benefit, for gifts come upon hardships. Allah says: 'And those who strive in Our cause, We will surely guide them to Our ways' (Quran 29:69). So in the first refutation, he says: I say: after conceding what the Mu'tazila claim (that they entail a ruling rationally, as is the meaning of condescension, which is descending from the high view (which is that good does not entail a ruling)...
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute concerning the rational obligation of gratitude to the Benefactor (shukr al-mun'im). The author, following the matn of Musallam al-Thubut, adopts a tanzil approach: he temporarily sets aside his own view (that rational good and evil do not entail legal rulings) and adopts the Mu'tazilite and Maturidi premise that they do. Nevertheless, he argues, even on that premise, gratitude is not rationally obligatory. The Ash'ari proof is that obligation requires a benefit; the Benefactor needs no benefit, and the servant gains neither worldly benefit (it is hardship) nor knowledge of the Hereafter (the intellect cannot access that realm). The author begins a refutation (three points, partially given): first, if the argument were sound, it would negate all obligation universally, whereas the discussion is about this specific case after conceding the general principle; second, hardship does not negate benefit—indeed, divine gifts often follow hardship (citing Quran 29:69). The passage cuts off before completing the refutation, but the author's position is clear: gratitude is rationally obligatory, contrary to the Ash'ari claim.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم (shukr al-mun'im): Gratitude to the Benefactor, i.e., thanking God for His blessings.
التنزل (al-tanzil): Condescension or setting aside one's own view to argue on an opponent's premises.
الحسن والقبح العقليين (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyayn): Rational good and evil, i.e., moral values known by reason independent of revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation the author gives against the Ash'ari proof?
A) The proof would negate all obligation universally.
B) Gratitude is not really good.
C) The intellect can grasp the Hereafter.
D) God is not the Benefactor.

Key Takeaway
Even when conceding that rational good entails rulings, the Ash'ari proof that gratitude is not obligatory fails because it over-proves and ignores that hardship brings benefit.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Refutation of Ash'ari on Rational Goodness and Obligation
Author refutes Ash'ari argument that rational goodness does not entail obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Ash'ari argument against rational obligation and explain the Hanafi-Maturidi refutation using the three rebuttals.

Core Idea
Rational goodness does not impose obligation; revelation is required, but reason can perceive benefit.

In this passage, a Hanafi-Maturidi scholar engages with the Ash'ari position that rational categories of good and evil have no legal effect. The Ash'ari concedes for argument that rational goodness exists but denies it creates obligation, using the example of thanking the Benefactor. The author refutes this with three incisive arguments, drawing on both logical consistency and Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine the exact point of dispute: whether rational goodness (husn 'aqli) alone can impose a legal obligation (wujub). Note the schools involved: Ash'ari vs. Hanafi-Maturidi (and Mu'tazila as backdrop).
The Ash'ari uses 'tanazzul' (concession) to adopt the Mu'tazila framework temporarily.
2
State the Ash'ari position and its proof
Articulate the Ash'ari argument: thanking the Benefactor is rationally good but not obligatory because obligation would require a benefit, and there is none—neither for Allah (self-sufficient) nor for the servant (hardship in this world, reason cannot determine Hereafter).
The Ash'ari's proof is based on the absence of benefit (fā'ida).
3
Analyze the Ash'ari proof's premises
Break down the proof: (1) obligation requires benefit; (2) no benefit exists; (3) therefore no obligation. Evaluate each premise from a Hanafi-Maturidi perspective.
The author's refutations target these premises directly.
4
Examine the author's three refutations
Walk through each refutation: (a) conceding rational good implies reason can perceive Hereafter matters—contradicts Ash'ari's claim of no rational scope; (b) the argument would negate all rational obligations, but Ash'ari meant only the specific case after accepting general principle; (c) hardship does not negate benefit—Quranic example shows reward for striving.
The third refutation cites Quran 29:69 to show hardship can bring benefit.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Conclude the practical consequence: the Hanafi-Maturidi view that revelation is the source of obligation, but reason can perceive wisdom, versus the Ash'ari view that revelation alone governs. The Hanafi position allows a limited role for reason in understanding rulings but not in creating them.
The mufta bihi in Hanafi school follows Abu Hanifa: obligation comes from shar', not pure reason.

لاستلزم عدم الوجوب عدم وجوب الحكم بالحسن العقلى مطلقاً، سواء كان وجوب شكر المنعم أو وجوب فعل آخر والظاهر أن كلام الأشعرى في الخاص (وهو) وجوب شكر المنعم) بعد تسليم المطلق، أي بعد تسليم وجوب مطلق الحكم لأزيدنكم دليل المعتزلة على وجوب شكر المنعم قالوا : إنه يستلزم الأمن من احتمال العقاب بتركه ، وكلما كان كذلك فهو واجب قالوا إنه (شكر المنعم يستلزم الأمن من احتمال العقاب بتركه ترك شكر المنعم)، وكل ماكان كذلك فهو واجب (فشكر المنعم واجب)، قال تعالى : (ما يفعل الله بعذابكم إن شكرتم وآمنتم فعلم أن ترك الشكر موجب للعقاب والشكر سبب للأمن منه .
Then the Ash'ari took the particular issue, which is the goodness of thanking the Benefactor, to investigate: is it rationally obligatory or not? So the author mentioned his statement by way of descending from his high opinion (which is that rational good and bad do not entail a ruling) to the low opinion of the Mu'tazila and Maturidis (that they entail a ruling) and said: The Ash'ari said, by way of descending, that rational good and bad entail a ruling, and that thanking the Benefactor is rationally good, so thanking the Benefactor is not rationally obligatory (i.e., this rational goodness does not entail the ruling of obligation), contrary to the Mu'tazila (who say that thanking the Benefactor is rationally obligatory). Then the author mentioned the Ash'ari's proof and said: The Ash'ari argued that if thanking the Benefactor were obligatory, it would be for a benefit, and there is no benefit in it—neither for the Benefactor nor for the grateful beneficiary. There is no benefit for Allah (the Benefactor) because He is exalted above benefits and has no need of them, and no benefit for the grateful servant, neither in this world nor in the Hereafter: in this world, because gratitude is hardship, and in the Hereafter, reason has no scope in perceiving matters of the Hereafter and the way of gratitude therein. The author refuted this proof in three ways: I say: after conceding what the Mu'tazila claimed (that they are effective for ruling rationally, as is the meaning of descending—descending from the high opinion (that rational good and bad do not entail a ruling) to the low opinion, which is the choice that rational good and bad are motivators for a ruling): the statement that reason has no scope (in perceiving matters of the Hereafter) is problematic; because since you conceded that a ruling is obligatory rationally, you have conceded reason's scope in the Hereafter's benefit and perceiving matters of the Hereafter. So the claim of no scope for reason is unsustainable; because conceding that some acts are good and some bad rationally—meaning deserving praise and blame immediately and deserving reward or punishment later—necessitates the possibility of reason's scope in matters of the Hereafter. And in the second refutation he said: Moreover, if this (argument) were sound, it would entail that non-obligation would be the ruling for rational goodness absolutely, whether it is the obligation to thank the Benefactor or any other act. And apparently, the Ash'ari's speech is about the particular (which is the obligation to thank the Benefactor) after conceding the absolute, i.e., after conceding the absolute obligation of a ruling rationally, as indicated by his statement "by way of descending." And in the third refutation he said: Moreover, hardship does not negate benefit; indeed, gifts come along with trials. Allah said: {And those who strive in Our way, We will surely guide them to Our paths}. And in reality, this is a response to the Ash'ari's statement that there is no benefit in it (i.e., in the obligation of gratitude). The gist of the response is that there is benefit in gratitude for the grateful beneficiary. So the author mentioned two proofs, because his statement "gifts come along with trials" is understood and a summary of His saying: {If you are grateful, I will surely increase you}. Proof of the Mu'tazila for the obligation of thanking the Benefactor: They said: It entails safety from the possibility of punishment for abandoning it, and whatever is like that is obligatory. They said: It (thanking the Benefactor) entails safety from the possibility of punishment for abandoning it (abandoning thanking the Benefactor), and whatever is like that is obligatory (so thanking the Benefactor is obligatory). Allah said: {What would Allah do with your punishment if you are grateful and believe?} So it is known that abandoning gratitude causes punishment, and gratitude is a cause for safety from it.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a classic usul al-fiqh dispute concerning whether rational categories of good and evil (husn wa qubh 'aqliyyan) can themselves impose a legal ruling (hukm) such as obligation (wujub). The author, a Hanafi-Maturidi scholar, presents the Ash'ari position that rational goodness does not entail legal obligation, then refutes it with three arguments. The Ash'ari concedes for the sake of argument that rational good and bad have some effect, but denies that they establish obligation, using the example of thanking a benefactor (shukr al-mun'im). The Ash'ari argues that if it were obligatory, there would be no benefit: Allah has no need, and the servant gains only hardship in this world; in the Hereafter, reason cannot determine matters. The author's first refutation points out that if reason can judge goodness and badness (implying reward/punishment), then reason does have scope in the Hereafter. The second refutation notes that the Ash'ari's argument would negate all rational obligations, but he only means the specific case after accepting the general principle. The third refutation uses the Quranic verse 'And those who strive in Our way...' to show that hardship does not negate benefit; indeed, trials bring rewards. The author also cites the Mu'tazila proof that gratitude ensures safety from punishment, supported by Quran 4:147. This debate illustrates the Hanafi-Maturidi view that reason can perceive goodness and badness but does not independently create obligation; revelation is needed to make a ruling binding.

Key Terms
الحسن العقلي (al-husn al-'aqli): The quality of being good as perceived by reason independently of revelation.
شكر المنعم (shukr al-mun'im): Thanking the Benefactor (Allah) for His blessings; an act whose obligation is debated between rationalists and textualists.
التنزل (al-tanazzul): The rhetorical concession where the speaker temporarily adopts the opponent's premise for the sake of argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the author's refutations of the Ash'ari argument?
A) Conceding rational good implies reason can perceive Hereafter matters.
B) The Ash'ari argument would negate all rational obligations if accepted generally.
C) The Ash'ari's claim is based on a misunderstanding of Quran 29:69.
D) Hardship does not negate benefit; the Quran shows reward for striving.

Key Takeaway
Rational goodness (ḥusn 'aqlī) can motivate but does not create legal obligation (wujūb) in the Hanafi-Maturidi school; revelation is the sole source of binding rulings.

Rational Obligation of Thanking the Benefactor: Proof and Objections
Defending the rational proof for wujub shukr al-mun'im against Ash'ari objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position on shukr al-mun'im to evaluate Ash'ari objections and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Thanking the Benefactor is rationally obligatory because reason perceives the benefit of avoiding punishment.

This passage delves into a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: whether thanking the Benefactor (Allah) is a rational obligation (wajib 'aqli) or known only through revelation. The Hanafi school, influenced by Mu'tazili thought, generally accepts rational obligation, while the Ash'ari school rejects it. The author presents the Mu'tazili proof from the Quran, then addresses two key Ash'ari objections, demonstrating how reason and revelation cooperate in the Hanafi framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Read the passage and pinpoint the exact issue: is thanking the Benefactor (shukr al-mun'im) obligatory by reason (wajib 'aqli) or only by revelation (shar')? The Hanafi/Mu'tazili position vs. Ash'ari.
The passage starts by refuting the Ash'ari claim that reason has no role in the afterlife.
2
Extract the Mu'tazili proof
Locate the proof: the Quranic verse (4:147) and the rational argument that avoiding punishment makes thanksgiving obligatory. Note the dalil type and its wajh al-dalalah.
The verse implies that abandoning thanksgiving leads to punishment, thus making it obligatory.
3
Analyze the first Ash'ari objection and response
The first objection: thanksgiving is an unauthorized disposal of Allah's property (human faculties). The Hanafi response: reason itself grants permission, and the act is harmless like seeking shade.
The response uses the analogy of istizlal (seeking shade) and istisbah (using light).
4
Analyze the second Ash'ari objection and response
The second objection: limited thanksgiving for infinite blessings resembles mockery. The response: sincerity (ikhlas) is what matters, and revelation would not command mockery.
This objection is considered weak by the author.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: whether an adult who never received revelation is obligated to affirm the Creator and thank Him. Hanafi says yes; Ash'ari says no until revelation arrives.
This is the real-world difference between the two positions.

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary defending the rational obligation of thanking the Benefactor (wujub shukr al-mun'im) – a position held by the Mu'tazila and generally accepted within the Hanafi school. The author first refutes the Ash'ari claim that reason has no role in matters of the afterlife by pointing out the logical inconsistency: if one concedes that rational obligation exists (even in this world), then reason must have scope in the afterlife, as the same rational faculty perceives benefit and harm. The second refutation notes that Ash'ari's argument would negate all rational obligations, not just that of thanksgiving. The third refutation counters the Ash'ari that no benefit is found in thanksgiving; in fact, benefit exists (increase of blessings, guidance).

The author then presents the Mu'tazili proof: thanking the Benefactor is obligatory because it secures one from the possibility of punishment, as deduced from Quran 4:147. This is a clear textual proof.

Two objections are raised by the Ash'ari school. First, that thanksgiving is an unauthorized disposal of Allah's property (the human faculties). Two responses: (1) reason itself signals permission, as reason is like a messenger from Allah; (2) analogically, using another's property for shade or light without explicit permission is permissible if no harm occurs. Second, that limited thanksgiving for infinite blessings resembles mockery. The response: mockery cannot be assumed when sincerity is present; moreover, the Law would not mandate something resembling mockery.

The commentary maintains the Hanafi usul principle that reason is a valid source of obligation in cooperation with revelation.

Key Terms
شكر المنعم (shukr al-mun'im): Thanking the Benefactor (Allah) by using His blessings for obedience and acknowledging His favor.
حسن وقبح عقلي (husn wa qubh 'aqli): The rational perception of an act being good (praiseworthy) or evil (blameworthy) independent of revelation.
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): An objection or counter-argument raised against a proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) 'And your Lord says, Call upon Me; I will respond to you' (Ghafir 40:60).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the rational obligation of thanking the Benefactor, supported by Quranic indication and rational proof, and refutes Ash'ari objections using arguments from permission, harmlessness, and sincerity.

Refuting Objections to the Rational Obligation of Gratitude
Refutes two objections against Mu'tazila proof for rational obligation of gratitude

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the Mu'tazila proof for rational obligation of gratitude and the Hanafi objections and responses.

Core Idea
Gratitude is obligatory by revelation, not pure reason; rational permission is not legal obligation.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute between the Mu'tazila and the Hanafi/Ash'ari school regarding whether gratitude to God is obligatory independently through reason. The Mu'tazila claim that reason alone dictates thankfulness to avoid the rational evil of ingratitude. The Hanafi position holds that true obligation requires divine command; reason only indicates goodness. The author presents two objections raised against the Mu'tazila proof and systematically refutes them, demonstrating the weakness of the rational obligation thesis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila proof
The Mu'tazila argue that reason independently necessitates gratitude to God because ingratitude is rationally evil. This claim implies that obligation can arise without revelation.
This is the target of the objections.
2
First objection: Gratitude as unauthorized disposal
The first objection argues that using God-given faculties for gratitude is a disposal of another's property without permission, which is forbidden. The author gives two answers: (a) rational permission exists because reason itself is like a messenger indicating divine permission; (b) even if faculties belong to God, using them for gratitude is like using someone's shade or lamp without harm, which is permissible.
The second answer compares gratitude to acts of benefit that do not harm the owner.
3
Second objection: Gratitude resembles mockery
The second objection claims that because human gratitude is utterly disproportionate to God's infinite blessings, it resembles mockery (istihza'), which is forbidden. The author rebuts: God values sincerity (ikhlas), not quantity; the Quran commands gratitude, proving it is not mockery.
Quranic verses like 'Be grateful to Me' are cited to show gratitude is ordained.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: whether a person who never received revelation is punished for failing to be grateful. Mu'tazila say yes; Hanafi/Ash'ari say no, because legal obligation depends on revelation.
This difference has implications for the status of pre-revelation actions.

الشكر، قال المصنف في الجواب الأول عنها : وهو ضعيف جدا ؛ فإن المعتبر عند الله (هو) الاخلاص لا كثرة العمل، وقلته . "انّما الأعمال بالنيات " وقال في الجواب الثاني عنها : وأيضا كيف يقال : ان الشرع ورد بوجوب ما يشبه الاستهزاء ؟ ولو كان الشكر مشابها بالاستهزاء لما قال تعالى واشكروا لي ولا تكفرون ، شاكرا لأنعمه ) ، (لئن شكرتم لأزيدنكم.

Mention of the two objections directed at the proof of the Mu'tazila and the answers to them.

First objection: It is an act of disposing of another's property without permission.
Answer: Rather by rational permission, and that it is like seeking shade and using light. Second: that it resembles mockery, and this is weak because what matters with God is sincerity. Also, how can it be said that the Shari'a came with obligation of what resembles mockery? So ponder.

First objection: It is said that gratitude is disposing of another's property (from) the external and internal faculties that God deposited in man without permission, and everything of this nature is forbidden, so is gratitude to the Benefactor.
Then the author mentioned two answers to this objection:
Answer (first): Rather it is disposal of another's property with permission (established) by reason, for reason in their view is like a messenger in the obligation of obligation when it exists. So the granting of reason is evidence of permission to use it and use its instruments of external and internal senses.
(Second): That using the faculties for gratitude is like seeking shade in another's shade and using another's lamp, and there is no doubt about their permissibility, because there is no harm to the other in seeking shade and using light. The summary of the answer to the first objection: The faculties necessary for performing gratitude are all the property of the slave, the noblest of them being reason. Second: Even if we concede they are the property of another (i.e., God), He has allowed us to benefit from them. If He had not permitted their use, would He not ask about them on the Day of Judgment? 'Indeed, the hearing, the sight, and the heart – all of those will be questioned.'

Second objection: It is said that (gratitude to the Benefactor whose blessings cannot be counted) resembles mockery because of the little gratitude and abundance of blessings. So mockery is forbidden, thus what resembles it from gratitude. The author said in the first answer to it: It is very weak because what matters with God is sincerity, not abundance or scarcity of action. 'Actions are by intentions.' And in the second answer: Also, how can it be said that the Shari'a came with the obligation of what resembles mockery? Had gratitude resembled mockery, He would not have said, 'And be grateful to Me and do not deny Me,' 'Grateful for His blessings,' 'If you are grateful, I will surely increase you.'

And in His saying: (so ponder) is an indication of the subtlety of the station, or that the truth is the obligation of gratitude rationally, just as it is obligatory legally.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute where the author, Sharh al-Musallam al-Thubut, defends the Ash'ari/Maturidi position (adopted in Hanafi usul) against the Mu'tazila claim that gratitude to God is rationally obligatory. The Mu'tazila argue that reason alone necessitates gratitude; the author presents two objections raised against this proof and refutes them.

First objection: Gratitude uses God-given faculties without permission. The author responds: (1) Reason itself grants permission – reason is like a messenger from God, and its existence implies permission to use faculties. (2) Even if the faculties are God's property, using them for gratitude is like using someone's shade or lamp without harm, which is permissible. The summary adds that the faculties are actually the slave's property (by a different argument), but even if they were God's, He has allowed their use as evidenced by the Quranic verse 'Indeed, hearing, sight, heart will be questioned.'

Second objection: Gratitude is insufficient relative to God's blessings, thus resembling mockery which is forbidden. The author dismisses this as weak: God values sincerity (ikhlas), not quantity. He also quotes Quranic verses commanding gratitude, proving it is not mockery.

Thus the author concludes that the truth is that gratitude is obligatory both rationally (in the sense that reason provides a basis) and legally.

Key Terms
الشكر (al-shukr): Gratitude to the Benefactor (God) through acknowledgment and obedience.
المعتزلة (al-Mu'tazila): A theological school that prioritizes reason, claiming that obligations can be known rationally independent of revelation.
الاذن العقلي (al-idhn al-aqli): Permission derived from reason, which is considered a valid indicator of divine permission for using faculties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Gratitude is impossible due to the abundance of blessings.
B) God looks at sincerity (ikhlas), not the quantity of action.
C) The Prophet prohibited mockery but allowed gratitude.
D) Reason alone can determine the obligation of gratitude.

Key Takeaway
Gratitude is legally obligatory by revelation; reason alone provides permission but not obligation.

Mutazilite Division of Actions and Pre-Revelation Rulings
Analyzes Mutazilite classification of actions and their three pre-revelation positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Mutazilite classification of actions, explain their two pre-revelation positions (ibaha and hazr), and evaluate the Hanafi response using rational argument.

Core Idea
Before revelation, actions have no ruling—reason alone cannot impose obligation or prohibition.

This passage from al-Badakhshani's commentary on Musallam al-Thubut presents the Mutazilite approach to classifying actions and their controversial claim that actions have a pre-revelation ruling. The Hanafi position rejects this, arguing that without divine command, actions are neutral (waqf). We will dissect the Mutazilite arguments and the Hanafi rational response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mutazilite classification of actions
The Mutazila divide actions into voluntary (ikhtiyari) – those survival can do without, like eating fruit – and involuntary. Voluntary actions are further split into those where reason perceives good/bad (falling into the five categories) and those where it does not.
2
State the two main pre-revelation positions
For actions without perceived good/bad, the Mutazila propose: (1) ibaha (permissibility) to achieve wisdom and avoid futility; (2) hazr (prohibition) to prevent unauthorized use of others' property. A third 'rebuttal' (ربما يمنع الاستلزام) challenges the entailment of ibaha.
The 'third opinion' is not an independent view but a critique of ibaha.
3
Examine the Hanafi objection to hazr
The Hanafi response: intellectual permission (al-idhn al-'aqli) suffices, analogous to using another's shade or light without harm. The assumption of detailed knowledge of the cause ('illa) is not required; general knowledge is sufficient.
4
Understand the epistemological difference
The Hanafi-Ash'ari position (waqf) holds that before revelation there is no hukm. The Mutazila claim reason can know the ruling. The debate hinges on whether reason alone can impose obligation (taklif) without a divine command.
Hanafi usul: 'aql cannot create obligation; only revelation can.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المعتزلة فقسموا الأفعال الاختيارية - وهي التي يمكن البقاء والتعيش بدونها، كأكل الفاكهة، مثلاً- إلى ما يدرك فيه جهة محسنة أو مقبحة، فينقسم إلى الأقسام الخمسة المشهورة، وإلى ماليس كذلك. ولهم فيه قبل الشرع ثلاثة أقوال: الإباحة تحصيلاً لحكمة الخلق ودفعاً للعبث، وربما يمنع الاستلزام، والحظر لئلا يلزم التصرف في ملك الغير بغير إذنه. وقد مر الجواب عن هذا الدليل بأن الاذن العقلي ثابت وأنه كالاستظلال بظل غيره والاستصباح بمصباح غيره إذا لم يكن فيها للغير ضرر. ولا يرد عليهما أنه كيف يقال بالإباحة والحظر العقليين، وقد فرض أنه لا حكم له فيه، لأن المفروض أن لا علم بعلة الحكم تفصيلاً، ولا ينافي ذلك العلم إجمالاً.
As for the Mutazila, they divided voluntary actions – which are those that survival and living can do without, such as eating fruit, for example – into those in which a good or bad aspect is perceived, so they are divided into the well-known five categories, and into those that are not like that. And they have three statements regarding it before revelation: permissibility (ibaha) to achieve the wisdom of creation and to prevent futility, and perhaps the entailment is prevented, and prohibition (hazr) so that acting in the property of another without permission does not follow. And the answer to this proof has already been given: that intellectual permission is established, and that it is like seeking shade under the shade of another and lighting with the lamp of another if there is no harm to the other. And it is not objected to these two that how can intellectual permissibility and prohibition be said, given that it is assumed that there is no ruling for it, because it is assumed that there is no detailed knowledge of the cause of the ruling, and that does not contradict general knowledge.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage discusses the Mutazilite school's classification of actions and their views on the original ruling (hukm) of actions before the advent of revelation (shar'). The author, al-Badakhshani, is commenting on a section of Musallam al-Thubut. First, the Mutazila divide actions into voluntary (ikhtiyari) – those that a person can live without, like eating fruit for pleasure – and involuntary. They further subdivide voluntary actions into those where the mind can perceive a good or bad quality (hasan or qabih), which then fall into the five categories of obligation (wajib), prohibition (haram), dislike (makruh), recommendation (mandub), and permission (mubah). For voluntary actions where no such quality is perceived, the Mutazila have three opinions before revelation: (1) they are permissible (ibaha) to achieve God's wisdom and avoid futility; (2) a rebuttal that this permissibility is not entailed; (3) they are prohibited (hazr) to prevent unauthorized use of another's property. The author notes that the proof for hazr is answered by saying that intellectual permission is sufficient, like using another's shade or light without harm. Finally, the objection that rational ibaha or hazr contradicts the assumption of no ruling is dismissed: detailed knowledge of the cause is absent, but general knowledge is possible. This sets up the Hanafi-Ash'ari position: before revelation, actions have no ruling (waqf), not ibaha or hazr.

Key Terms
الأفعال الاختيارية (al-afʿāl al-ikhtiyāriyya): Actions that a person can choose to do or not do, which are not essential for survival, as defined by the Mutazila.
الإباحة (al-ibāḥa): The ruling that something is permitted (mubah) before revelation, according to one Mutazilite view.
الحظر (al-ḥaẓr): The ruling that something is prohibited (haram) before revelation, according to another Mutazilite view.
الوقف (al-waqf): The position that actions have no ruling (hukm) before revelation; suspension of judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the Mutazilite argument for hazr (prohibition) before revelation?
A) Reason alone cannot impose prohibition; only revelation can.
B) Intellectual permission is sufficient, like using another's shade without harm.
C) The action is always permissible because it is natural.
D) Both A and B.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position that actions have no pre-revelation ruling (waqf) is defended by rational arguments that intellectual permission suffices, unlike the Mutazilite insistence on inherent ibaha or hazr.

Mu'tazili Views on Pre-Revelation Rulings and Hanafi Critique
Presents and critiques Mu'tazili views on pre-revelation ibaha and hazr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Mu'tazili positions on pre-revelation rulings and apply the Hanafi usul critique regarding the impossibility of contradictory rulings derived from reason alone.

Core Idea
Reason alone cannot determine moral rulings before revelation; rational ibaha and hazr both collapse into contradiction.

The Mu'tazila argued that before divine law, acts must be either permissible or prohibited by reason alone. This passage presents their three positions and subjects them to a rigorous Hanafi critique, exposing the logical flaw that reason cannot produce consistent legal rulings without revelation. Understanding this dispute is foundational for appreciating why Hanafi usul insists on revelation as the sole source of legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Mu'tazili positions
Read the first part of the passage where the author lists the three sayings: (1) original ibaha, (2) original hazr, and (3) a second hazr view. Note the evidence each gives.
The third position is essentially a variant of hazr.
2
Analyze the rational proofs for ibaha
The Mu'tazila cite Quranic verses to support ibaha: 'It is He who created for you all that is on the earth' and 'We did not create the heavens and the earth ... in vain.' Identify how they use these verses to argue for permissibility.
These are the main dalil for ibaha.
3
Understand the Hanafi objection to ibaha
The author denies the implication that without ibaha, creation would be futile. He gives the example of God creating prohibited things so that believers can be patient and gain reward. This shows that prohibition does not entail absurdity.
This rebuts the 'avoidance of futility' argument.
4
Analyze the rational proof for hazr and its rebuttal
The Mu'tazila argue that acting without permission in God's property is prohibited. The Hanafi response: rational permission exists, likened to using someone's shade or light without harm.
The rebuttal relies on the concept of 'idhn 'aqli' (rational permission).
5
Examine the dialectic of contradiction
The author raises a new objection: if reason cannot perceive the true moral quality of an act, then it is possible for the same act to be both permissible and prohibited in reality, leading to a logical contradiction. He then refutes the Mu'tazili attempt to resolve this by distinguishing summary vs. detailed knowledge.
The key critique: 'ikhtilaf al-'illa la yarfa' al-tanaqud' (difference in cause does not remove contradiction).

The Mu'tazila have three sayings regarding it before the mission [of the Prophet]: First: permissibility (ibaha), to achieve the wisdom of creation and to avoid futility. The Mu'tazila said: Things before the coming of the law were permissible, for two reasons: First: attaining knowledge of Allah's wisdom in creating things, which is benefit; He said: 'It is He who created for you all that is on the earth.' Then he pointed in his commentary to the aspect of denial of implication and said: 'Indeed, Allah created prohibited things so that the servant may desire them and then be patient, and be rewarded for it, so no futility follows from the absence of permissibility.' So the author responded by saying: 'and it is not raised against them...' because the intended matter is that there is no knowledge of the cause of the ruling (its goodness or badness) before the mission in detail, and that does not contradict knowledge of it before in summary; because the time of detail comes after the time of summary. Then the author raised another objection from himself against those two sayings or doctrines and said: 'I say: it is raised against them that it necessitates the possibility of an act whose aspect of goodness or badness is not perceived being described by two contradictory rulings in reality; because if the ruling of that act is permissibility and solution according to some, it is possible that its ruling is prohibition and restriction according to others. He said: 'And summary and detail do not avail: because difference in the cause (cause of the ruling) by summary and detail does not remove contradiction. Because if the cause of permissibility is summary, and the cause of prohibition is detail, it does not necessitate that the supposed thing—which is the act—be two so that contradiction is removed.' His statement (so reflect): there is an indication of the response to his objection; for he said in his commentary on this saying: there is an indication that the basis of the objection is on the assumption of the proponents of the two schools (school of permissibility and school of prohibition), which is knowledge of the absolute ruling (summary) along with lack of knowledge of the specific ruling (detailed), the consideration of the Lawgiver with a view to the particularity of the act, and it is possible to respond regarding the original...

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary presents and critically examines the Mu'tazili rational arguments regarding the status of acts before revelation. The author first outlines the three Mu'tazili positions: (1) original permissibility (ibaha) argued from divine wisdom and avoidance of futility, (2) original prohibition (hazr) argued from respecting God's ownership, and (3) a second prohibition view. The author then presents objections and responses. The discussion focuses on whether the intellect can independently determine good and evil before divine law. The author raises the problem of contradictory rulings being possible for the same act if reason cannot perceive its moral quality. He then critiques the attempted 'summary/detail' distinction as insufficient to resolve the contradiction. The passage exemplifies the Hanafi usul method of critically engaging with rationalist theological schools while maintaining that divine revelation is necessary for definitive moral knowledge.

Key Terms
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility; the ruling that an act is allowed without reward or punishment before revelation
الحظر (al-hazr): Prohibition; the ruling that an act is forbidden before revelation, argued by some Mu'tazila
العقل (al-'aql): Intellect; used here to denote rational faculty that allegedly can determine good and evil independently of revelation
الإذن العقلي (al-idhn al-'aqli): Rational permission; the concept that reason allows certain actions (like using another's property without harm) before revelation

Key Takeaway

The Third View: Tawaqquf (Suspension) Before Revelation
Explaining and critiquing tawaqquf before revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the basis for the view of tawaqquf and evaluate the Hanafi objection to it, distinguishing between specific and general rulings.

Core Idea
Suspension (tawaqquf) is only necessary for the specific ruling, not for general knowledge – a key Hanafi distinction.

This passage introduces the third view on the status of acts before revelation: that one must suspend judgment (tawaqquf) because reason alone cannot determine which of the five rulings applies. The author then critiques this view by distinguishing between knowledge of a specific ruling and general knowledge that a ruling exists. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Hanafi position that general rational knowledge (ijmali) is possible without committing to a specific falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third view and its rationale
Read the initial description of tawaqquf: the proponents argue that since a specific ruling (one of the five) exists in reality but we cannot know which one, asserting any specific ruling risks contradicting reality (kadhib). Thus, one must suspend judgment.
The key fear is of contradicting nafs al-amr (reality).
2
Locate the author’s objection
The author responds: 'This necessitates suspension in specificity (al-khususiyya) and does not contradict ruling in generality (al-ijmal).' This is the core critique.
Focus on the two terms: khususiyya (specific) and ijmal (general).
3
Analyze the distinction between specific and general rulings
The objection is that lack of knowledge of the specific ruling does not entail lack of knowledge of the general ruling. One can know in general that an act has a ruling (one of the five) without specifying which one. This general knowledge does not risk falsehood because it does not affirm a particular incorrect ruling.
This shows how ijmali knowledge is epistemologically safe.
4
Evaluate the strength of the objection
Consider whether the objection fully rebuts tawaqquf. The author claims that tawaqquf is only required for the specific ruling but not for general knowledge. This undermines the necessity of absolute suspension, as the proponent of tawaqquf might still insist on suspension even of general judgment.
The Hanafi position leans towards ijmali knowledge being sufficient for taklif before revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : التوقف لأن ثمّه حكماً معيناً من الخمسة ولا يدرى أيها واقع أقول : هذا يقتضي الوقف في الخصوصية ، ولا ينافي الحكم في الإجمال فتدبر والثالث التوقف : لأن ثمة حكماً معيناً من الخمسة، ولا يدرى أتها واقع، يعنى لو اعتقدنا حكماً معيناً من الأحكام الخمسة، كا الاباحة أو الحرمة، وكان الحكم المعين في نفس الأمر خلاف ما اعتقدنا، يلزم الكذب، وخلاف الواقعي، وهذا باطل، فوجب التوقف. ثم اعترض المصنف على قائلى التوقف وقال : أقول : هذا يقتضي الوقف (التوقف) في الخصوصية (أي في الحكم الخاص من الأحكام الخمسة، ولا ينافي (العلم) بالحكم الاجمالى في كل فعل فتدبر . وحاصل الاعتراض أنه لا يلزم من عدم العلم بالحكم الخاص وعلته عدم العلم بعلة الحكم الاجمالي، فمن الممكن أن يعلم المكلف قبل ورود الشرع من الأحكام اجمالاً ، وان لم يعلم خصوصية ذلك الحكم، فليس دليل التوقف (من لزوم مخالفة نفس الأمر) بلازم.
The third: suspension (tawaqquf) because there is a specific ruling among the five and it is not known which one is actual. I say: This necessitates suspension in specificity, and does not contradict ruling in generality, so ponder. And the third is suspension: because there is a specific ruling among the five, and it is not known which one is actual, meaning if we believe a specific ruling among the five rulings, such as permissibility or prohibition, and the specific ruling in reality is contrary to what we believed, lying and contradiction of reality would be necessary, and that is invalid, so suspension is necessary. Then the author objected to the proponents of suspension and said: I say: This necessitates suspension in specificity (i.e., in the specific ruling among the five rulings), and does not contradict (knowledge of) the general ruling in every act, so ponder. The summary of the objection is that lack of knowledge of the specific ruling and its cause does not necessitate lack of knowledge of the cause of the general ruling. So it is possible for the legally responsible person to know before the coming of the Law of rulings in general, even if he does not know the specificity of that ruling. So the evidence for suspension (of necessitating contradicting reality) is not necessary.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the third view on the status of acts before revelation: tawaqquf (suspension). The proponents argue that there must be a specific ruling (one of the five: wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, haram) for every act in reality, but reason cannot determine which one. If we assert a specific ruling without knowledge, we risk contradicting the actual divine ruling, which is a form of lying. Therefore, one should suspend judgment (tawaqquf) and not attribute any specific ruling. The author (the commentator) objects by distinguishing between specific and general rulings. He argues that tawaqquf is only required for the specific ruling, not for general knowledge. A person can know in general that an act has a ruling (one of the five) without knowing which one. This general knowledge does not entail lying because it does not commit to a specific falsehood. Thus, the argument for tawaqquf is not compelling. This shows the Hanafi tendency to allow for a rational ijmali knowledge even before revelation, distinguishing it from the mutakallimin who might insist on tawaqquf.

Key Terms
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspension of judgment regarding the specific ruling of an act before revelation, due to the risk of error.
الخصوصية (al-khususiyya): Specificity; the particular ruling (hukm khass) among the five that applies to an act in nafs al-amr.
نفس الأمر (nafs al-amr): The reality or actuality of a matter, as it is in itself, independent of human perception or opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author reject the necessity of tawaqquf?
A) Because it is impossible to have any knowledge before revelation.
B) Because tawaqquf would lead to practical paralysis.
C) Because lack of specific knowledge does not prevent general knowledge.
D) Because the five rulings are not applicable before revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on pre-revelation rulings is that one can have general rational knowledge (ijmali) of a ruling without knowing the specific category, thereby avoiding the extreme of tawaqquf.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Tawaqquf Before Revelation: General vs. Specific Ruling
Analysis of tawaqquf argument and Hanafi rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the argument for tawaqquf (suspension) by distinguishing knowledge of the specific ruling from knowledge of the general ruling, and explain why the Hanafi school rejects the necessity of suspension.

Core Idea
Tawaqquf is not required because one can know the general ruling without knowing the specific ruling.

Before divine revelation, what can a person know about the ruling of an act? This passage presents the third opinion—tawaqquf—which argues that one must suspend all judgment. However, the author responds that this suspension only concerns the specific ruling, not the general, and thus tawaqquf is not necessary. Understanding this distinction is key to the Hanafi approach to rulings before revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
The passage refers to a previous objection and answer, then introduces the third position (tawaqquf). Recognize that the context is about knowledge of rulings before revelation.
The first two positions are not detailed here; focus on the third.
2
Analyze the proof of the proponents of tawaqquf
The mutawaqqifun argue that if one assumes a specific ruling (e.g., permissibility) and it contradicts the actual divine ruling, then one falls into falsehood. Thus, one must suspend all judgment about the specific ruling.
This is based on the fear of contradicting reality.
3
Analyze the author's objection
The author counters that the lack of knowledge of the specific ruling does not imply lack of knowledge of the general ruling. One can know that an act is subject to divine command without knowing which of the five categories applies.
The key distinction: hukm ijmaili vs. hukm tafsili.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that a person before revelation is not required to suspend action; they may act based on general obligation and avoid specific assumptions.
This allows practical orientation without risking falsehood about the specific.
5
Evaluate the author's resolution
The author suggests that the proponents actually meant suspension of specific knowledge only, not general. This resolves the dispute partially, but the author still rejects the necessity of tawaqquf.
The author's phrase 'so reflect' indicates this nuanced resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الايراد بأن الحكم الاجمالي موقت إلى ورود الشرع بالخصوص (بالحكم التفصيل) فكان كالاجتهاد إذا كان خطأ فهناك يجب العمل به إلى ظهور الضواب مع عدم اتصاف الفعل بالحكمين . وخلاصة الجواب : أنّ لزوم اجتماع المتضادين ليس بموجود في بعض الصور، كما إذا كان الوجوب والاباحة بمعنى مالا حرج في فعله، ففي هذه الصورة يمكن أن يكون الفعل واجباً ومباحاً، لأنه ليس في فعلهما حرج، وان كان فعل أحدهما لازماً وفي تركه خرج وليس هنا بينهما تضاد . وفي بعض الصور يكون التضاد بينهما موجوداً، كما في الاباحة والحرمة ولكن الجواب عن هذا التضاد ممكن، بأن يكون أحدهما في نفس الأمر، والآخر في زعم أهل المذهب، نعم، لو كان كلاهما في نفس الأمر، أو في زعم أهل المذهب يلزم الجمع بين المتضادين، ولكن ليس الأمر كذلك . الثالث : التوقف لأن ثمّه حكماً معيناً من الخمسة ولا يدرى أيها واقع أقول : هذا يقتضي الوقف في الخصوصية ، ولا ينافي الحكم في الإجمال فتدبر والثالث التوقف : لأن ثمة حكماً معيناً من الخمسة، ولا يدرى أتها واقع، يعنى لو اعتقدنا حكماً معيناً من الأحكام الخمسة، كالاباحة أو الحرمة، وكان الحكم المعين في نفس الأمر خلاف ما اعتقدنا، يلزم الكذب، وخلاف الواقعي، وهذا باطل، فوجب التوقف. ثم اعترض المصنف على قائلى التوقف وقال : أقول : هذا يقتضي الوقف (التوقف) في الخصوصية (أي في الحكم الخاص من الأحكام الخمسة، ولا ينافي (العلم) بالحكم الاجمالى في كل فعل فتدبر . وحاصل الاعتراض أنه لا يلزم من عدم العلم بالحكم الخاص وعلته عدم العلم بعلة الحكم الاجمالي، فمن الممكن أن يعلم المكلف قبل ورود الشرع من الأحكام اجمالاً ، وان لم يعلم خصوصية ذلك الحكم، فليس دليل التوقف (من لزوم مخالفة نفس الأمر والواقع) تاماً ومفيداً للقطع بالتوقف. وفي قوله فتدبر " إشارة إلى الجواب وهو أن مراد المتوقفين التوقف عن العلم بحكم خاص وعلته، دون التوقف عن العلم الاجمالي.
The objection is that the general ruling is temporary until the arrival of the specific ruling, so it is like ijtihad; if it is wrong, then one must act on it until the appearance of the controls, without the act being described by both rulings. The summary of the answer: The necessity of combining opposites does not exist in some cases, as when obligation and permissibility mean no difficulty in performing the act. In such a case, the act can be obligatory and permissible because there is no difficulty in performing them, even if performing one is necessary and omitting it entails difficulty, and there is no opposition here. In some cases, opposition exists between them, as in permissibility and prohibition, but the answer to this opposition is possible by making one of them in reality and the other in the estimation of the adherents of the school. Yes, if both were in reality or both in the estimation of the adherents of the school, then combining opposites would be necessary, but that is not the case. The third position: Suspension because there is a specific ruling among the five and one does not know which one is actual. I say: This necessitates suspension in specificity and does not negate the ruling in general. So reflect. And the third position: Suspension because there is a specific ruling among the five and one does not know which one is actual, meaning if we believe in a specific ruling among the five, like permissibility or prohibition, and the specific ruling in reality is contrary to what we believed, then lying and contradiction of reality would be necessary, and that is invalid, so suspension is necessary. Then the author objected to the proponents of suspension and said: I say: This necessitates suspension in specificity (i.e., in the specific ruling among the five rulings) and does not negate knowledge of the general ruling in every act. So reflect. The summary of the objection is that from the lack of knowledge of the specific ruling and its cause, the lack of knowledge of the general ruling does not follow. It is possible for the legally responsible person to know the rulings generally before the arrival of the law, even if they do not know the specificity of that ruling. So the proof of suspension (from the necessity of contradicting reality) is not complete and useful for certainty of suspension. And in his statement 'so reflect' there is an indication of the answer, which is that the proponents of suspension intended suspension of knowledge of a specific ruling and its cause, not suspension of general knowledge.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the necessity of tawaqquf (suspending judgment) regarding the specific ruling (hukm tafsili) before divine revelation. The author first presents an objection: If the general ruling (hukm ijmaili) is temporary until the specific ruling arrives, it resembles ijtihad—if wrong, one must act until evidence appears, but the act cannot be described by two opposing rulings. The answer notes that in some cases, like obligation and permissibility defined as 'no difficulty in performance', the act can be both without contradiction. In other cases, like permissibility and prohibition, the contradiction is resolved by attributing one ruling to reality and the other to the adherent's estimation. The third opinion (tawaqquf) argues that one must suspend because claiming a specific ruling risks falsehood if it contradicts reality. The author objects: tawaqquf is only needed for the specific ruling, not the general; one can know general obligation without knowing which specific ruling applies. Thus, the proof for tawaqquf is not definitive. The author finally clarifies that the proponents meant suspension of knowledge of the specific, not the general, thereby resolving the dispute.

Key Terms
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspending judgment about a specific ruling due to inability to determine which ruling applies
الحكم الإجمالي (al-hukm al-ijmali): The general ruling that an act is subject to divine command without specification of which of the five categories (obligation, prohibition, etc.)
الحكم التفصيلي (al-hukm al-tafsili): The specific ruling of one of the five categories (wajib, haram, etc.)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the proof of the proponents of tawaqquf not conclusive?
A) Because one can know the specific ruling through reason alone
B) Because lack of knowledge of the specific ruling does not imply lack of knowledge of the general ruling
C) Because the act is permanently praiseworthy regardless of whether revelation comes
D) Because the five rulings are mutually exclusive

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul rejects tawaqquf before revelation because general knowledge of obligation is sufficient; one need not know the specific ruling to act.
